The Specification of Threat and the Exception for Disbelievers
Objection-and-answer regarding changing speech and scope

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the Ash'ari position on conditional threats applies only to believers, not to disbelievers, based on the Quranic verse about not forgiving shirk.

Core Idea
God's choice in punishment is for believers; disbelievers face certain punishment.

The final part of the passage addresses further objections: does the conditional nature of threat lead to alteration of speech or imply that disbelievers may be forgiven? The Ash'aris clarify that the prohibition of changing speech applies only to threats against disbelievers, and the possibility of forgiveness is restricted to sinning believers, as derived from the verse on shirk.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection about changing speech
Note that the objection says that if God does not carry out a threat, He is changing His word.
2
Understand the Ash'ari specification
They argue that the prohibition of changing speech refers specifically to threats against disbelievers, so it does not apply to believers.
3
Grasp the limitation to believers
They clarify that forgiveness is only for sins other than shirk, so disbelievers are not included.
This is based on the verse from An-Nisa'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قسم الإلهيات
الأزل (١)؛ إذ المراد لا يتخلَّفُ عن الإرادة؛ لأنَّه لو تخلّف إعطاء الموعود به لزمَ الكِذبُ والسَّفه والخُلْف والتَّبديل في القول، وهو خلاف قوله تعالى: إِنَّكَ لَا تُخلِفُ الْمِيعَادَ) [آل عمران: ١٩٤) مَا يُبَدَّلُ الْقَوْلُ لَدَى [ف: ٢٩]، فالتَّوابُ فضل من الله تعالى وَعَدَ به المُطِيعَ، فيفى له به؛ لأنَّ الخُلْف في الوعد نقص يجب تنزيهه تعالى عنه، بخلاف الوعيد؛ فإنَّه لا يستحيل إخلافه، فيجوز.
وقد أجاب الأشاعرة على ذلك؛ أما عن لزوم الكذب فقالوا: لا نُسلّم أنه يلزم الكذب؛ لأنَّ الكريم إذا أخبر بالوعيد فإن اللائق بكرمه أنْ يَبْني إخباره به على المشيئة وإن لم يُصرح بها؛ فإذا قال: «لأعذبن زيدا» مثلا، فإنَّ نيَّته أن يعذَّبه إن شاء؛ فلو لم يعذِّبه لم يكن كاذبا؛ لأنَّه لم يشأ عذابه؛ بخلاف الوعد فإنَّ اللائق بالكرم أنْ يَبْني إخبارَهُ على الجزم، وقد وجَدْنَا في الحديث مصداق ذلك، وهو قول النَّبِيِّ صلى الله عليه وسلم: «من وعده الله على عمل ثوابا فهو مُنجز له، ومَن أوعده على عمل عقابا فهو بالخيار إن شاء عذبه، وإن شاء غفر له» [مسند أبي يعلى، برقم: ٣٣١٦ ومسند البزار: ٦٨٨٢].
يرد
وأما عن لزوم تبديل القول: فإن الممنوع إنما هو تبديل القول في وعيد الكفار، أو من لم يرد الله تعالى العفو عنه، وهو نحو الجواب عن الموضوع السابق. وأما استتباعه القول بعدم خلود الكُفَّار في النَّار: فإِنَّا لا نُسلّم ذلك؛ لأننا نقرر أنه لا يجوز العفو عن الكفر؛ فيكون ما ذكرناه مقصوراً على عُصاة المؤمنين، وهذا التخصيص مُستفاد من قوله تعالى: (إِنَّ اللَّهَ لَا يَغْفِرُ أَن يُشْرَكَ بِهِ، وَيَغْفِرُ مَا دُونَ ذَلِكَ لِمَن يَشَاءُ) [النساء: ٤٨].
(١) قوله: (الذي سبقت به إرادته الأولى: وعده الذي وعد به على لسان نبيه أو في كتابه، وإلا فالوعد والوعيد بالنظر للإرادة الأزلية لا يتخلفان، وغرضنا التفرقة بينهما أفاده شيخنا، ولك أن تقول: هذا وصف كاشف إشارة إلى أنه يلزم الوعد الإرادة الأزلية ضرورة أنه لا يتخلف، والوعيد قد تسبق الإرادة بغفرانه فتدبر الأمير.
Section of Ilahiyyat. The eternal (al-azal) (1); because the intended (al-murad) does not lag behind the will (al-irada); for if the giving of the promised thing were to lag, lying, foolishness, breaking of promise, and changing of speech would follow, contrary to His statement: 'Indeed, You do not break the promise' [Aal-e-Imran: 194] and 'The word is not changed before Me' [Qaf: 29]. Thus reward (al-thawab) is a favor from Allah, which He promised to the obedient, so He fulfills it for him, because breaking a promise (al-khulf fi al-wa'd) is a deficiency from which Allah must be exalted. Unlike the threat (al-wa'id), for it is not impossible to break it, so it is permissible. The Ash'aris have answered this: As for the necessitation of lying, they said: We do not concede that lying is necessitated; because the generous one, when he informs of a threat, it is befitting to his generosity that he builds his information on the will, even if he does not explicitly state it. If he says, 'I will punish Zayd,' for example, his intention is to punish him if He wills. So if He does not punish him, he is not a liar because He did not will his punishment. Unlike the promise, for it is befitting to generosity that he builds his information on certainty. We have found confirmation of this in the hadith, which is the saying of the Prophet (peace be upon him): 'Whoever Allah promises a reward for an action, He fulfills it for him, and whoever He threatens with punishment for an action, He has the choice: if He wills, He punishes him, and if He wills, He forgives him' [Musnad Abi Ya'la, no. 3316, and Musnad al-Bazzar, no. 6882]. It is objected: As for the necessitation of changing speech, what is prevented is only changing speech in the threat to the disbelievers, or to those for whom Allah has not intended forgiveness, and it is similar to the answer to the previous subject. As for it entailing the statement that the disbelievers will not remain eternally in the Fire, we do not concede that; because we affirm that forgiveness of disbelief is not permissible; so what we have mentioned is restricted to sinning believers. This specification is derived from His statement: 'Indeed, Allah does not forgive that partners be associated with Him, and He forgives what is less than that for whom He wills' [An-Nisa': 48]. (1) His saying: ('which His first will preceded'): His promise which He promised upon the tongue of His prophet or in His book; otherwise, promise and threat, with regard to the eternal will, do not lag; and our purpose is to differentiate between them, as our teacher taught us. And you may say: this is a descriptive attribute indicating that the promise necessitates the eternal will necessarily because it does not lag, and the threat may be preceded by the will to forgive it. So reflect, O Amir.
Source pages p. 337

Commentary
This passage presents the Ash'ari argument regarding divine promises (wa'd) and threats (wa'id). It begins by defining the promise: the intended thing does not lag behind the will, otherwise lying, foolishness, breach of promise, and alteration of speech would occur, contradicting Qur'anic verses that affirm God's faithfulness. Reward (thawab) is a favor promised to the obedient, and God must fulfill it because breaking a promise would be a deficiency unbecoming of His perfection. In contrast, breaking a threat is not impossible, so it is permissible. The Ash'aris then respond to an objection about lying: the objection claims that if God threatens but does not carry it out, He would be lying. The Ash'aris answer that a generous person, when uttering a threat, implicitly conditions it on His will, even if not stated. For example, saying 'I will punish Zayd' means 'if I will it.' If He does not punish, He is not a liar because He did not will it. For a promise, the implication is certainty. This is supported by a hadith where the Prophet states that God fulfills a promise but has choice regarding a threat. Then a second objection is addressed: that changing the threat would be altering speech, which is prohibited. The Ash'aris respond that prohibition of changing speech applies only to threats against disbelievers or those for whom God does not intend forgiveness. As for the implication that this would mean disbelievers are not eternally punished, the Ash'aris reject that because forgiveness of disbelief is impossible; the discussion is restricted to sinning believers, as derived from the verse that God does not forgive shirk but forgives lesser sins for whom He wills. The footnote clarifies that the term 'al-azal' refers to the promise preceded by the first will, emphasizing that promise and threat differ in relation to the eternal will, with the promise being necessary and the threat possibly overtaken by the will to forgive.

Key Terms
تبديل القول (tabdeel al-qawl)
كفر (kufr)
شرك (shirk)
عفو (afw)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Ash'ari conditional threat implies that everything, including punishment of disbelievers, is conditional.
Correction: The passage specifies that the conditional nature applies only to believers; disbelievers are not forgiven because of shirk.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, to whom does the possibility of forgiveness apply?
A) All humans
B) Disbelievers
C) Sinning believers
D) Only prophets

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari position on conditional threat applies only to sinful believers; disbelievers are not forgiven due to shirk.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you commit a sin, do you assume that God's threat of punishment applies to you in the same way as it does to a disbeliever? How does the lesson's distinction between believers and disbelievers change the way you view your own accountability and hope for forgiveness? What specific step will you take today to turn back to Allah with sincere repentance?
?
The passage says that forgiveness of shirk is not permissible. Think of someone you know who is caught in shirk—whether through idolatry, pride, or love of this world. Does this lesson soften your heart toward them or harden it? What is one thing you can do this week to invite them to tawheed without compromising the truth?
?
The footnote says the promise is necessarily tied to God's eternal will, but the threat may be preceded by the will to forgive. Which of these two—hope in mercy or fear of punishment—dominates your relationship with Allah right now? Are you living as if His promise is certain and His threat is just a warning, or are you paralyzed by fear? What would change if you truly believed both?

The Ash'ari and Maturidi Views on Happiness and Misery
Compares Ash'ari and Maturidi positions on the permanence of happiness and misery

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the difference between the Ash'ari and Maturidi positions on happiness and misery, especially regarding the immutability of eternal decree.

Core Idea
Ash'aris fix final destiny in eternity; Maturidis allow change at death.

This lesson explores a key theological disagreement within Sunni Islam: whether a person's ultimate happiness or misery is fixed from eternity (Ash'ari) or can change based on their final state at death (Maturidi). The passage from the matn and its commentary present both views, grounding them in the concept of al-azal (eternity) and iman al-muwafaat (faith at death). Understanding this difference clarifies how each school reconciles divine decree with human responsibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the poetic line and its explanation
Start by reading the line from the matn: 'The happy one's success is with Him in eternity, likewise the miserable one, then it does not change.' Then read the commentary that follows, which defines key terms and introduces the Ash'ari position.
Notice the phrase 'then it does not change' — this hints at the Ash'ari view.
2
Understand the Ash'ari position on eternal decree
The commentary explains that for Ash'aris, happiness (death on faith) and misery (death on disbelief) are decreed in eternity and never change. A person's final state indicates their eternal destiny, regardless of prior actions.
This means a person who dies a believer was always decreed happy, even if they previously disbelieved.
3
Compare with the Maturidi position
Now read the Maturidi view: happiness and misery are defined by present faith or disbelief, and they can change with the final state at death. A disbeliever who dies in faith becomes happy, and a believer who dies in disbelief becomes miserable.
Contrast this with the Ash'ari emphasis on immutability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فوز فوزُ السَّعِيدِ عِنْدَهُ في الأَزَلِ كَذا الشَّقِيُّ ثُمّ لَمْ يَنَتِقل
وأشار إلى اختلافهما أيضًا في السَّعَادة والشَّقاوة بقوله: ومما يجب اعتقاده أنْ يكونَ فَوْزُ السَّعِيدِ أي : ظَفَرُه بحُسْن الخاتمة وإيمانِ المُوافَاة عِنْدَهُ تعالى فِي الأَزَلِ على ما ذهب إليه الأشاعرة، والأزل: عبارة عن القدم، والمراد بالسَّعيد هنا عند الأشاعرة الذي يموت على الإيمان، والمراد بالشَّقي الذي يموت على الكفر؛ والغرض تقرير أنَّ سعادةَ السَّعيد، أي موته على الإيمان، وشقاوة الشَّقي، أي: موته على الكفر، مُقدَّرتان في الأزل لا يتغيران ولا يتبدَّلان؛ فمَن خُتم له بالإيمان دلّ على أنه في الأزل كان من السُّعداء وإن سبقَ ذلك كفر، ومَن خُتم له بالكفر دلّ على أنه في الأزل كان من الأشقياء وإن تقدَّمه إيمان. وذهب الماتريدية إلى أن السعادة هي الإيمان في الحال، والشقاوة هي الكفر في الحال، وإذا مات الكافر على الإيمان فقد انقلب شقاؤه سعادة، وإذا مات المؤمن على الكفر ـ والعياذ بالله تعالى ـ فقد انقلبت سعادته شقاوة.
"The happy one's success is with Him in eternity, likewise the miserable one, then it does not change." And he pointed to their difference also concerning happiness and misery by saying: "And it is necessary to believe that the success of the happy one, i.e., his attainment of a good end and faith at death (iman al-muwafaat), is with Him, exalted, in eternity" according to what the Ash'aris held. Eternity (al-azal) is an expression for pre-eternity. What is meant by the happy one here according to the Ash'aris is the one who dies upon faith, and what is meant by the miserable one is the one who dies upon disbelief. The purpose is to establish that the happiness of the happy one, i.e., his death upon faith, and the misery of the miserable one, i.e., his death upon disbelief, are decreed in eternity, not changing nor altering. Whoever ends with faith indicates that he was in eternity among the happy even if preceded by disbelief, and whoever ends with disbelief indicates that he was in eternity among the miserable even if preceded by faith. The Maturidis held that happiness is faith in the present state, and misery is disbelief in the present state. If the disbeliever dies upon faith, his misery has turned into happiness, and if the believer dies upon disbelief—God forbid—his happiness has turned into misery.
Source pages p. 338

Commentary
This passage explains two contrasting positions on the nature of happiness (sa'ada) and misery (shaqawa) in relation to predestination. It begins with a line from the matn: 'The happy one's success is with Him in eternity, likewise the miserable one, then it does not change.' The commentator then clarifies that the phrase 'success of the happy one' means attaining a good end (husn al-khatima) and faith at death (iman al-muwafaat) according to the Ash'ari school. The Ash'aris define the happy person as one who dies in faith and the miserable as one who dies in disbelief. They assert that both states are decreed from eternity (al-azal) and are absolutely unchangeable: a person's final destiny is fixed regardless of prior deeds. The commentator cites a hadith from the Sahihayn illustrating that a person may perform works of the people of Paradise or Hell until only a short distance remains, but the divine decree (kitab) overrides and determines the outcome. Then the passage presents the Maturidi view: happiness is faith in the present moment, and misery is disbelief in the present moment. Therefore, if a disbeliever dies in faith, his misery has turned into happiness; if a believer dies in disbelief (God forbid), his happiness has turned into misery. This establishes a genuine theological disagreement: the Ash'aris emphasize eternal decree and immutability, while the Maturidis emphasize the possibility of change based on the final state.

Key Terms
السعادة
الشقاوة
الأزل
إيمان الموافاة
الأشاعرة
الماتريدية
الخاتمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Ash'ari view implies that present good deeds are insignificant, since everything is fixed.
Correction: The Ash'ari view does not make good deeds insignificant; rather, the eternal decree is known to God alone, so humans must strive and work as if their actions matter. The decree only becomes apparent at death.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'ari view presented in this passage, what is the relationship between a person's final state (faith or disbelief) and their eternal decree?
A) The final state can change based on one's actions in life.
B) The final state is decreed in eternity and does not change, though a person's prior state may appear opposite.
C) The final state is determined only at the moment of death with no prior decree.
D) The final state is the same as one's present state of faith or disbelief.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari view holds that happiness and misery are eternally fixed and unchangeable, while the Maturidi view holds that they can change based on a person's final state at death.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you hold the Ash'ari view that your final state is eternally fixed, does that make you more diligent or more careless in your faith right now? What does your daily life reveal?
?
The Maturidi view teaches that happiness can change at the last breath. Are you living with the urgency that this moment may be your last opportunity to turn misery into true faith?
?
Both schools agree that the end matters most. What one concrete change will you make today to ensure that your end is upon faith, regardless of your past?

The Eternity of Divine Decree: Happiness and Misery Unchangeable
Ash'ari proof of unchangeable divine decree using impossibility of knowledge change.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari proof that happiness (sa'ada) and misery (shaqawa) are eternally decreed by Allah and cannot change, based on the impossibility of divine knowledge turning into ignorance.

Core Idea
God's eternal knowledge makes final destiny unchangeable.

This passage addresses a foundational Ash'ari doctrine: that a person's ultimate fate — whether happiness (faith at death) or misery (disbelief at death) — is fixed in pre-eternity by God's knowledge. The author first defines key terms like al-azal (pre-eternity) and iman al-muwafah (faith at death), then presents a logical proof: if destiny could change, God's knowledge would become ignorance, which is impossible. This argument grounds the Ash'ari conviction that divine decree is immutable.

Guided Path
1
Define key terms
Start by understanding the definitions of 'pre-eternity' (al-azal) and 'faith at death' (iman al-muwafah). These set the stage for the proof.
Notice the two definitions of al-azal: absence of a beginning or continuation in infinite past.
2
Trace the logical argument
Follow the conditional reasoning: if the final state could change, then God's eternal knowledge would have to change too, which is impossible. This is the core proof.
The author calls this 'self-evidently impossible' (badihi al-istihala).
3
Reflect on the conclusion
Note that the author restates the doctrine: happiness and misery are pre-eternal decrees, not subject to alteration.
Happiness = dying upon faith; misery = dying upon disbelief.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعتقاده أن يكون فوز السعيد ظفره بحسن الخاتمة وإيمان الموافاة عنده تعالى في الأزل على ما ذهب إليه الأشاعرة. والأزل: عبارة عن عدم الأولية أو عن استمرار الوجود في أزمنة مقدرة غير متناهية في جانب الماضي. كذا الشقي أي شقاؤه ووقوعه في سوء الخاتمة وكفر الموافاة أزلي عنده تعالى مثل سعادة السعيد. ثم لم ينتقل كل واحد عما ختم له به، وإلا لزم انقلاب العلم جهلا، وتبدل الإيمان كفرا بعد الموت، وعكسه، وهو بديهي الاستحالة. ومراد المصنف أن السعادة والشقاوة أزليتان أي مقدرتان في الأزل، لا تتغيران ولا تتبدلان; فالسعادة: الموت على الإيمان، والشقاوة: الموت على الكفر; لتعلق العلم الأزلي بهما كذلك.
His belief that the success of the happy one is his attainment of a good end and faith at the time of death with Him Almighty in pre-eternity according to what the Ash'aris hold. And pre-eternity (al-azal) is an expression for non-existence of priority or for the continuation of existence in estimated infinite times on the side of the past. Likewise the miserable one, i.e., his misery and his falling into an evil end and disbelief at death is pre-eternal with Him Almighty like the happiness of the happy one. Then each one does not change from what has been decreed for him; for if it were otherwise, it would necessitate the transformation of knowledge into ignorance, and the change of faith into disbelief after death, and its opposite, and that is self-evidently impossible. And the author's intention is that happiness and misery are pre-eternal, i.e., determined in pre-eternity, not changing nor altering; so happiness is dying upon faith, and misery is dying upon disbelief, because of the attachment of pre-eternal knowledge to them as such.
Source pages p. 339

Commentary
This passage presents the Ash'ari doctrine on the nature of happiness (sa'ada) and misery (shaqawa). It begins by defining the key term: the happy person is the one who dies with faith (iman al-muwafah), and this destiny is pre-eternally known to God. 'Pre-eternity' (al-azal) is then defined in two ways: either as the absence of a prior beginning or as continuous existence through estimated infinite past moments. Similarly, misery is defined as dying in disbelief, and this is also pre-eternally fixed. The core proof follows: each person's final state does not change from what has been decreed. If it could change, it would require that God's knowledge turn into ignorance—since God knows the end eternally, a change would mean His knowledge was wrong, which is impossible. This is called 'self-evidently impossible.' The passage concludes by restating the author's intention: happiness and misery are pre-eternal decrees, unchangeable, with happiness being death upon faith and misery death upon disbelief, because God's eternal knowledge encompasses them exactly as they will occur.

Key Terms
الأزل (al-azal)
إيمان الموافاة (iman al-muwafah)
انقلاب العلم جهلا (transformation of knowledge into ignorance)
بديهي الاستحالة (self-evidently impossible)
السعادة (al-sa'ada)
الشقاوة (al-shaqawa)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's decree can change if a person repents or changes their ways.
Correction: The passage asserts that any change would imply God's knowledge was incorrect, which is impossible. Therefore, one's final destiny is fixed in pre-eternity.

Checkpoint
What would happen if happiness and misery were not eternally fixed, according to the Ash'ari proof?
A) God's mercy would be limited
B) God's knowledge would turn into ignorance
C) Free will would be negated
D) Humans would have no responsibility

Key Takeaway
Happiness and misery are eternally fixed in God's knowledge and cannot change, because change would imply divine ignorance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You believe your final destination is fixed in God's eternal knowledge. Does that knowledge make you more diligent in your faith or more complacent? What would need to change in your daily life to truly live as if your end is already written?
?
If you knew with certainty that you would die in a state of faith, would you take that for granted? If you feared you might not, what specific habits or beliefs would you confront today?
?
The passage declares that change is impossible. Does that thought bring you peace or unease? How does it affect your repentance and your hope in God's mercy – do you truly rely on His decree or try to earn your salvation?

The Eternity and Unchangeability of Sa'adah and Shaqawah
Defines sa'adah and shaqawah as eternally decreed and unchangeable.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari doctrine that happiness (sa'adah) and misery (shaqawah) are eternally decreed and unchangeable, based on divine eternal knowledge.

Core Idea
Sa'adah and shaqawah are fixed in pre-eternity and cannot change, else divine knowledge would become ignorance.

In this passage, the author clarifies a fundamental point of Ash'ari theology: the states of happiness and misery are not temporary or changeable. Rather, they are predetermined in Allah's eternal knowledge. This lesson explains why change is impossible and defines who is truly happy or miserable.

Guided Path
1
Understand the dispute
Read the opening lines that frame the question: do happiness and misery change or not? Note that the answer is they are predetermined in pre-eternity.
The phrase 'azal' refers to pre-eternity.
2
Grasp the logical argument
The passage argues that if they changed, it would require divine knowledge to become ignorance, which is impossible. This is a key rational proof.
3
Define the terms
Learn the precise definitions: sa'adah is dying upon faith, shaqawah is dying upon disbelief. Understand the consequences that follow from each.
The happy person is one whom Allah knew would die a Muslim, even if he previously disbelieved.

السعادة والشقاوة، والخلاف فيهما هل تتبدلان أو لا؟ عدم الأوّليّة، أو عن استمرار الوجود في أزمنة مقدّرة غير متناهية في جانب الماضي. كذا الشقيّ أي: شقاؤه ووقوعه في سوء الخاتمة وكفر الموافاة أزلي عنده تعالى، مثل سعادة السعيد، ثم لم ينتقل كلّ واحد عمّا ختم له به، وإلا لزم انقلاب العلم جهلا، وتبدّل الإيمان كفرا بعد الموت، وعكسه، وهو بديهي الاستحالة. ومراد المصنف - رحمه الله تعالى - أن السعادة والشقاوة أزليتان، أي: مقدّرتان في الأزل، لا تتغيّران، ولا تتبدّلان؛ فالسعادة: الموت على الإيمان، والشقاوة: الموت على الكفر؛ لتعلّق العلم الأزلي بهما كذلك. فالسعيد: من علم الله في الأزل موته على الإسلام وإن تقدّم منه كفر. والشقيّ: من علم الله في الأزل موته على الكفر وإن تقدّم منه إسلام. ويترتب على السعادة الخلود في الجنة وتوابعه، وعلى الشقاوة: الخلود في النار وتوابعه.
Happiness and misery, and the dispute concerning them: do they change or not? [They are] not having priority, or about the continuation of existence in determined times infinite on the past side. Likewise the miserable one: i.e., his misery and his falling into an evil end and the disbelief of final state is eternal with Him, exalted, like the happiness of the happy one. Then each one does not transfer from what he was sealed with, otherwise it would necessitate the turning of knowledge into ignorance, and the changing of faith into disbelief after death, and the opposite, and its impossibility is self-evident. And the intent of the author - may Allah have mercy on him - is that happiness and misery are eternal, i.e., predetermined in pre-eternity, they do not change and do not alter; so happiness: dying upon faith, and misery: dying upon disbelief, because of the attachment of eternal knowledge to them likewise. So the happy one: the one whom Allah knew in pre-eternity would die upon Islam even if disbelief preceded from him. And the miserable one: the one whom Allah knew in pre-eternity would die upon disbelief even if Islam preceded from him. And upon happiness follows eternal abode in Paradise and its consequences, and upon misery: eternal abode in the Fire and its consequences.
Source pages p. 340

Commentary
This passage defines and defends the Ash'ari position that sa'adah (happiness) and shaqawah (misery) are eternal and unchangeable. It begins by stating the point of dispute: whether they can change or not. It then explains that misery, like happiness, is eternally decreed by Allah, and that neither can change because that would require divine knowledge to become ignorance, which is impossible. The author clarifies that what is meant by 'eternal' is that they were predetermined in pre-eternity, not that they are uncreated attributes. The definitions are given: sa'adah is dying upon faith, shaqawah is dying upon disbelief, based on eternal knowledge. It then defines the happy person as one whom Allah eternally knows will die a Muslim, even if he was previously a disbeliever, and the miserable person similarly. Finally, it states the consequences: eternal paradise for the happy, eternal hell for the miserable.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That sa'adah and shaqawah are temporal states that can change during a person's life.
Correction: The passage shows they are fixed by eternal knowledge and cannot change; otherwise divine knowledge would become ignorance.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the consequence of believing that sa'adah or shaqawah can change?
A) It implies that Allah's knowledge is incomplete.
B) It implies that divine knowledge becomes ignorance.
C) It implies that human effort is futile.
D) It implies that repentance is pointless.

Key Takeaway
Sa'adah and shaqawah are eternally decreed based on Allah's knowledge, ending with either eternal paradise or hell, and they cannot change.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your final end is already known to Allah, how does that affect the way you approach repentance and spiritual effort today? Are you living with the urgency of one who knows their seal could be set at any moment, or are you complacent?
?
The passage states that a person may have previously disbelieved but still die as a Muslim. Does this truth humble you when you judge others based on their past, or does it make you examine your own heart for pride about your current faith?
?
If you truly believed that your last moment could be one of disbelief despite years of faith, what would you change about your relationships, your worship, and your attachment to this world starting now?

Allah's Reward and Punishment: Pure Grace and Justice
Al-Laqqani states that reward and punishment are purely by Allah's grace and justice, not obligation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why reward and punishment are not obligations upon Allah but flow from His grace and justice.

Core Idea
Reward is a gift of grace, not a wage earned.

This lesson explores a crucial consequence of Allah's sole creation of actions. Since He creates both good and evil deeds, no action inherently deserves a reward or punishment. Instead, if He rewards, it is by pure grace; if He punishes, it is by pure justice. This distinguishes Islamic theology from any notion of human deservingness.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation to get the author's meaning.
Notice the repetition of the key phrase about pure grace and justice.
2
Identify the branching logic
Look for the word 'fa' (فَ) which indicates this statement is a conclusion from the previous premise that Allah creates all actions.
3
Reflect on human desert
Ask yourself: Do humans deserve reward for good deeds? The passage says no, because Allah creates the deed. Consider how this affects your understanding of gratitude and humility.
4
Connect to broader kalam framework
Understand that this principle refutes the Mu'tazila notion that reward is obligatory. It positions reward as an act of pure grace, affirming Allah's absolute sovereignty.

Source Text

بيان أنه تعالى لا يجب عليه ثواب المطيع ولا عقاب العصاة] ه . فإِنْ يُثبْنَا فَبِمَحْضِ الفَضْلِ وإِنْ يُعَذِّبْ فَبِمَحْضِ العَدْلِ فرَّعَ على وجوب انفراده تعالى بخلق أفعال العباد ، وأنَّه لا تأثير لهم فيها سوى الكسب ؛ فقال : إذا علمتَ أنَّه سبحانه هو الخالق لأفعالنا خيرًا كانتْ أو شرا ، وأنَّ قدرتنا الحادثة ليست مؤثرة في أفعالنا (فَـ) اعتقد أنَّه تعالى (إنْ يُثبنَا) على الخير والطَّاعة (فَ) إثابتُه إِنَّما هي (بِمَحْضِ الفَضْلِ)
An explanation that He, exalted, is not obligated to reward the obedient nor punish the disobedient. Then: If He rewards us, it is by pure grace; and if He punishes, it is by pure justice. He branched on the obligation of His, exalted, being alone in creating the actions of servants, and that they have no effect in them except acquisition. He said: When you know that He, glorified, is the creator of our actions, good or evil, and that our originated power is not effective in our actions, then believe that He, exalted, if He rewards us for good and obedience, His reward is only by pure grace.
Source pages p. 351

Commentary
This passage explains that Allah is not obliged to reward the obedient or punish the disobedient. It branches from the previous proof that Allah alone creates all actions and that humans have no real effect (only kasb). Since both good and evil actions are created by Allah, no action deserves reward or punishment. Therefore, if Allah rewards us, it is by pure grace; if He punishes, it is by pure justice. The word 'فَـ' indicates this branching. The reader is instructed to believe this after knowing that Allah is the sole creator and that our power is not effective.

Misconception: Humans deserve reward for their good deeds because they earn them.
Correction: Since Allah creates all actions, humans do not truly earn reward. Reward is a gift of grace from Allah, not a wage owed.

Key Takeaway
Reward and punishment are not obligations upon Allah; reward is a pure grace, and punishment is pure justice, because He alone creates all actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Reward by Grace, Punishment by Justice
Exposition that reward is pure grace and punishment pure justice, refuting two false views.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that according to Ahl al-Sunna, Allah rewards by pure grace and punishes by pure justice, neither obligated by necessitation (ijab) nor by moral duty (wujub).

Core Idea
Allah's rewards are gifts of grace; His punishments are acts of justice, not debts.

This passage from Al-Shanwani unpacks the famous Ash'ari principle that divine reward is a free gift (faḍl) and punishment is pure justice (ʿadl). Building on the earlier doctrine that Allah alone creates all actions, the commentator explains why human obedience does not entitle one to reward nor does sin necessitate punishment. He contrasts this position with the claims of the philosophers who saw reward as a necessary result, and the Muʿtazila who considered it an obligation upon Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the English translation. Notice the dense commentary style.
The passage begins with the matn line and then expands.
2
Identify the two false claims refuted
Look for the refutation of the philosophers (ijab) and the Mu'tazila (wujub). Understand the difference: ijab means reward follows necessarily without divine choice; wujub means it is a moral obligation upon Allah.
The Ash'ari position rejects both.
3
Reflect on the proof from Ahl al-Sunna
The proof states that no amount of human obedience can repay Allah's blessings, so reward cannot be earned. Obedience and disobedience are merely customary signs (amarat 'adiyya) indicating recompense, not causes.
This preserves divine sovereignty and human responsibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(۱) قوله : (لا عن إيجاب) بحيث تكون الذات علة أو طبيعة تنشأ عنها معلولاتها من غير اختيار أنه يُثيب ولا اختيار له أبدا في الإثابة كحركة الإصبع مع حركة الخاتم. وقوله: (ولا عن وجوب) بحيث يصير مُستحقاً لازما يقبح عليه تعالى تركه ، فيثيبه باختياره لكن مع الوجوب ، فمذهب أهل السنة الإثابة بالفضل الخالص غير مشوب بإيجاب ولا وجوب، فبالفضل: ردُّ لقول الفلاسفة ، وبالخالص : ردُّ لمذهب المعتزلة الشنواني . (۲) الإضافة في قوله: «فبمحض «العدل» من إضافة الصفة إلى الموصوف، نظير ما ذكرناه في قوله السابق: فبمحض الفضل ومعنى العدل المحض وضع الشيء في موضعه من غير اعتراض على الفاعل ، وضده الظُّلْمُ: وهو وضع الشَّيء في غير موضعه من غير اعتراض على الفاعل ، والغرض بيان أنَّ الله الا الله لا تنفعه طاعة من أطاع ، ولا تضره معصية من عصاه، وأنَّ الكل بخلقه وإيجاده ، وأنَّ الطاعة ليست مُستلزمةٌ للثواب ، والمعصية ليست مستلزمة للعقاب، وإنَّما الطاعة أَمَارَة عادية تدلّ على ثواب فاعلها، والمعصية أمارة عادية تدلّ على عقاب=
H. So if He rewards us, it is by pure grace, and if He punishes, it is by pure justice. He branched upon the obligation of His uniqueness in creating the deeds of servants, and that they have no effect in them except acquisition (kash). He said: So if you know that He—glorified—is the Creator of our deeds, whether good or evil, and that our originated power is not effective in our deeds, then believe that He Almighty (if He rewards us) for good and obedience, then His reward is only by pure grace (3). (3) The fa' in the author's saying 'fa-in yuthibna' is a letter indicating branching, and what is branched upon is what preceded of the obligation of believing that He is unique in creating the deeds of servants, and that servants have in voluntary actions only mere acquisition (kash). The reason for branching this upon that is that in reality no good from servants has occurred that deserves reward, nor evil that deserves punishment. And it is possible that the fa' is the fa' of clarification, because it clarifies and makes apparent the answer to an implied understood conditional. 350. Explanation that He Almighty is not obligated to reward the obedient nor punish the disobedient. That is: by His pure grace, which is giving by choice, not by necessitation (ijab) as the philosophers say, nor by obligatoriness (wujub) (1) as the Mu'tazila say. (And if He punishes, it is by pure (1) justice (2)) i.e. His punishment by His pure justice, which is placing a thing in its place without ... from the previous speech referred to. And the estimation of the speech in this way: If you know that Allah Almighty is unique in creating the voluntary deeds of servants, whether good or evil, then know that if He rewards us... etc. And al-mahd (with fat-h on the mim and sukun on the ha) is a description meaning pure from impurities, and its addition to al-fadl is an addition of an adjective to the described. The intended meaning of 'pure grace' is the giving issuing from Him Almighty by complete choice not tainted by any taint of necessitation (ijab) nor any taint of obligatoriness (wujub). The purpose of this speech is to refute two sects: the first sect is the philosophers (al-falasifa), and the second sect is the Mu'tazila. As for the philosophers, they claimed that Allah Almighty rewards the obedient without choice in His rewarding them, because their obedience is a cause from which its effects arise without choice. As for the Mu'tazila, they claimed that rewarding the obedient is due upon Him—glorified—He rewards them by His choice, but because it would be ugly for Him not to do so with them. ... The proof of what Ahl al-Sunna hold: that the obediences of servants, even if numerous, do not fulfill some of what Allah has bestowed upon them that is due upon the servant, so how could one imagine being deserving of compensation for them? Muhy. (1) His saying: 'not by necessitation (ijab)' in such a way that the essence is a cause or nature from which its effects arise without choice, so that He rewards and has no choice at all in rewarding, like the movement of the finger with the movement of the ring. And His saying: 'nor by obligatoriness (wujub)' in such a way that it becomes a necessary due (mustahaqqan lazim) that it would be ugly for Him to leave, so He rewards by His choice but with obligatoriness. So the madhhab of Ahl al-Sunna is reward by pure grace not mixed with ijab nor wujub. By grace (fadl) is a refutation of the philosophers' saying, and by pure (khalis) is a refutation of the Mu'tazila's madhhab. Al-Shanwani. (2) The addition in His saying 'by pure justice' is an addition of an adjective to the described, similar to what we mentioned in His previous saying 'by pure grace'. And the meaning of pure justice is placing a thing in its place without objection to the agent, and its opposite is injustice (zulm), which is placing a thing in other than its place without objection to the agent. The purpose is to explain that Allah—there is no god but Him—no obedience of one who obeys benefits Him, nor does disobedience of one who disobeys harm Him, and that all is by His creating and bringing into existence, and that obedience is not necessarily entailing reward, nor is disobedience necessarily entailing punishment; rather obedience is a customary sign (amara 'adiyya) indicating the reward of its doer, and disobedience is a customary sign indicating the punishment.
Source pages p. 351

Commentary
This passage is a doctrinal exposition and refutation. It begins with the poetic line (matn): 'So if He rewards us, it is by pure grace, and if He punishes, it is by pure justice.' The commentary (sharh) then explains that this statement is a branch (tafri‘) on the preceding doctrine that Allah alone creates the actions of servants, and that servants have only acquisition (kash) without real effect. The author then analyzes the particle fa', interpreting it either as a particle of branching (tafri‘) or as the fa' of clarification (fa' fasiha). The core teaching is that Allah is not obligated to reward the obedient nor punish the disobedient. Reward is defined as pure grace (mahd al-fadl): a gift by choice, free from two forms of necessitation—ijab (philosophers' view that the divine essence necessitates reward like a cause) and wujub (Mu'tazila's view that reward is due upon Allah as a moral obligation). Punishment is pure justice (mahd al-'adl): placing something in its proper place without objection. The passage then explicitly states the purpose: to refute the philosophers (al-falasifa) who claim reward follows from obedience necessarily without divine choice, and the Mu'tazila who claim it is obligatory for Allah to reward. The proof from Ahl al-Sunna is given: no amount of human obedience can fully repay even a fraction of Allah's blessings upon the servant, so how could it deserve compensation? The footnote clarifies that 'by grace' refutes the philosophers (who assert necessitation) and 'by pure' refutes the Mu'tazila (who assert obligatoriness). The passage concludes that obedience and disobedience are merely customary signs (amarat 'adiyya) that indicate reward or punishment, not necessary causes.

Key Terms
فضل
عدل
إيجاب
وجوب
كسب
أمارة عادية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Ash'ari doctrine implies Allah's justice is arbitrary or that reward and punishment are random.
Correction: The passage teaches that while reward is not obligated, Allah has established a reliable customary connection (amara 'adiyya) between deeds and their recompense, based on His wisdom and promise.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the difference between the philosophers' view and the Mu'tazila's view on divine reward?
A) The philosophers say reward is by choice; the Mu'tazila say it is by nature
B) The philosophers say reward is necessary without choice (ijab); the Mu'tazila say it is obligatory upon Allah (wujub)
C) Both say reward is purely voluntary
D) The philosophers deny reward altogether; the Mu'tazila affirm it

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari doctrine affirms that Allah is free to reward or punish as He wills, but He has established a reliable customary link between deeds and their outcomes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly live as if your obedience earns you nothing from Allah, or do you secretly feel entitled to His reward? What would change in your worship if you embraced that it is pure grace?
?
When you see others suffering punishment—whether in this life or the next—do you ever think they 'deserved it' in a way that makes you feel superior? How does the doctrine of pure justice challenge that attitude?
?
If every good deed you do is ultimately a gift from Allah, and your reward is also His grace, then what is left for you to boast about? Will you today thank Him for the very ability to obey Him, and ask for His grace to accept it?

The Mu'tazilite Argument by Analogy
Mu'tazilites present their proof by analogy from human behavior.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite proof that God must act for the optimal benefit of His creatures, based on analogy with human behavior.

Core Idea
If a wise human would do it, they argue God must do it too.

The Mu'tazilites developed a famous argument known as 'qiyas al-gha'ib 'ala al-shahid' (analogy from the seen to the unseen). They argued that just as a wise human ruler is obligated to treat his subjects with kindness and provide what is needed for obedience, so too must God, the wisest of all, act for the optimal benefit of His creatures. In this passage, they present several everyday examples to support this analogy. However, as the commentator will later show, this reasoning mistakenly attributes human needs to God.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazilite argument
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the examples sink in.
2
Identify the three examples
The passage gives three analogies: a wise person failing to enable obedience, a caller to reconciliation using harshness, and a host not giving a warm welcome. Find each one.
They all share the idea that a wise person would not behave this way.
3
Understand the underlying principle
The Mu'tazilites assume that what is blameworthy in a human is also blameworthy in God. This is the core of their analogy.
4
Consider the intuitive appeal
Think about why this argument seems compelling from a human perspective. It appeals to our sense of fairness and wisdom.
Later we will see how the refutation responds.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالوا: نحن نقطع بأن الحكيم بطاعته وقَدَرَ على أن يعطي المأمور ما يصل به إلى الطاعة من غير تضرر بذلك ؛ ثم لم يفعل كان مذموماً عند العقلاء مَعدُودًا في زمرة البخلاء ، وكذلك من دعا عدوه إلى الموالاة والرُّجوع إلى الطاعة لا يجوز أن يعامله بالغِلْظَةِ والدِّينِ ؛ لأنَّه ينفر ولا يقبل ، وإنما يعامله بما أَنْجَعُ في حصول المراد، وأدعى إلى ترك العناد، وأيضا فإنَّ من اتخذ ضيافة لرجل واستدعى حضوره وعلم أنه لو تَلَقَّاه ببشر وطلاقَةِ وَجْه دخل وأكل ، وإلا فلا ؛ فالواجب عليه أن يتلقاه بالبشر والطلاقة والملاطفة ، لا بأضدادها، قلنا: أنَّ الأمر يستلزم الإرادة ـ إنما يكون ذلك من شأن حكيم محتاج إلى طاعةِ الأولياء أو رجوع الأعداء ، يتعزّز بكثرة الأعوان والأنصار، وتعظم لديه الأقدار، لا في حكيم غني كلَّ الغِنَى عن مُوالاةِ الأولياء، قادر كلَّ القدرة على الانتقام من الأعداء ، لا تنفعه طاعة من أطاعه، ولا تضره معاداة من عاداه ، لا يعتز بكثرة الأنصار والأعوان، ولا تخذله الأعداء ولو كانوا بعدد القطر والحصى والتراب.
They said: We are certain that a wise person, by his obedience and ability to give the commanded person what would enable him to reach obedience without harming himself, then if he did not do so, he would be blameworthy in the eyes of the rational and counted among the stingy. Likewise, one who calls his enemy to friendship and return to obedience may not treat him with harshness and hardness, because that would cause him to flee and not accept; rather, he should treat him with what is more effective in achieving the desired goal and more conducive to abandoning obstinacy. Also, one who prepares a banquet for a man and invites him, knowing that if he receives him with cheerfulness and a smiling face he would enter and eat, otherwise not—then it is obligatory upon him to receive him with cheerfulness, amiability, and gentleness, not their opposites. We said: This (i.e., the requirement of will) is only the concern of a wise person who needs the obedience of allies or the return of enemies, who is strengthened by many helpers and supporters, and for whom statuses are great. But not for a Wise One who is absolutely self-sufficient from the friendship of allies, absolutely powerful to take revenge from enemies, whom the obedience of those who obey Him does not benefit, nor does the enmity of those who oppose Him harm Him, who is not strengthened by many supporters and helpers, and whom enemies do not weaken even if they were as numerous as the drops of rain, pebbles, and dust.
Source pages p. 354

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu'tazilite proof for their doctrine by analogy (qiyas) from human behavior, followed by the commentator's refutation. The Mu'tazilite argument: If a wise human ruler has the ability to give a subordinate what would enable obedience without harming himself, but does not do so, he is blamed as stingy. Similarly, if someone calls an enemy to reconciliation, he should treat him gently, not harshly. And if a host knows that a friendly reception will make his guest come in, he must offer it. God is the wisest of all, so He must do the most beneficial for His servants. The commentator refutes: 'We said'—this analogy only applies to a wise person who is needy for allies or enemies' submission, who gains strength from supporters and is concerned with status. But God is absolutely self-sufficient, needing neither friends nor fearing enemies. His power is unchanged by obedience or disobedience, and He is not strengthened by supporters nor weakened by hosts of enemies. Therefore, the Mu'tazilite analogy fails because it attributes human needs to God. The commentator drives home the point by contrasting human rulers, who need to win loyalty, with God, who is free of all need. This refutes the Mu'tazilite claim that God must act for the optimal benefit of His creatures.

Key Terms
قياس الغائب على الشاهد (qiyas al-gha'ib 'ala al-shahid)
بخل (bukhl/stinginess)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazilite analogy is valid because it follows common sense.
Correction: The analogy fails because it attributes human needs and limitations to God, who is absolutely self-sufficient.

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazilite argument, what behavior would be blameworthy in a wise human ruler?
A) Giving a subordinate what is needed for obedience
B) Failing to enable obedience when able without harm
C) Treating an enemy with gentleness
D) Preparing a banquet for a guest

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilites use everyday human situations to argue that God must always act for the best, but this ignores God's absolute self-sufficiency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever demanded something from God as if He owes it to you, based on your own human sense of fairness? What if His wisdom operates on a plane you cannot imagine?
?
When you feel that God is 'harsh' or 'unjust' for not giving you what you think is best, pause: are you judging Him by the same standards you would judge a human friend? Would you be willing to let go of that expectation and trust that His wisdom is higher?
?
If you truly believe that God is not bound by human analogies, how would that change the way you respond to trials? Can you honestly say you live by that belief, or do you still expect Him to act like a wise human?

Refutation of the Mu'tazilite Analogy
Commentator refutes the Mu'tazilite analogy by highlighting God's absolute self-sufficiency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the commentator's refutation, which points out that the analogy fails because God is absolutely self-sufficient and has no needs.

Core Idea
The analogy works for needy humans, not for the Self-Sufficient God.

After presenting the Mu'tazilite argument, the commentator responds with a sharp refutation. The key flaw, he argues, is that the analogies are drawn from human rulers who need loyalty, support, and success. God, however, is absolutely self-sufficient (ghani) and free from any need. He does not benefit from obedience nor suffer from disobedience. Thus, what is blameworthy for a needy human is not necessarily so for Allah. This lesson unpacks that refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation carefully
Read the Arabic and translation of the commentator's response. Notice the contrast he draws.
2
Identify the key contrast
The commentator contrasts a needy human ruler with God. Look for phrases like 'needs the obedience of allies' vs. 'absolutely self-sufficient'.
3
Understand why the analogy fails
The Mu'tazilites assumed God is like a human ruler. The commentator shows that God's attributes (absolute power, self-sufficiency) break the analogy.
4
Reflect on the implications
Think about how this refutation defends the Ash'ari position that God's actions are not subject to human standards of wisdom.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Also, one who prepares a banquet for a man and invites him, knowing that if he receives him with cheerfulness and a smiling face he would enter and eat, otherwise not—then it is obligatory upon him to receive him with cheerfulness, amiability, and gentleness, not their opposites. We said: This (i.e., the requirement of will) is only the concern of a wise person who needs the obedience of allies or the return of enemies, who is strengthened by many helpers and supporters, and for whom statuses are great. But not for a Wise One who is absolutely self-sufficient from the friendship of allies, absolutely powerful to take revenge from enemies, whom the obedience of those who obey Him does not benefit, nor does the enmity of those who oppose Him harm Him, who is not strengthened by many supporters and helpers, and whom enemies do not weaken even if they were as numerous as the drops of rain, pebbles, and dust.

The Mu'tazilite argument: If a wise human ruler has the ability to give a subordinate what would enable obedience without harming himself, but does not do so, he is blamed as stingy. ?

?
If your obedience does not benefit God and your disobedience does not harm Him, why should you still strive to obey? What does this truth demand of your heart today?

The Argument from Gratitude against Al-Aslah
Second proof: if al-aslah obligatory, no thanks due to God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Quranic command to thank God disproves the Mu'tazilite claim that al-aslah is obligatory upon Him.

Core Idea
If al-aslah were God's duty, gratitude would be meaningless.

This lesson examines the second Sunni proof against the Mu'tazilite view that God must do what is most beneficial (al-aslah). The argument hinges on the concept of gratitude: if God were obligated to do something, He would not deserve thanks for it. Yet the Quran repeatedly commands believers to thank God, implying His blessings are voluntary gifts, not obligatory duties.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof statement
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the logical structure: if X were obligatory, then Y would follow; but Y is false, so X is false.
The proof uses a conditional argument (if... then...).
2
Examine the analogy
The author draws an analogy to human actions: returning a deposit or paying a debt does not warrant special thanks because it is merely fulfilling an obligation. Understand how this analogy illustrates the point.
The analogy is meant to be intuitive: we do not thank someone for doing what they are required to do.
3
Connect to Quranic evidence
Read the Quranic verses cited (2:152, 2:47, 31:14) that command gratitude to God. Reflect on why these verses would be meaningless if God’s actions were obligatory.
The verses show that God actively seeks thanks from His servants, implying His favors are voluntary.
4
Synthesize the argument
Combine the logical step, the analogy, and the scriptural evidence to see the full proof: since God commands thanks, His actions cannot be obligatory; therefore al-aslah is not obligatory on Him.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum: the Mu'tazilite position leads to a contradiction with the Quran.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثاني: لو كان فعل الأصلح واجبًا على الله لم يستوجب عليه شكرا، لكونه مؤدياً للواجب عليه، كمَن يرد وديعة أودعها، وكمن يؤدي دَيْنًا لَزِمَه، مع أنه سبحانه قد طلب من عباده أن يشكروه على ما فعل معهم، قال الله تعالى: ﴿وَاشْكُرُوا لِي وَلَا تَكْفُرُونِ﴾ [البقرة: ١٥٢]، وقال: ﴿يَا بَنِي إِسْرَاءِيلَ اذْكُرُوا نِعْمَتِيَ الَّتِي أَنْعَمْتُ عَلَيْكُمْ وَأَنِّي فَضَّلْتُكُمْ عَلَى الْعَالَمِينَ﴾ [البقرة: ٤٧]، وقال: ﴿أَنِ اشْكُرْ لِي وَلِوَالِدَيْكَ﴾ [لقمان: ١٤]، وما لا يحصى من الآيات.
The second proof: If the performance of the most proper were obligatory upon God, He would not deserve thanks for it, because He would be performing an obligation upon Him, like one who returns a deposit entrusted to him, or one who pays a debt owed by him. Yet He, exalted, has asked His servants to thank Him for what He does with them. God Almighty said: 'And thank Me and do not disbelieve in Me' (Quran 2:152), and said: 'O Children of Israel, remember My favor which I bestowed upon you and that I preferred you over the worlds' (Quran 2:47), and said: 'Give thanks to Me and to your parents' (Quran 31:14), and countless other verses.
Source pages p. 356

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof against the Mu'tazilite doctrine. The argument is straightforward: if performing al-aslah were an obligation on God, then His doing so would be like fulfilling a duty, similar to returning a borrowed item or paying a debt — such actions do not call for gratitude because the agent is merely doing what is required. However, the Quran repeatedly commands people to thank God for His favors, implying that His actions are voluntary gifts, not obligatory duties. Thus, the fact that God commands thanks shows that al-aslah is not obligatory on Him; otherwise, those verses would be meaningless.

Key Terms
الشكر
الوجوب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that God deserves thanks even for obligatory actions.
Correction: The proof relies on the human analogy that fulfilling an obligation does not warrant special thanks, as in returning a deposit or paying a debt. The Quran commands thanks for God's favors, which implies His actions are voluntary.

Checkpoint
Why does the second proof argue that al-aslah cannot be obligatory on God?
A) Because God is not subject to obligations
B) Because if it were obligatory, God would not deserve thanks for it, yet He commands thanks
C) Because the Quran says al-aslah is not obligatory
D) Because the Mu'tazilites deny the Quran

Key Takeaway
The command to thank God shows His actions are voluntary gifts, not obligatory duties.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever catch yourself feeling entitled to God's blessings, as if He owes you something? How does this proof expose that attitude as ungrateful? What will you do today to thank God for something you usually take for granted?
?
If you knew that every good thing in your life was simply God repaying a debt, would your heart still leap with gratitude? Be honest: when was the last time you thanked God for a blessing without any sense of deserving it?
?
The Mu'tazilites thought God must always give you the best. When life disappoints you, do you question God's goodness? How does this lesson call you to trust that God's gifts are voluntary and that even hardship is a reason for gratitude? Resolve now to thank Him for one specific trial.

The Third Proof: Infinite Possibilities and the Refutation of the Medical Analogy
The author presents the third proof and handles the objection with a refutation of the medical analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third rational proof that infinite divine power renders the concept of a limited 'most proper' incoherent, and how the Sunni response refutes the Mu'tazilite objection using a distinction in types of addition.

Core Idea
God's infinite power makes a fixed 'most proper' either limited and surpassable or unlimited and unknowable.

Sanusi presents the third proof against the Mu'tazilite doctrine of al-aslah (the most proper). The argument hinges on the infinity of God's power: if God must do the most proper, that most proper must be either bounded or boundless—both lead to problems. The Mu'tazilites object by claiming that adding more could cause harm, like medicine. The Sunni response exposes the flaw in this analogy.

Guided Path
1
Understand the premise
Recognize that the proof starts from the fact that God's power (maqdurat) is infinite. This is the key underlying assumption.
2
Examine the two possibilities
The proof considers two cases for the obligatory 'most proper': either it is of a limited measure or not. Understand the implications of each.
If limited, a more proper is possible; if unlimited, we cannot know what is obligatory.
3
Grasp the Mu'tazilite objection
The opponent chooses the first horn (limited) and argues that adding more could cause corruption, analogizing to a doctor's prescription.
Note the intuitive appeal of the medical example.
4
Analyze the refutation
The author rejects the analogy, distinguishing between adding beneficial to beneficial and adding harmful to beneficial. The extra medicine is not beneficial but harmful in context.
This preserves the proof: adding proper to proper only increases goodness.
5
Synthesize the argument
See how the proof and refutation together establish that the Mu'tazilite position is rationally untenable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والوجه الثالث: أن مَقدُورَات الله تعالى غير متناهية إلى حد، وهذا الأصلح الذي أوجبوه عليه تعالى، إما أن يكون معلومَ المِقدار، أي: محدودًا بحدٍ لا يتجاوزه، وإما ألا يكون كذلك، فإن كان معلوم المقدار محدوداً بحدٍ ينتهي إليه فإن الزيادة على هذا القدر أمر ممكن واقع تحت مقدوره سبحانه، فلا يكون ما وصل إلى الحد المقدر هو الأصلح؛ لأنَّ الأكثر صلاحاً ممكن، وإن لم يكن محدوداً بحد لا يتجاوزه، لزم الجهل بحقيقة الواجب. فإن قيل: نختار الأول، ولا نسلم أنَّ الزِّيادة أصلح؛ لأنه ربما يصير ضم المزيد مفسدة، كما ضم النافع إلى النافع قد مضرة، ألا ترى أنَّ الطبيب يصف قَدْرًا مُعيَّنا من الدواء يكون فيه الشَّفاء، فإذا أضيف قدرٌ آخرُ مِنْه كان مَضرَّةً، فالجواب: أَنَّا لا تعقل أَنَّ ضَمَّ الصَّلاح إلى الصَّلاح يكون فساداً، وقياس ذلك على الدواء والعلاج به قياس فاسد؛ لأن الزيادة في الدواء ليس من باب ضم النافع إلى النَّافع، بل هو من باب ضمّ غيرِ النَّافع؛ بل الضار إلى النافع، ألا ترى أنه يطلب في الحمى مثلاً شرب قدرٍ معلوم من الدواء، يقاوم الحَرَارة الَّتِي تغلب في مثل هذه الحال، فإذا زيدَ على القَدْرِ المطلوب قَدْرٌ آخرُ فَإِنَّ هذا القَدْرَ الزَّائِدَ لا يعمل عمل القدر المطلوب من دفع الحرارة، وإنما يثبت برودة تزيل الصحة والاعتدال.
The third proof: That the things within the power of God Almighty are infinite without limit. And this 'most proper' which they have made obligatory upon Him, exalted, either is of known measure, i.e., limited by a limit it does not exceed, or it is not so. If it is of known measure, limited by a limit at which it ends, then an increase beyond this measure is possible and falls under His power, so what has reached the designated limit is not the most proper, because a more proper is possible. And if it is not limited by a limit it does not exceed, then ignorance of the reality of the obligation necessarily follows. If it is said: We choose the first (i.e., that it is limited), and we do not concede that the increase is more proper; because perhaps adding the extra becomes a cause of corruption, just as adding beneficial to beneficial may cause harm. Do you not see that a doctor prescribes a specific amount of medicine that brings healing, and if a further amount is added, it becomes harmful? The answer: We do not conceive that adding proper to proper becomes corruption. And analogizing that to medicine and treatment is a false analogy; because the increase in medicine is not of the type of adding beneficial to beneficial, but rather of the type of adding non-beneficial—indeed harmful—to beneficial. Do you not see that for fever, for example, a specific amount of medicine is sought to counteract the heat that predominates in such a condition; if an extra amount is added beyond the required amount, that extra amount does not perform the function of the required amount in removing the heat, but rather produces a coldness that removes health and balance.
Source pages p. 356

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof from the infinity of divine power. The argument: God's power is unlimited. The 'most proper' that the Mu'tazilites claim is obligatory must either be limited or unlimited. If it is limited, then a more proper amount is possible, so the limited amount is not truly the most proper. If it is unlimited, then we cannot know what the obligation is, which is absurd. The Mu'tazilite objects by choosing the first horn (limited) and argues that adding more could cause corruption, using the analogy of medicine: a doctor prescribes a precise amount; adding more harms. The Sunni author refutes this: the analogy is false because adding medicine is adding a harmful substance to a beneficial one, not adding beneficial to beneficial. The extra amount in medicine is not beneficial in itself. Therefore, the objection does not apply: adding proper to proper would only increase goodness, not cause corruption. Thus the proof stands.

Key Terms
المقدورات
معلوم المقدار
زيادة الصلاح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Adding more of something good always results in more good, or at least not harm, as with medicine where extra dose causes harm.
Correction: The extra dose in medicine is not adding beneficial to beneficial; it is adding a harmful quantity. In the case of proper actions, adding more proper to proper would only increase goodness, not cause corruption.

Checkpoint
What is the main problem the third proof raises against a limited al-aslah?
A) A limited al-aslah would mean God's power is limited
B) A limited al-aslah would allow a more proper possibility, so it is not truly the most proper
C) A limited al-aslah is impossible because God cannot create limits
D) A limited al-aslah would make God unjust

Key Takeaway
The third proof shows that God's infinite power makes a fixed 'most proper' impossible, and the medical analogy does not refute it because adding harmful to beneficial differs from adding proper to proper.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you pray or ask God for something, do you ever think you know exactly what He should give you? How does this lesson's argument about God's infinite power challenge that assumption? Will you train yourself to say 'perhaps this is not the best for me' more often?
?
The medical analogy shows how easily we compare God's actions to human, limited actions. Where in your daily life do you judge God's wisdom by your own, like a patient questioning a physician? What step can you take today to surrender that judgment?
?
If you truly believed that God's power is infinite and that He always does what is truly best—even when it seems harmful—how would your reaction to a recent trial change? What specific complaint about your life would you need to retract?
