The Fourth Objection: A Reductio on Al-Aslah
A reductio against the Mu'tazila obligation of al-aslah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth objection against the Mu'tazilite doctrine of al-aslah and why it is a reductio ad absurdum.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila's obligation of al-aslah would make killing prophets better than sparing Iblis.

The author now presents a powerful objection. If God is obligated to always do what is most beneficial for people, then an absurd consequence follows: causing the death of prophets and righteous guides while keeping Satan and his followers alive would be considered 'most beneficial'. This conclusion is clearly repugnant, and it reveals the flaw in the Mu'tazilite position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once and then its literal translation. Notice the structure: 'The fourth aspect: that from what they hold it follows...'
2
Understand the reductio
Identify the logical structure: if al-aslah is obligatory (premise), then it would entail a repugnant consequence (death of prophets, life of Iblis). Since the consequence is false, the premise must be rejected.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum.
3
Appreciate the force of the argument
Reflect on why this objection is considered sufficient ('enough as an atrocity'). The absurdity is meant to be self-evident: no one would rationally hold that God's best action is to kill prophets and spare Satan.
This shows the danger of imposing human reasoning on divine actions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الرابع : أنَّه يلزم على ما ذهبوا إليه أن تكون إماتة الأنبياء والمرشدين بعد حين من حياتهم مع تبقية إبليسَ وذُرِّياته من الضالين المُضلين إلى يوم الدين أصلح عندكم لعباد الله ، وكفى بهذا فظاعة.
The fourth aspect: that from what they hold it follows that causing the death of the prophets and guides after a period of their lives while leaving Iblis (Satan) and his descendants among the misguided misleaders alive until the Day of Judgment is [considered] more beneficial (aslah) for God's servants according to you — and this is enough as an atrocity.
Source pages p. 357

Commentary
This passage is a critique of the Mu'tazilite doctrine that God is obligated to do what is best (al-aslah) for His creatures. The author presents the fourth objection (al-wajh al-rabi') in a series. The argument is a reductio ad absurdum: if the obligation of al-aslah were true, then it would logically require that God cause the death of prophets and righteous guides early in their lives while keeping Iblis (Satan) and his misled followers alive until the end of time, because that would allegedly be 'more beneficial' for people. But this conclusion is clearly repugnant and false. The author ends by declaring that this alone is sufficient to show the absurdity of the Mu'tazilite position.

تبقية إبليس
الأصلح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila believed that God is rationally obligated to always do what is most beneficial for people (al-aslah).
Correction: This objection shows that such an obligation would lead to absurd consequences that no reasonable person would accept, thus refuting the doctrine.

Checkpoint
According to the fourth objection, what would the Mu'tazilite doctrine of al-aslah require?
A) God to keep all humans alive equally.
B) God to cause the death of prophets early while keeping Satan alive.
C) God to punish Iblis immediately.
D) God to make all prophets immortal.

Key Takeaway
The fourth objection shows that the Mu'tazilite obligation of al-aslah leads to an absurd and repugnant conclusion, thereby refuting the doctrine.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever caught yourself thinking that God 'should have' done something different in your life? What does this objection teach you about the danger of imposing human standards of 'benefit' on God's actions?
?
This passage confronts us with the absurdity of claiming God must always do what seems most beneficial to us. In what specific area of your life are you most tempted to question God's wisdom? How will you remind yourself that His knowledge surpasses yours?
?
If you truly believe that God does not owe anyone anything, how does that change the way you react when you see injustice or suffering? Will you trust Him even when you don't understand?

Nothing Is Obligatory Upon Allah
The author refutes the aslah obligation and affirms divine freedom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari refutation of the Mu'tazilite claim that God is obliged to do what is best, and the principle that nothing is rationally obligatory upon Allah.

Core Idea
God is bound by no rational obligation; all His acts are permissible.

The Ash'aris defend divine sovereignty by rejecting any notion that God must act in a particular way. In this passage, the author exposes the inner corruption of the Mu'tazilite claim that God must always do what is most beneficial, and establishes that Allah's acts are either of grace or justice, never of compulsion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify its two parts
First, read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that the passage begins with a refutation (naqd) of the Mu'tazilite opinion, then concludes with the positive Ash'ari stance. Mark where the transition occurs.
The refutation uses two arguments; the positive statement follows.
2
Analyze the two arguments against the aslah
The author provides two arguments: (1) If God were obliged to do the best, He would not create the suffering disbeliever or the afflicted believer. (2) If it were obligatory, there would be no room for divine favor and choice. These are supported by Quranic verses.
Consider how these arguments expose the logical flaws in the Mu'tazilite claim.
3
Understand the conclusion: acts are permissible
The passage affirms that nothing is obligatory or impossible for God by reason. All His acts are permissible in themselves, occurring as grace or justice. This is the foundational Ash'ari principle of divine sovereignty.
This ties back to the core idea: God is not subject to any external obligation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نقضُ أهل السنَّةِ لمسألة الإيجاب على الله تعالى خبر المبتدأ ، أي : مُزَيَّنُ الظَّاهر (۱) فاسد الباطن فهو باطل ؛ لأنَّه لو وجب عليه تعالى الأصلح لعباده لما خلق الكافر الفقير المُعذِّبَ في الدُّنيا بالفقر وفي الآخرة بالعذاب الأليم المُخلَّد ، لا سيّما المُبتلى في الدُّنيا بالأسقام والمحن والآفات ، وأيضا لو وجب عليه الأصلحُ لما بقي للتفضيل مجالٌ (۲) ، ولم يكن تعالى خِيرَةً في الإنعام ، وهو باطل ؛ لقوله تعالى : ﴿وَرَبُّكَ يَخْلُقُ مَا يَشَاءُ وَيَختَارُ [القصص: ٦٨] . ويَخْتَصُّ بِرَحْمَتِهِ مَن يَشَاءُ ﴾ [البقرة: ١٠٥] . [ نقضُ أهل السنّةِ لمسألة الإيجاب على الله تعالى] (ما) أي: ليس (عَلَيْهِ) تعالى لخَلْقِه شيءٌ (وَاجِبُ) مِن فعل أو ترك ؛ لأنَّ أفعاله كلَّها جائزة بالنظر إلى ذاتها (۳) ، واقعةٌ على وجه الإحسان والفضل، (٤) أو على وجه المُؤاخذة والعدل، لا يجب منها شيءٌ عقلا ولا يستحيل (4) . = المعتزلة، وإثباتِ ما وردت به السُّنَّة ومضى عليه الجماعة مِن السَّلف، ولذلك سموا أهل السُّنَّة والجماعة .
The refutation by the People of the Sunnah of the issue of obligating [something] upon Allah. It is a predicate that outwardly appears attractive but is inwardly corrupt, so it is false. Because if the most beneficial (al-aslah) for His servants were obligatory upon Him, He would not have created the disbelieving poor person who is tortured in this world by poverty and in the Hereafter by eternal painful punishment, especially the one afflicted in this world with illnesses, trials, and calamities. Also, if the most beneficial were obligatory, no room would remain for favoring (al-tafdil), and the Exalted would not have freedom of choice (khiyarah) in bestowing favors. This is false, due to His saying: 'And your Lord creates what He wills and chooses' (Q. 28:68), and 'He singles out for His mercy whom He wills' (Q. 2:105). [Refutation by the People of the Sunnah of the issue of obligating upon Allah.] (The word ma) means: there is nothing (upon) the Exalted (obligatory) for His creation, whether act or omission, because all of His acts are permissible (ja'iz) with respect to their essence, occurring either on the basis of beneficence (ihsan) and favor (fadl), or on the basis of calling to account and justice ('adl); none of them is rationally necessary (wajib) nor impossible (mustahil).
Source pages p. 358

Commentary
This passage is the Ash'ari refutation (naqd) of the Mu'tazilite claim that God is obligated (wajib) to perform certain acts, specifically the obligation to do what is best (al-aslah) for His servants. The author begins by characterizing this claim as a predicate (khabar al-mubtada') that appears outwardly attractive but is inwardly corrupt (fasiḍ al-bāṭin), hence false. He then provides two main arguments: First, if al-aslah were obligatory, God would not have created the disbelieving poor person who suffers in this world and the Hereafter, nor would He afflict people with diseases and calamities. Second, if al-aslah were obligatory, there would be no room for tafḍīl (preferential favor) among creatures, and God would not have free choice (khiyarah) in granting favors. This contradicts verses such as 'Your Lord creates what He wills and chooses' (Q. 28:68) and 'He singles out for His mercy whom He wills' (Q. 2:105). The passage then affirms the Ash'ari position: nothing is obligatory (wājib) upon God in His actions or omissions. All His acts are rationally permissible (jā'iz) in themselves; they occur either as beneficence and grace (ihsan wa faḍl) or as justice and recompense ('adl). None of them is rationally obligatory nor rationally impossible. This is the foundational Ash'ari principle: God is not subject to any rational obligation; He is the absolute sovereign.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Ash'aris deny that God does good; they claim God could be unjust.
Correction: Ash'aris affirm that God's actions are good by His will and wisdom; they are either acts of grace or justice, but God is not externally obliged to perform them.

Checkpoint

B) Because all of His acts are permissible in themselves and occur by grace or justice, not by reason.
C) Because Allah is not capable of doing some things.
D) Because the Qur'an says so, but reason could require obligation.

Key Takeaway
Allah's actions are not subject to rational obligation; they are either gracious or just, and He is free to choose.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a trial or see injustice in the world, do you secretly feel that God owes you an explanation or a better situation? This lesson says nothing is obligatory upon Him. What would it mean for you to release that sense of entitlement and instead see His actions as either gracious or just, even when you don't understand? Are you willing to say, 'You are not obligated, but I trust You'? What would change in your prayer and patience if you really believed that?
?
You have likely heard that God is 'the most merciful' and 'the just'. This lesson teaches that His mercy is a free gift, not a requirement. When you receive blessings, do you take them for granted as if God had to give them? How would your gratitude deepen if you saw every good thing as a pure favor that He was not obliged to grant? What can you do today to acknowledge that His kindness is a choice, not a debt?

The Argument from Divine Choice Against Obligation
Proves that nothing can be obligatory upon God if He is a free agent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the kalam argument that because God is a free agent, nothing can be obligatory upon Him.

Core Idea
Divine freedom excludes any external obligation.

This lesson presents a concise yet powerful argument from the kalam tradition: if God acts by free choice, then no act or omission can be required of Him. The logic hinges on the definition of a free agent as one for whom both doing and refraining are possible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the concise style of the proof.
The argument is only two lines but contains a complete logical structure.
2
Understand the conditional logic
Identify the conditional statement: 'if an act were obligatory, then God would not be free in it.' Ask yourself why obligation negates freedom.
The definition of freedom (mukhtār) is key.
3
Connect to divine sovereignty
Reflect on how this argument upholds God's absolute sovereignty. No external force can impose obligations on Him.
This is a cornerstone of Ashʿarī theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولأنه تعالى فاعل بالاختيار ؛ فلو وجب عليه تعالى فعل أو ترك لما كان مُختارا فيه ؛ لأنَّ المُختار هو : الذي يتأتى منه الفعل والتّرك .
And because He Almighty is a free agent (fāʿil bi al-ikhtiyār); for if an act or abandonment were obligatory upon Him Almighty, He would not be free in it; because the free agent (al-mukhtār) is one from whom both the action and the abandonment are possible.
Source pages p. 359

Commentary
This passage presents a second argument against the obligation of any act upon God: God acts by free choice (al-ikhtiyār). If an action or its omission were obligatory (wājib) upon Him, He would not be free in that matter. But the definition of a free agent is one for whom both doing and refraining are possible. Therefore, obligation is incompatible with divine freedom, so nothing can be obligatory upon God.

Key Terms
الفاعل بالاختيار
المختار
الواجب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument implies that God's actions are random or arbitrary.
Correction: The argument does not claim randomness; it upholds that God's actions are freely chosen, not compelled. Divine freedom is compatible with wisdom and purpose.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the definition of a free agent?
A) One who acts according to obligation.
B) One for whom both action and abandonment are possible.
C) One who always does the best.
D) One who cannot refrain.

Key Takeaway
God's freedom means He is not bound by any external obligation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If nothing is obligatory upon God, then every blessing in your life is a pure gift. How does that change the way you thank Him today? Will you truly feel gratitude now, or still act entitled?
?
Where in your heart do you harbor the belief that God owes you something—health, wealth, or answers to prayers? Confess that assumption and ask yourself: What does it mean to live as a recipient of pure grace?
?
Since God is free from any external obligation, is your worship a response to His generosity or a transaction you expect Him to repay? Right now, choose one simple act of worship that you will do purely out of love, not out of feeling owed.

Affirming that God Creates Both Good and Evil
Affirms God's power over both good and evil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni position that God has the power and permissibility to create both good and evil, and the Mu'tazilite error in denying this.

Core Idea
God's sovereignty encompasses both good and evil; He is not constrained.

This lesson examines a key line from the Jawharat al-Tawhid that affirms God's creation of both good and evil. The commentator immediately addresses the Mu'tazilite claim that God cannot will evil. By understanding this passage, you will see how Sunni theology upholds God's absolute power over all creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic line and its literal translation carefully. Notice the direct affirmation that it is permissible for God to create both evil and good.
The examples given are Islam (good) and ignorance of disbelief (evil).
2
Identify the Mu'tazilite position
The passage describes the Mu'tazilite claim that God cannot will evil. Find where they state that God wills faith from the disbeliever but not disbelief, and obedience from the sinner but not sin.
3
Understand the implications
Reflect on why the Mu'tazilite view is problematic: if God does not will evil, then evil occurs without His will, which challenges His sovereignty and omnipotence.
The commentary begins to refute this, but the text is cut off.
4
Appreciate the Sunni stance
Contrast the Mu'tazilite view with the Sunni affirmation that God's will encompasses all things, both good and evil, while maintaining that He is just and wise.
This is a foundational point in Sunni Aqeedah regarding Qadr.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٥٣ . وجَائز عَليْه خَلْقُ الشَّرَّ والخَيْـرِ كَالْإِسْلَامِ وجَهْلِ الكُفْرِ
ثُمَّ ردّ على المعتزلة أيضًا في قولهم: إن الله تعالى يمتنع عليه إرادة الشرور والقبائح زعموا أنه تعالى أراد من الكافر الإيمان وإن لم يقع منه ، لا الكفر وإن وقع ، وكذا أراد من الفاسق الطَّاعةَ لا الفِسَقَ ، حَتَّى إِنَّ أكثرَ ما
53. And permissible upon Him is the creation of evil and good, like Islam and the ignorance of disbelief. Then he refutes the Mu'tazilites also regarding their statement that Allah Almighty is prevented from willing evils and ugly actions. They claim that He Almighty willed faith from the disbeliever even if it does not occur from him, but not disbelief even if it occurs; similarly He willed obedience from the sinner, not sin, so that most of what...
Source pages p. 359

Commentary
This passage affirms the Sunni position that God has the power and permissibility to create both good and evil. The poem line states: 'And permissible upon Him is the creation of evil and good, like Islam and the ignorance of disbelief.' This means that God is not forced to create only good; He can create evil as well, because His actions are not constrained by any external obligation. Then the commentary goes on to refute the Mu'tazilite view that God cannot will evil. The Mu'tazilites claim that God wills faith from the disbeliever (but that faith does not occur), and that He does not will the disbelief that actually occurs from the disbeliever. Similarly, they claim that God wills obedience from the sinner, not the sin. The passage begins to refute this by pointing out that such a view leads to contradictions, but the text cuts off. The key point is that God's will is all-encompassing and He creates both good and evil, and whatever occurs is by His will.

Key Terms
الشّرّ
الخير
الإرادة
المعتزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God cannot create or will evil because He is good.
Correction: God creates both good and evil, but evil is not attributed to Him as a deficiency; He creates it for a wise purpose.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the Mu'tazilite position regarding God's willing of evil?
A) God wills both good and evil.
B) God cannot will evil.
C) God wills evil but does not create it.
D) God wills evil for a greater good.

Key Takeaway
God's will and creative power encompass both good and evil, and no event occurs outside His will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that every event in your life, including your failures and sins, is under God's control? How does this affect your view of your own shortcomings and your reliance on Him?
?
When you encounter evil or suffering in the world, do you find yourself subtly limiting God's will to protect His goodness? What would it mean for you to fully accept that He creates both good and evil, trusting His wisdom beyond your understanding?
?
If you are a parent or teacher, do you acknowledge that the choices of those under your care are ultimately created by God? Does this worry you or give you peace, and how does it change your approach to guiding them?

The Permissibility of God Creating Good and Evil
Commentator refutes Mu'tazila and defines good and evil relationally.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni position on God's creation of good and evil, and how good and evil are defined in relation to divine praise and blame rather than intrinsic rational properties.

Core Idea
Good and evil are defined by divine praise/blame, not by rational intrinsic properties.

The Mu'tazila argued that God cannot will evil because evil is rationally abominable. The Sunni response is that God's power is absolute, and good and evil are defined not by reason alone but by their consequences: praise/reward for good and blame/punishment for evil. This passage from the commentary on Jawharat al-Tawhid establishes that it is rationally permissible for God to create both good and evil, and defines these terms accordingly.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation to grasp the overall argument and context.
Notice the heading sets the Sunni stance, then the poem line introduces the permissible.
2
Identify the definitions of good and evil
Focus on the commentary's definition: evil is what is connected to blame in this life and punishment in the next; good is what is connected to praise in this life and reward in the next.
These definitions are relational, not based on inherent qualities.
3
Contrast with the Mu'tazila view
Observe how the Mu'tazila claim that God can only will good, not evil, and that the Sunni refutation rests on the rational permissibility of God creating both.
The commentary also distinguishes Sunni concepts of will from Mu'tazila conflation of will, contentment, and command.

Source Text

٥٣ . وجَائز عَليْه خَلْقُ الشَّرَّ والخيْرِ كَالْإِسْلَامِ وجَهْلِ الكُفْرِ
ثُمَّ ردّ على المعتزلة أيضًا في قولهم: إن الله تعالى يمتنع عليه إرادة الشرور والقبائح زعموا أنه تعالى أراد من الكافر الإيمان وإن لم يقع منه، لا الكفر وإن وقع، وكذا أراد من الفاسق الطَّاعةَ لا الفِسَقَ، حَتَّى إِنَّ أكثرَ ما مِن العباد خِلافُ مُراده تعالى، بنوا ذلك على أصلهم الفاسد مِن الحَسَن والقبيح العقليين بقوله:

(١) قد علمت فيما تقدم في مسألة خلق الأفعال أنَّ مذهب أهل السُّنَّة: أنَّ الله خالق لأفعال العباد الاختيارية، سواء في ذلك خيرها وشرها، وأنَّ العِباد ليس لهم في أفعالهم الاختيارية إلا الكسب، وأنَّ مذهب المعتزلة: أنَّ العباد هم الذين يخلقون أفعال أنفسهم الاختيارية خيرها وشرها، وعلمت أنَّ الكلام في هذه المسألة من فروع الجائز في حقه تعالى، وفروع عموم تعلّق قدرته سبحانه بكل الممكنات؛ فلو أَنَّكَ أبقيت كلام المصنف على ظاهره كان هذا الكلام تكرارًا لما سبق بيانه، لهذا تجد الشارحَ صرف الكلام عن ظاهره بتفسيره «خلق الخير» في كلام المصنف بإرادته سبحانه الخير، وعلى هذا يكون في كلام المصنف مجاز بالحذف، وتقدير الكلام: وجائز عليه إرادة خلق الخير، وعلى هذا يكون الكلام ههنا فرعاً من فروع الإرادة، وقد علمتَ أنَّ مذهب أهل السُّنَّة: أنَّ الإرادة تتعلَّق بالممكنات بأسرها كالقدرة، لكن تعلق تخصيص لا إيجاد، وأن الإرادة عندهم غير العلم والرّضَا والأمر؛ فأما مذهب المعتزلة: فالإرادة والرضا والأمر شيء واحد، ولا تتعلق إلا بما هو خيرٌ، وافقوا أهل السُّنَّة أنَّ الله يريد الخير، وخالفوهم في أنَّه يريد الشَّرَّ، فقالو: يمتنع عليه سبحانه إرادة الشرور والقبائح، وقد تكلَّمْنا عن ذلك فيما سبق كلاماً مستفيضاً، فلا نرى أن نُعيد شيئًا منه ههنا.
(٢) قوله: (خلق الشر) وأما حديث: [صحيح مسلم] لبيك وسعديك، والخير كله في يديك والشر ليس إليك فمعناه أن الشرَّ لا يُتقرب به إليك، ولا يُبتغي به وجهك، أو أن الشر لا يصعد إليك، وإنما يصعد إليك الطيب من القول والعمل [؟].
(٣) قوله: (متعلق المدح دخل فيه الواجب والمندوب. وقوله فيما يلي: (والأحسن تفسيره)= [ناقص]
Statement that God is the Creator of good and evil according to the People of the Sunnah. 53. And permissible upon Him is the creation of evil and good, like Islam and the ignorance of disbelief. Then He refuted the Mu'tazila also in their saying that God, exalted, it is impossible for Him to will evils and abominations. They claimed that He, exalted, willed faith from the disbeliever even if it did not occur from him, not disbelief even if it occurred, and likewise He willed obedience from the sinner, not sinfulness, until indeed most of the servants are contrary to His will, exalted. They built that on their corrupt principle of rational good and evil, by His saying: (And permissible) rationally in our view (upon Him) exalted (the creation) i.e., willing to bring into existence (of evil) by its execution at the hands of servants, and it is what they call abominable, and it is that which is connected to blame in the immediate and punishment in the afterlife; and (the willing to create) (of good) likewise, and it is what they call good, and it is that which is connected to praise in the immediate and reward. (1) You have already known in what preceded in the issue of the creation of actions that the doctrine of the People of the Sunnah is that God is the Creator of the voluntary actions of servants, both their good and evil, and that servants have no part in their voluntary actions except acquisition, and that the doctrine of the Mu'tazila is that servants themselves create their own voluntary actions, both good and evil, and you know that the discussion in this issue is a branch of the permissible in His right, exalted, and branches of the generality of the attachment of His power, glorified, to all possible things. So if you had left the author's words on their apparent meaning, this speech would be repetition of what was previously explained. For this, you find the commentator diverted the speech from its apparent meaning by his interpretation of 'creation of good' in the author's words as His willing good, exalted. And on this, there is in the author's words a metonymy by ellipsis, and the estimation of the speech is: And permissible upon Him is the willing to create good. And on this, the speech here is a branch of the branches of will. And you have known that the doctrine of the People of the Sunnah is that the will attaches to all possible things like power, but attachment of specification not bringing into existence, and that the will in their view is other than knowledge, contentment, and command. As for the doctrine of the Mu'tazila, will, contentment, and command are one thing, and it attaches only to what is good. They agreed with the People of the Sunnah that God wills good, and they differed from them in that He wills evil. They said: It is impossible for Him, glorified, to will evils and abominations. And we have spoken about that in what preceded extensively, and we do not see repeating anything from it here. (2) His saying: (creation of evil) — as for the hadith [Sahih Muslim]: 'Here I am at Your service, and all good is in Your hands, and evil is not attributed to You,' its meaning is that evil is not used to draw near to You, nor is Your face sought by it, or that evil does not ascend to You, but only good speech and action ascend to You [؟]. (3) His saying: (connected to praise) includes the obligatory and the recommended. And his saying in what follows: (and the best interpretation is) = [incomplete]
Source pages p. 359

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazila on the issue of God's creation of good and evil, and a definition of these terms according to the People of the Sunnah. The commentator begins by stating the heading: 'Statement that God is the Creator of good and evil according to the People of the Sunnah.' Then he cites verse 53 of the poem: 'And permissible upon Him is the creation of evil and good, like Islam and the ignorance of disbelief.' This sets up the Sunni position that God is free to create both good and evil, contrary to the Mu'tazila. The commentary then explains that this refutes the Mu'tazila who claim that God cannot will evil or abominations. The Mu'tazila argue that God willed faith from the disbeliever but the disbeliever did not perform it, while God did not will disbelief even though it occurred; similarly, God willed obedience from the sinner, not sinfulness. The commentator says they built this on their corrupt principle of rational good and evil (al-husn wa al-qubh al-'aqliyyayn). The Sunni response is that it is rationally permissible for God to create evil and good. The commentator defines evil (sharr) as that which the Mu'tazila call 'abominable' (qabih), and it is connected to blame (dhamm) in this life and punishment ('iqab) in the next. Good (khayr) is what they call 'good' (hasan), connected to praise (madh) in this life and reward (thawab) in the next. This definition is relational, not intrinsic. The commentator then adds a long footnote (1) clarifying that the author's phrase 'creation of good and evil' should be interpreted as 'willing to create', to avoid repetition of the earlier discussion on the creation of actions. He explains that the Sunni view on will is that it attaches to all possible things, but it is a specification not creation, and that will is distinct from knowledge, contentment, and command. The Mu'tazila, however, equate will, contentment, and command, and they say will only attaches to good, not evil. The commentator notes that this was discussed previously. Footnote (2) addresses the hadith 'Evil is not attributed to You', explaining that it means evil is not a means to draw near to God nor does it ascend to Him. Footnote (3) begins to note that 'connected to praise' includes obligatory and recommended acts. The passage overall defends Sunni divine power and will against Mu'tazila rationalism.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that defining evil as blameworthy implies God is blameworthy for creating it.
Correction: The passage shows that God's creation of evil is part of His absolute power and is not subject to human standards. Evil is only blameworthy for the servant who acquires it, not for God who creates it.

Checkpoint
According to the People of the Sunnah as explained in this passage, what is 'evil' (sharr)?
A) What is intrinsically harmful in itself regardless of context.
B) What is connected to blame in this life and punishment in the next.
C) What is forbidden by God's law.
D) What causes suffering to others.

Key Takeaway
Good and evil are not intrinsic qualities but are defined by their connection to divine praise/reward and blame/punishment, affirming God's absolute power to create all actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a calamity or witness evil in the world, do you instinctively blame Allah or question His goodness? How does the lesson's teaching that Allah creates both good and evil, and that evil is defined by blame in this life but not by intrinsic wrongness, challenge your reaction? Will you now train yourself to say 'Alhamdulillah' even in hardship, recognizing that Allah's creation of evil does not diminish His perfection?
?
The Mu'tazila argued that Allah cannot will evil, so they reinterpreted the disbeliever's disbelief as contrary to Allah's will. If you truly believe that Allah wills everything that happens—including your own sins—how does that affect your repentance? Do you use divine decree as an excuse to persist in sin, or does it drive you to seek His forgiveness with deeper humility, knowing that He willed your sin but also commands you to repent? Make a firm intention today to never use predestination as a justification for disobedience.
?
The hadith says 'evil is not attributed to You,' yet the lesson affirms Allah creates evil. This tension can be unsettling. Will you allow this apparent paradox to weaken your faith in Allah's wisdom, or will you surrender to His absolute sovereignty and praise Him for both the good and the evil He ordains? Commit now to trusting that Allah's decree is always good, even when you cannot see it, and to glorify Him for everything that befalls you.

Allah Creates Good and Evil: Definitions and Refutation of Mu'tazila
Author defines good and evil, asserts Allah creates both, and refutes Mu'tazila.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari position on good and evil: that Allah creates both, but distinguishes between His will (which encompasses all things) and His approval (which is only for good).

Core Idea
Allah wills all things, but only approves of good.

This passage from the commentary on Jawharat al-Tawhid establishes a foundational Ash'ari doctrine: both good and evil are created by Allah. The author addresses a common misunderstanding by distinguishing between divine will and divine approval. He also refutes the Mu'tazila who argue that evil cannot be willed by Allah. Let's unpack this teaching step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then the literal translation. Notice the structure: the author first states the position, then defines terms, then gives proofs.
The passage uses a poetic line (from the poem) and then expands on it.
2
Identify the definitions of good and evil
The author defines 'good' (al-khayr) as what is connected to praise and reward, and 'evil' (al-sharr) as what is connected to blame and punishment. He then offers a more comprehensive definition of good to include permissible actions.
Note that the author prefers to define good as 'what is not connected to blame and punishment'.
3
Understand the distinction between will and approval
The author clarifies that Allah's will encompasses both good and evil, but His approval (riḍā) and love (maḥabba) are only for good. This is supported by Quranic verses (39:7 and 7:28).
This distinction is crucial to avoid the misconception that whatever Allah wills He must also approve of.
4
See the refutation of the Mu'tazila
The author argues that if evil were not willed by Allah, then most events in His kingdom would be outside His will, which contradicts the hadith 'What Allah wills happens, and what He does not will does not happen.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بيانُ أنَّ الله خالق الخير والشر عند أهل السُّنَّة مِن العباد خِلافُ مُراده تعالى، بنوا ذلك على أصلهم الفاسد مِن الحَسَن والقبيح العقليين بقوله: (وَجَائِز) عقلا عندنا (عَلَيْهِ) تعالى ( خَلْقُ ) أي : إرادة إيجاد (الشَّرِّ) بإجرائه على أيدي العباد ، وهو ما يعبرون عنه بالقبيح، وهو ما يكون مُتعلق : الدم في العاجل، والعِقاب في الآجل (و) إرادة خلق (الخَيْرِ) كذلك ، وهو ما يعبرون عنه بالحسن، وهو ما يكون مُتعلَّقَ المَدح في العاجل ، والثَّواب في الآجل، والأحسنُ تفسيره : بما لا يكون متعلَّقا للذَّم والعِقاب؛ ليشمل المباحَ ، وهذا واقع عندنا برضاه تعالى ومحبَّته ، أي ترك الاعتراض على فاعله ، والأوّل بخلافه ؛ لما على فاعله من الاعتراض قال الله تعالى: ﴿وَلَا يَرْضَى لِعِبَادِهِ الكفر﴾ [الزمر: ٧]. ﴿ إِنَّ اللَّهَ لَا يَأْمُرُ بِالْفَحْشَاءِ ﴾ [الأعراف: ۲۸] . وكلاهما واقع عندنا بإرادته تعالى؛ لأنَّ إرادته تعالى متعلقة بكل ممكن كائن ، غير متعلقة بما ليس بكائن ، لقوله : «ما شاء الله كان ، وما لم يشأ لم يكن». ويلزم على ما ذهب إليه المعتزلة أنّ أكثر ما يقعُ في ملكه تعالى غيرُ مرادٍ له. ومَثَّل للشرّ والخير على طريق اللف والنَّشر المشوش ؛ فمثل للخير بقوله: (كَالإِسْلَامِ أي كإرادته تعالى خَلْق الإسلام فيمن شاء من عباده، ومَثَل للشَّرِّ بقوله : (وَجَهْل الكُفْرِ) أي : وكإرادته تعالى خلق ما ذُكر فيمن أراد من عباده ، وتقدَّم تعريف الجهل وانقسامه إلى بسيط ومركّب ، والكفر : ضدُّ الإيمان ، فهو : إنكار ما عُلِم مَجيءُ النَّبِيِّ صلى الله عليه وسلم به من الدين بالضرورة.
An explanation that Allah is the creator of good and evil according to the Ahl al-Sunna, contrary to the will of the servants—they built that on their corrupt principle of rational good and evil—by his saying: 'And it is rationally permissible for Him, exalted, to create—meaning the will to bring into existence—evil, by causing it to occur at the hands of servants, and it is what they refer to as the ugly (al-qabīḥ), that which is connected to blame in the immediate term and punishment in the later term; and the will to create good likewise, and it is what they refer to as the beautiful (al-ḥasan), that which is connected to praise in the immediate term and reward in the later term. And the best interpretation of it is: that which is not connected to blame and punishment, so as to include the permissible (al-mubāḥ). And this [the permissible] exists according to us with His pleasure and love, i.e., withholding objection against its doer, unlike the first [evil], because its doer is subject to objection. Allah said: 'And He does not approve for His servants disbelief' (Quran 39:7) and 'Indeed, Allah does not command immorality' (Quran 7:28). And both [good and evil] exist according to us by His will, because His will is connected to every possible thing that occurs, and not connected to what does not occur, due to His saying: 'What Allah wills happens, and what He does not will does not happen.' And it necessarily follows from what the Mu'tazila hold that most of what occurs in His kingdom is not willed by Him. And he gave an example of evil and good by the method of confusing dispersal and collection; so he gave an example for good by his saying: 'such as Islam'—that is, like His will to create Islam in whomever He wills of His servants—and he gave an example for evil by his saying: 'and ignorance of disbelief'—that is, like His will to create what was mentioned in whomever He wills of His servants. And the definition of ignorance and its division into simple and compound has preceded; and disbelief is the opposite of faith, so it is the denial of what is known necessarily from the religion brought by the Prophet, peace be upon him.
Source pages p. 360

Commentary
This passage is a doctrinal exposition and proof. The author begins by stating the fundamental Ash'ari position: Allah is the creator of both good and evil, contrary to the will of servants. He notes that the Mu'tazila built their opposing view on a corrupt foundation of rational good and evil (al-ḥasan wa-l-qabīḥ al-'aqliyyān). Then he cites the poetic line 'Wa-jā'iz 'alayhi khalqu al-sharri wa-l-khayr' and explains that 'khalq' here means 'irādat ījād' (will to bring into existence). He defines evil (al-sharr) as what is called 'ugly' (qabīḥ), which is connected to blame in this world and punishment in the next, and good (al-khayr) as what is called 'beautiful' (ḥasan), connected to praise and reward. He then offers a more comprehensive definition of good: what is not subject to blame and punishment, thus including permissible actions (mubāḥ). The author clarifies that permissible actions occur with Allah's pleasure and love (i.e., no objection), while evil occurs without His pleasure (as per Quran 39:7 and 7:28) but both occur by His will. He supports this with the prophetic hadith: 'What Allah wills happens, and what He does not will does not happen.' From this, he argues that if the Mu'tazila were correct, most events in Allah's dominion would not be willed by Him, which is absurd. Finally, he gives examples: good as Islam (i.e., Allah willing to create Islam in someone) and evil as ignorance of disbelief (jahl al-kufr), defining kufr as the denial of what is necessarily known from the Prophet's message.

الحسن والقبيح
الإرادة
الرضا

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that because Allah does not approve of evil, He does not will it.
Correction: The passage clarifies that will and approval are distinct; Allah wills everything that occurs, but does not approve of evil.

Checkpoint
According to the author, how does the Ash'ari position on evil differ from that of the Mu'tazila?
A) The Ash'aris deny that Allah creates evil; the Mu'tazila affirm it.
B) The Ash'aris affirm that Allah creates evil by His will, while the Mu'tazila deny that Allah wills evil.
C) Both affirm that Allah creates evil, but the Ash'aris deny that He wills it.
D) The Ash'aris say evil is not created by Allah at all.

Key Takeaway
Allah creates all things, good and evil, by His will, but He only approves of good, and this is proven by Quran and hadith.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a trial or evil in your life, do you truly believe that it happened by Allah's will, and yet He does not approve of it? How does that shape your trust in Him while you strive to avoid sin?
?
The Mu'tazila denied that Allah wills evil because they thought it would make Him unjust. But this lesson shows that Allah's will is distinct from His command. Is there any action in your life that you know Allah has willed (since it happened) but that you are still accountable for? How does this accountability move you to repentance?
?
You recite 'What Allah wills happens, what He does not will does not happen.' Do you live as if every moment—including your mistakes and blessings—is under His control, or do you sometimes feel things are random? What would change if you internalized this?

Definition of al-Hasan and Divine Will in Ash'ari Theology
Ibn al-Sanūsī defines al-hasan and explains the Ash'ari position on divine will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari definition of al-hasan as that which is not subject to blame, and how this relates to divine will encompassing all actions, including evil.

Core Idea
The good is defined by absence of blame, and both good and evil occur by God's will.

This passage from Ibn al-Sanūsī's Jawhara establishes a key distinction: al-hasan (the good) is not defined by divine command but by being free from blame. It then affirms that all events, good and evil, are willed by Allah, refuting the Mu'tazila who limit divine will to good only.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of al-hasan
Read the opening definition carefully: al-hasan is that which is not a basis for blame and punishment, including the permissible. Note that this is the 'best' interpretation.
This definition is broader than common usage.
2
Connect to divine approval and will
Notice that good actions occur by God's satisfaction and love, while evil actions are disliked but still occur by His will. Both are actual by His will, as supported by the hadith.
God's will is comprehensive; His approval is selective.
3
Recognize the Mu'tazili error
The text draws a consequence: if evil were not willed by God, then most events in His kingdom would be outside His will, which is absurd. This refutes the Mu'tazila position.
The Mu'tazila deny that God wills evil.
4
Examine the examples and definitions
The good is exemplified by Islam, evil by disbelief. The text also defines ignorance and disbelief. These clarify the types of actions under discussion.
The chiastic order (laf wa nashr) is a rhetorical device.
5
Review the footnote on classification
The commentary by al-Shanwānī expands the definition to include obligatory, recommended, permissible, disliked, and contrary to the better. It also notes the disagreement between Mu'tazila and Ahl al-Sunna, and the preferred view of Imām al-Ḥaramayn.
This shows the nuance in classifying moral actions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قسم الإلهيات
في الآجل، والأحسنُ تفسيره : بما لا يكون متعلَّقا للذَّم والعِقاب؛ ليشمل المباحَ ، وهذا واقع عندنا برضاه تعالى ومحبَّته ، أي ترك الاعتراض على فاعله ، والأوّل بخلافه ؛ لما على فاعله من الاعتراض قال الله تعالى: ﴿وَلَا يَرْضَى لِعِبَادِهِ الكفر [الزمر: ٧]. ﴿ إِنَّ اللَّهَ لَا يَأْمُرُ بِالْفَحْشَاءِ ﴾ [الأعراف: ۲۸] . وكلاهما واقع عندنا بإرادته تعالى؛ لأنَّ إرادته تعالى متعلقة بكل ممكن كائن ، غير متعلقة بما ليس بكائن(١) ، لقوله : «ما شاء الله كان ، وما لم يشأ لم يكن (۲). ويلزم على ما ذهب إليه المعتزلة أنّ أكثر ما يقعُ في ملكه تعالى غيرُ مرادٍ له. ومَثَّل للشرّ والخير على طريق اللف والنَّشر المشوش ؛ فمثل للخير بقوله: (كَالإِسْلَامِ أي كإرادته تعالى خَلْق الإسلام فيمن شاء من عباده، ومَثَل للشَّرِّ بقوله : (وَجَهْل الكُفْرِ (۳)) أي : وكإرادته تعالى خلق ما ذُكر فيمن أراد من عباده ، وتقدَّم تعريف الجهل وانقسامه إلى بسيط ومركّب ، والكُفر : ضدُّ الإيمان (٤) ، فهو : إنكار ما عُلِم مَجيءُ النَّبِيِّ صلى الله عليه وسلم به من الدين بالضرورة،

أي تفسير الحسن بما لا يكون متعلقا للذم والعقاب فيشمل الواجب ، والمندوب ، والمباح ، والمكروه، وخلاف الأولى ، فهذه الأمور كلها حسنة كما هو ظاهر ، وهو جري على اصطلاح المعتزلة ، فإنهم جعلوا المكروه من قسم الحسن، وأهل السنة اصطلح كثير منهم على أنه قسم من القبيح، فإن المنهي عنه مطلقا قبيح، والأحسن ما قاله إمام الحرمين: إن المكروه ويدخل فيه خلاف الأولى ليس حسنا ولا قبيحا. الشنواني.
Section on Divinities. Regarding the term [al-ājil?], and the best interpretation of it [al-ḥasan] is: that which is not a basis for blame and punishment, so that it includes the permissible. And this is actual (wāqiʿ) with us by His satisfaction and love, i.e., not objecting to the doer of it. And the first [al-awwal?] is contrary to it, because there is objection to its doer. Allah the Exalted said: {And He does not approve for His servants disbelief} [39:7]; {Indeed Allah does not command immorality} [7:28]. And both are actual with us by His will the Exalted, because His will is attached to every contingent existence, and not attached to what is not existence, due to his saying: 'What Allah wills happens, and what He does not will does not happen.' And it follows from what the Muʿtazila hold that most of what occurs in His dominion is not intended by Him. And he gave an example for evil and good in the manner of chiastic order (al-laf wa al-nashr al-mushawwash); he gave an example for good with his saying: (like Islam), i.e., like His willing the creation of Islam in whom He wills from His servants. And he gave an example for evil with his saying: (and ignorance of disbelief), i.e., like His willing the creation of what was mentioned in whom He wills from His servants. And the definition of ignorance and its division into simple and compound has preceded. And disbelief is the opposite of faith, so it is denial of what is known by necessity to have been brought by the Prophet (peace be upon him) from the religion.

That is, interpretation of al-ḥasan as that which is not a basis for blame and punishment, so it includes the obligatory, the recommended, the permissible, the disliked, and contrary to the better (khilāf al-awlā). So these matters are all good (ḥasana) as is apparent, and this follows the terminology of the Muʿtazila, for they made the disliked part of the good. Many of the Ahl al-Sunna made it part of the ugly (qabīḥ), for what is forbidden absolutely is ugly. And the best is what Imām al-Ḥaramayn said: The disliked, and included in it contrary to the better, is neither good nor ugly. Al-Shanwānī.
Source pages p. 361

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'al-ḥasan' (the good) in the context of divine attributes and actions. The author begins by stating that the best interpretation of al-ḥasan is 'that which is not a basis for blame and punishment', thereby expanding it to include the permissible. He then explains that what is good (like Islam) occurs by God's satisfaction and love, meaning He does not object to its doer, while what is evil (like disbelief) is contrary to this because there is objection to its doer, citing two Quranic verses to show that God does not approve of disbelief or command immorality. Both good and evil are actual by God's will, as God's will attaches to every possible existent, in line with the prophetic hadith. The author then draws a necessary consequence of the Mu'tazila position: that most events in God's kingdom would not be intended by Him. He clarifies an example given in the text using the rhetorical device of chiastic order (al-laf wa al-nashr al-mushawwash): good is exemplified by Islam (i.e., God's willing creation of Islam in some servants), and evil by disbelief. Finally, he provides definitions of ignorance (simple and compound) and disbelief as the denial of what is known from the Prophet by necessity. A footnote by al-Shanwānī expands the definition of al-ḥasan to include obligatory, recommended, permissible, disliked, and contrary to the better, noting that the Mu'tazila consider the disliked as part of the good, while many Ahl al-Sunna consider it part of the ugly. The preferred view of Imām al-Ḥaramayn is that the disliked (including contrary to the better) is neither good nor ugly.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'al-ḥasan' is defined by divine command rather than by absence of blame.
Correction: The passage defines al-ḥasan as 'that which is not a basis for blame and punishment', not as what God commands.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Al-ḥasan is defined by not being subject to blame or punishment, and all actions, good and evil, are willed by Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt that a tragedy or injustice in your life was outside God's control? How does this lesson's teaching that even evil occurs by His will reshape your trust in His sovereignty? Commit today to saying 'What Allah wills happens' even in the hardest moments.
?
When you sin, do you separate God's will from His approval? This lesson says He wills your sin but does not approve. Does that knowledge make you feel more accountable or less? How will you use this to deepen your repentance?
?
The Mu'tazila believed that God cannot will evil because that would contradict His goodness. Are there times you have judged God's actions by your own standard of good? Will you now accept His definition of good—that which He does not blame? Write down one situation where you will intentionally submit to His decree despite not understanding.

Qadar in Ash'ari and Maturidi Thought
Defines qadar from Ash'ari and Maturidi perspectives and harmonizes them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definitions of qadar according to the Ash'ari and Maturidi schools and see how they are harmonized.

Core Idea
Qadar is one truth expressed with two complementary wordings.

The concept of divine predestination (qadar) is central to Islamic theology, but its precise definition varies between schools. In this passage, the author presents the definitions of the Ash'aris and Maturidis, then explains that the apparent difference is merely verbal. Both ultimately point to the same reality: God's eternal knowledge, power, and will determining all things.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text slowly. Notice the key terms: قدر, أشاعرة, ماتريدية. The passage has three parts: the Ash'ari definition, the Maturidi definition, and a harmonizing statement.
The phrase 'قسم الإلهيات' is a heading; the definition begins with 'وهو عند الأشاعرة'.
2
Compare the two definitions
Identify the core elements of each definition. For Ash'aris: إيجاد (creating) with a specific measure (قدر مخصوص) in accordance with prior knowledge. For Maturidis: تحديد (determining) each creature by its limit (حد) eternally, including moral and physical aspects.
The Maturidi definition is more detailed, listing good/evil, benefit/harm, time/place, obedience/disobedience, etc.
3
Understand the harmonization
Read the last sentence: 'والظاهر أنَّه اختلاف عبارة' – the apparent difference is verbal. Both definitions go back to the same statement: God knew measures and times before creating, then created accordingly.
This shows that theological differences can sometimes be reconciled by looking at the underlying meaning.
4
Take the checkpoint quiz
Answer the question to test your understanding of the relationship between the two definitions.
The correct answer is that they are the same in meaning but differ in wording.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قسم الإلهيات
[ تعريف القدر عند أهل السنة]
وهو عند الأشاعرة : إيجاد الله تعالى الأشياء، على قَدْرٍ مخصوص، وتقدير مُعيَّن في ذواتها وأحوالها، طبق ما سَبقَ به العِلم.
وعند الماتُريديَّة: تحديده تعالى (۱) أزلا كلَّ مخلوق بحده الذي يوجد به ، من حسن وقبيح، ونفع وضر ، وما يحويه من زمان ومكان، وما يترتب عليه من طاعة وعصيان وثواب وعقاب وغفران.
والظاهر أنَّه اختلاف عبارة ؛ فهما راجعان إلى قول بعضهم مِن القَدَر: أنَّ الله تعالى علم مقادير الأشياء، وأزمانَها قبل إيجادها، ثمَّ أوجد ما سَبَق في عِلمه أنَّه يُوجد ؛ فكلُّ مُحدثٍ صادر عن علمه، وقدرته ، وإرادته .
The Section of Divine Matters. The definition of Qadar according to the Ahl al-Sunna. And it according to the Ash'aris: the creation by God of things upon a specific measure and a determined estimation in their essences and states, in accordance with what preceded in knowledge. And according to the Maturidis: His (Exalted) determining (1) eternally every creature by its limit by which it exists, of good and evil, benefit and harm, and what it contains of time and place, and what follows upon it of obedience and disobedience, reward and punishment, and forgiveness. And the apparent is that it is a difference in wording; for both go back to the statement of some of them concerning Qadar: that God knew the measures of things and their times before creating them, then He created what He knew in His knowledge that it would be created; so every originated thing proceeds from His knowledge, power, and will.
Source pages p. 365

Commentary
This passage provides definitions of qadar (divine predestination) from the two major Sunni theological schools: Ash'aris and Maturidis. According to the Ash'aris, qadar is God's act of creating things with a specific measure and determined estimation, in accordance with His prior knowledge. According to the Maturidis, it is God's eternal determination of every creature by its limit (had) that defines its existence, including its moral value (good/evil), benefit/harm, time/place, and the consequences (obedience, disobedience, reward, punishment, forgiveness). The author then notes that the apparent difference is merely verbal, as both definitions refer to the same reality: God knew the measures and times of things before creating them, then created what He foreknew. Thus every originated thing comes from His knowledge, power, and will.

Key Terms

أشاعرة
ماتريدية
تحديد
إيجاد
علم
قدرة
إرادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How do the Ash'ari and Maturidi definitions of qadar relate to each other?
A) They are contradictory.
B) They are the same in meaning but differ in wording.
C) The Ash'ari definition is more precise.
D) The Maturidi definition is rejected.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari and Maturidi definitions of qadar, though worded differently, both affirm that everything proceeds from God's eternal knowledge, power, and will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Qada: Linguistic and Maturidi Technical Definition
Defines qada linguistically as judgment and gives the Maturidi technical definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that qada linguistically means judgment and that in Maturidi theology it is an attribute of God's essence, not an act.

Core Idea
Qada is God's eternal decree as an essential attribute, not a temporal act.

This passage clarifies the term al-qada (divine decree) by first stating its linguistic meaning—judgment—and then explaining the Maturidi technical position that qada is an attribute of God's essence, not an action. The discussion also touches on a related hadith to prevent misuse of predestination arguments in this life.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get the full context. Notice that the text moves from linguistic definition to technical nuance and then to a hadith commentary.
The passage includes footnotes (1) and (2) that are integral to the technical definition.
2
Identify the linguistic meaning
Find where the passage states the linguistic meaning of qada. It is explicitly given as 'al-hukm' (judgment).
This is a straightforward definition.
3
Understand the Maturidi technical definition
Look at the footnote marked (1). It explains that 'determining' (tahdid) refers to God's eternal specifying and knowledge, and that qada is not an attribute of action but of essence according to the Maturidis.
This distinguishes the Maturidi view from others who might consider qada an act.
4
Note the hadith clarification
The commentary on the hadith of Adam and Moses illustrates that the debate about predestination occurred after death, so it does not justify arguing with predestination in this life of obligation.
This reinforces that qada and qadar are not to be used as excuses for sin.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تعريف القضاء لغة وعرفا]
(وَبِالقَضَا) أي : وبقضاء الله تعالى، وهو لغة : الحكم، وعرَّفه الماتريدية:
قال المحشي الشنواني معلقا على هذا الحديث : فهو خصوصية لآدم وليس لغيره أن يتعلق بها، على أنها مناظرة جرت بعد الموت وانقطاع التكليف ؛ فلا يلزم من صحتها صحة ما يقع من نظيرها في دار التكليف والمؤاخذة . اهـ مختصرا . وقال العلامة الأمير: «فحج آدم أي غلبه، فذلك تأديب في البرزخ، والمنع إنما هو في دار التكليف، أي: الأليق بالولد أن ينظر لجهة عذر والده.
(۱) قوله: (تحديده (تعالى) أي: تعيينه وعلمه في الأزل ، وليس المراد أنه صفة فعل، لأنه عند الماتريدية صفة ذات .
(۲) قوله : (قول بعضهم هو الحافظ ابن حجر العسقلاني في شرحه على صحيح البخاري. باب سؤال جبريل عن الإيمان والإسلام. [فتح الباري ج ۱ ص۱۱۸]
The definition of al-qada linguistically and technically. And with al-qada: i.e., and with God's decree, and it is linguistically: judgment. And the Maturidis defined it: The commentator al-Shanwani said, commenting on this hadith: 'It is a special characteristic for Adam and not for others to use as evidence, as it was a debate that took place after death and the cessation of obligation; so the validity of that debate does not entail the validity of similar debates in the abode of obligation and accountability.' End, summarized. And the scholar al-Amir said: 'Thus Adam defeated him' meaning he overcame him. So that is an instruction in the barzakh, and the prohibition is only in the abode of obligation. That is, it is more fitting for the child to consider the excuse of his father. (1) His statement: 'His (Exalted) determining' meaning His specifying and knowing in eternity, and it is not intended that it is an attribute of action, because according to the Maturidis it is an attribute of essence. (2) His statement: 'The statement of some of them' is al-Hafiz Ibn Hajar al-Asqalani in his commentary on Sahih al-Bukhari, the chapter on Jibril's question about faith and Islam. [Fath al-Bari vol. 1 p. 118]
Source pages p. 365

Commentary
This passage defines qada (divine decree). Linguistically, qada means judgment (hukm). Technically, the Maturidis are said to have defined it, but the text immediately shifts to a commentary note on the hadith of Adam and Moses, explaining that the debate was specific to Adam and occurred after death when obligations cease, so it does not justify using predestination as an argument in this life. Then a note explains that the term 'determining' (tahdid) in the definition of qadar refers to God's eternal specifying and knowledge, not an attribute of action. Finally, a reference attributes the harmonization statement to Ibn Hajar. The definition of qada itself is not fully given in the main text; rather the passage clarifies surrounding issues.

Al-Shafi'i's Argument Against the Qadariyyah Through Knowledge
Al-Shafi'i's binding argument against Qadariyya using knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical structure of al-Shafi'i's argument that affirming God's knowledge refutes the Qadariyya position on human free will.

Core Idea
God's knowledge of all events defeats claims of independent human creation.

Al-Shafi'i presents a powerful logical argument that uses the Qadariyyah's own belief in God's knowledge to force a conclusion against them. This lesson unpacks his reasoning and shows why the commentator considers it a binding proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Familiarize yourself with the flow of the argument.
2
Analyze the conditional argument
Notice how al-Shafi'i sets up a dilemma: if the Qadariyya admit God's knowledge, they must answer whether anything can happen contrary to it. Trace the two possible answers and their implications.
3
Understand the context and scope
The commentator notes that this argument is specific to the first group (those who deny predestination but affirm knowledge). Consider why the author limits the refutation here to avoid repetition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإلزام الشَّافعي إيَّاهم بقوله: «إِنْ سَلَّم القدَريَّة العِلْمَ ؛ خُصِموا ، إذ يُقال لهم: أتجوزون أن يقع في الوجود خلاف ما تضمَّنه العِلمُ ؟ فإن منعوا ؛ وَافَقُونا ، وإن أجازوا ؛ لزمهم نسبة الجهل إليه، تعالى الله عَن ذلك عُلُوًّا كبيرًا» خاص بالأولى. ومراد الناظم : الرَّدُّ عليهم فقط ؛ لئلا يتكرّر مع قوله السابق: «فَخَالِقٌ لِعَبْدِه ومَا عَمِلْ) والأدلة القطعية من الكتاب والسُّنَّة وإجماع الصحابة وغيرهم متظاهرة على إثبات قدرته
And al-Shafi'i's binding argument against them by his saying: 'If the Qadariyyah admit knowledge, they are defeated, for it is said to them: Do you deem it possible that what knowledge encompasses could occur contrary to it? If they forbid it, they agree with us, and if they permit it, then ignorance is attributed to Him—exalted is Allah above that greatly.' It is specific to the first. And the intention of the author is only the refutation of them, so that it does not repeat with his previous saying: 'For He is the Creator of His servant and what he does.' And the definitive evidences from the Book, the Sunnah, the consensus of the Companions, and others are manifest in establishing His power.
Source pages p. 367

Commentary
This passage is an exposition of al-Shafi'i's argument against the Qadariyyah (those who claim that humans create their own actions independently). The commentator states that al-Shafi'i's argument is a binding proof (ilzam) against them. The argument is: if the Qadariyyah affirm that Allah has complete and detailed knowledge of all events before they occur, then they are asked: can anything happen contrary to what Allah's knowledge encompasses? If they say no, then they agree with the Ahl al-Sunnah that Allah's knowledge is determinative and humans cannot act independently. If they say yes, then they must attribute ignorance to Allah, which is impossible. Thus, they are forced to either concede or blaspheme. The commentator then notes that this argument is specific to the first group (the one that denies predestination but affirms knowledge). The author's intention (مراد الناظم) is to refute only that first group, so that his argument does not repeat his earlier line. The definitive evidences from Quran, Sunnah, and consensus also establish Allah's power.

Key Terms
إلزام
القدرية
العلم
الجهل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument is circular because it assumes what it tries to prove.
Correction: The argument does not assume the conclusion; it uses the Qadariyya's own acceptance of God's knowledge as a premise and shows it leads to a contradiction with their view on human free will.

Checkpoint
What is the key premise that al-Shafi'i uses to refute the Qadariyyah?
A) The Qadariyyah deny God's power.
B) The Qadariyyah affirm God's knowledge.
C) The Qadariyyah affirm human free will.
D) The Qadariyyah deny the afterlife.

Key Takeaway
Al-Shafi'i's argument shows that affirming God's knowledge logically forces one to accept that all events are within His knowledge, refuting the Qadariyya claim of independent human creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In what specific area of your life do you act as if your decisions are outside God's knowledge? When you make plans, do you truly believe that nothing can occur contrary to what He knows?
?
If God's knowledge includes every detail of your future, why do you sometimes feel anxious about outcomes? Is your anxiety rooted in a hidden belief that things might not go as God knows they will?
?
Consider a time when you insisted on your own will over what seemed to be God's decree. Did that stem from a failure to internalize that God's knowledge is perfect and that His will prevails? What would change in your heart if you fully surrendered to that truth?

Quranic Proofs for Seeing Allah in the Hereafter
Sanusi cites two Quranic proofs for seeing Allah and refutes al-Jubbai.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Quranic basis for the beatific vision and the refutation of a deviant interpretation.

Core Idea
The Quran explicitly promises believers the vision of Allah in Paradise.

This lesson presents two key Quranic verses that Sunni scholars use to prove that believers will see Allah in the Hereafter. It also shows how Imam Sanusi refutes an alternative interpretation by al-Jubbai that would deny this vision. The passage is concise but foundational for the Sunni doctrine of seeing Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic text and its literal translation to grasp the author's argument.
Notice the two verses cited.
2
Identify the first proof
Look at the verse from Surah al-Qiyamah and understand how it is used as evidence for seeing Allah.
Al-Jubbai's interpretation is mentioned and refuted.
3
Examine the second proof
Consider the verse from Surah Yunus and the majority exegesis that 'al-ziyada' refers to looking at Allah's face.
4
Compare the two interpretations
Reflect on why the author calls al-Jubbai's view 'astonishing' and why the majority view is preferred.
This highlights the importance of following the consensus of the Salaf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
رؤية المؤمنين ربهم سبحانه وتعالى في الآخرة
وقد استدلَّ أهلُ السُّنَّة على جواز رؤية الله تعالى بالكتاب والسُّنَّة: أما الكتاب فآيات منها قوله: ﴿وُجُوهٌ يَوْمَيذٍ نَاضِرَةُ إِلَى رَيْهَا نَاظِرَةٌ ) [القيامة]. والجُبَّائي حمل: (ناظرة) في هذه الآية على معنى الانتظار، وجعل: (إلى) بمعنى النعمة ، وكأنه قيل : منتظرةٌ نعمة ربها ، وهو كلام عجيب.

The vision of the believers of their Lord, glorified and exalted, in the Hereafter
And Ahl al-Sunnah have adduced as proof for the permissibility of seeing Allah Almighty from the Book and the Sunnah: as for the Book, among the verses is His saying: 'Faces on that day will be radiant, looking at their Lord' [al-Qiyamah]. And al-Jubbai interpreted 'nadhira' (looking) in this verse to mean 'waiting', and made 'ila' (at/to) to mean 'favor', as if it were said: 'waiting for the favor of their Lord', and that is astonishing speech.
And among the verses is His saying: 'For those who have done good is the best and an increase' [Yunus 26]. The majority of exegetes said: 'al-husna' is Paradise, and 'the increase' is looking at His noble face.
Source pages p. 372

Commentary
This passage provides Quranic proof for the vision of Allah in the Hereafter. The author first states that Ahl al-Sunnah use both Quran and Sunnah as evidence. He then cites two key Quranic verses:
1. Surah al-Qiyamah (75:22-23): 'وجوه يومئذ ناضرة إلى ربها ناظرة' — faces are described as 'nadhira' (radiant) and 'nadhira' (looking). The author refutes al-Jubbai's interpretation that 'nadhira' means 'waiting' and 'ila' means 'favor', calling it astonishing because the plain meaning indicates sight.
2. Surah Yunus (10:26): 'للذين أحسنوا الحسنى وزيادة' — the majority of exegetes interpret 'al-husna' as Paradise and 'al-ziyada' (the increase) as looking at Allah's face. This is a standard proof for the beatific vision in Sunni theology.
The passage ends abruptly after mentioning these two verses, with 'الحُسنى هي' which seems incomplete.

Misconception: Al-Jubbai's interpretation that the word 'ناظرة' in the Quran means 'waiting' for Allah's favor, not direct vision.
Correction: The majority of scholars understand it literally as 'looking at' their Lord, as supported by the context and consensus of the Salaf.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The Quran provides explicit evidence that believers will see Allah in the Hereafter, and deviant interpretations such as al-Jubbai's are rejected by the majority of scholars.

Quranic Proofs for the Vision of Allah
Author cites three Qur'anic verses as evidence for believers seeing Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand three key Quranic verses used by Ahl al-Sunna to prove that believers will see Allah in the Hereafter, and why the Mu'tazilite reinterpretation fails.

Core Idea
Three Quranic verses prove believers will literally see Allah.

The Ahl al-Sunna rely on both the Quran and Sunna to establish the doctrine of seeing Allah in the afterlife. In this passage, the author presents three specific Quranic verses, each of which, when understood in its plain sense, indicates that the believers will actually see their Lord. He also notes and dismisses the Mu'tazilite reinterpretation of the first verse.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its English translation to get an overview of the three proofs.
Notice the author's dismissive tone toward al-Jubba'i's interpretation.
2
Analyze the first verse (al-Qiyama 22)
Focus on the verse 'وجوه يومئذ ناضرة إلى ربها ناظرة'. The author points out that al-Jubba'i reinterpreted 'ناظرة' as 'awaiting' rather than 'looking'. Consider the plain Arabic usage of 'نظر إلى' for seeing.
This is the main point of contention with the Mu'tazila.
3
Examine the second verse (Yunus 26)
The verse speaks of 'الحسنى' and 'زيادة'. The author reports the majority tafsir that 'الزيادة' is looking at Allah's face. This is a classic proof for ru'yat Allah.
4
Reflect on the third verse (al-Mutaffifin 23)
The absolute verb 'ينظرون' in context implies visual sight. Relate this to the previous verses.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد استدلَّ أهلُ السُّنَّة على جواز رؤية الله تعالى بالكتاب والسُّنَّة: أما الكتاب فآيات منها قوله: ﴿وُجُوهٌ يَوْمَئِذٍ نَاضِرَةٌ إِلَى رَبِّهَا نَاظِرَةٌ﴾ [القيامة: 22]. والجُبَّائي حمل: (ناظرة) في هذه الآية على معنى الانتظار، وجعل: (إلى) بمعنى النعمة، وكأنه قيل: منتظرة نعمة ربها، وهو كلام عجيب. ومن الآيات قوله تعالى: ﴿لِلَّذِينَ أَحْسَنُوا الْحُسْنَى وَزِيَادَةٌ﴾ [يونس: 26] قال جمهور المفسرين: الحسنى هي الجنة، والزيادة هي النظر إلى وجهه الكريم. ومنها قوله: ﴿عَلَى الْأَرَائِكِ يَنظُرُونَ﴾ [المطففين: 23].
And the Ahl al-Sunna have argued for the permissibility of seeing Allah by the Book and the Sunna. Source pages p. 372

Commentary
This passage presents the Quranic evidence for the believers' vision of Allah in the Hereafter. It begins by stating that Ahl al-Sunna use both the Quran and Hadith as proof. The author then lists three verses. The first is from Surah al-Qiyama (75:22-23): 'Faces on that day are radiant, looking at their Lord.' The word 'nāẓira' (looking) with the preposition 'ilā' clearly indicates visual sight. The author notes the Mu'tazilite interpretation of al-Jubba'i, who reinterpreted 'nāẓira' as 'awaiting' (muntaẓira) and 'ilā' as 'blessing' (ni‘ma), making the phrase mean 'awaiting the blessing of their Lord.' The author dismisses this as 'strange speech' (kalām ‘ajīb). The second verse is from Surah Yunus (10:26): 'For those who do good is the best and an increase.' The author reports the majority tafsir: 'al-husnā' is Paradise, and the 'increase' (ziyāda) is looking at Allah's noble face. This is a classic proof for ru’yat Allāh. The third verse is from Surah al-Mutaffifin (83:23): 'On couches, looking.' The verb 'yanẓurūn' (they look) is absolute, implying visual sight of something—identified by context as Allah. The passage thus provides three specific Quranic proofs, each with an explanation of how they indicate actual vision, and refutes the Mu'tazilite metaphorical interpretations.

Correction: The plain Arabic usage of 'نظر إلى' means 'to look at', and the majority of commentators understand it as literal vision. The author calls al-Jubba'i's interpretation 'strange speech'.

Key Takeaway
Three Quranic verses—al-Qiyama 22, Yunus 26, and al-Mutaffifin 23—provide clear proof that believers will see Allah in the Hereafter, and the Mu'tazilite reinterpretation of the first verse is linguistically untenable.

Hadith Proof for the Vision of Allah Like the Moon
Author cites the hadith of seeing Allah like the moon as proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the hadith 'You will see your Lord as you see the moon' serves as evidence for the vision of Allah, and how the author clarifies the simile and refutes the Mu'tazila interpretation.

Core Idea
The simile is about the seeing, not the seen—certainty, not similarity.

This lesson examines one of the primary hadith proofs for the vision of Allah in the afterlife. The author cites a widely transmitted hadith from Sahih al-Bukhari and Muslim, then explains its correct interpretation. He shows that the comparison is about the clarity and certainty of the vision, not any physical resemblance, and he dismisses the Mu'tazila's attempt to reinterpret the hadith metaphorically.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith and translation
Carefully read the Arabic text of the hadith and its literal English translation. Notice the key phrase 'سترون ربكم' (you will see your Lord).
The hadith is from Sahih al-Bukhari and Muslim.
2
Identify the author's clarifications
The author states that the simile (tashbīh) applies to the act of seeing (ru'ya), not the one seen (mar'ī). Then he gives the point of similarity (wajh al-shabah) as absence of doubt and concealment.
This refutes any notion that Allah is like the moon.
3
Examine the Mu'tazila's claim
The Mu'tazila claimed the hadith is a metaphor of ellipsis (majāz al-ḥadhf) meaning 'see the blessing of your Lord.' The author rejects it as unnecessary, because the plain meaning suffices.
The author does not provide a detailed refutation here, just a brief dismissal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الأحاديث فمنها الحديث الذي ورد في الصحيح: «إنكم سترون ربكم كما ترون القمر ليلة البدر» والتشبيه للرؤية لا للمرئي، ووجه الشبه عدم الشك والخفاء، وجعله المعتزلة من مجاز الحذف، فزعموا أنَّ التقدير: سترون نعمة ربكم، ولا داعي له.
As for the hadiths, among them is the hadith that has come in the Sahih: 'Indeed you will see your Lord as you see the moon on the night of the full moon.' And the comparison is for the seeing, not the seen, and the point of similarity is the absence of doubt and concealment. And the Mu'tazila made it a metaphor of ellipsis, claiming that the estimation is: you will see the blessing of your Lord, and there is no need for that.
Source pages p. 373

Commentary
This passage provides a hadith proof for the vision of Allah. The hadith from Sahih (Bukhari and Muslim) states: 'You will see your Lord as you see the moon on the night of the full moon.' The author clarifies two points. First, the simile (tashbīh) applies to the act of seeing (ru'ya), not to the one seen (mar'ī). The believers will see Allah with clarity and certainty, just as they see the moon without doubt. The point of similarity (wajh al-shabah) is the absence of doubt (shakk) and concealment (khafā'). Second, the Mu'tazila attempted to reinterpret this as a metaphor of ellipsis (majāz al-ḥadhf), claiming the implied meaning is 'you will see the blessing of your Lord.' The author rejects this as unnecessary (lā dā‘ī lahu), meaning the plain meaning is sufficient and there is no need to invent a metaphorical interpretation. The passage thus defends the literal understanding of the hadith as evidence for visual sight of Allah.

Key Terms
سترون ربكم
التشبيه
وجه الشبه
مجاز الحذف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The hadith comparing seeing Allah to seeing the moon implies that Allah is like the moon in form or appearance.
Correction: The author clarifies that the simile (tashbīh) applies to the act of seeing (ru'ya), not the one seen (mar'ī). The point of similarity is the clarity and certainty of the vision, not physical resemblance.

Checkpoint

A) Both are round.
B) Both emit light.
C) The absence of doubt and concealment.
D) Both appear at night.

Key Takeaway
The hadith proof for the vision of Allah is understood by focusing on the clarity of seeing, not any physical resemblance, and the Mu'tazila's metaphorical reinterpretation is unnecessary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The comparison is about clarity of seeing. How clearly do you 'see' Allah in your daily life? What habit can you establish this week to increase your awareness of His presence?
