Consensus and Proofs for the Beatific Vision
Author cites scholarly consensus and imams' statements supporting vision.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the vision of Allah in the Hereafter is established by the consensus of the Companions and reinforced by arguments from Imam Malik and Imam al-Shafi'i.

Core Idea
The veiling of disbelievers proves the vision of believers.

The author establishes the doctrine of seeing Allah in the afterlife as a matter of consensus among the Companions and later scholars. He then cites two great Imams—Malik and Shafi'i—who each use a powerful argument from contrast: if Allah veils His enemies from seeing Him, then His friends must see Him. This lesson unpacks these proofs and their Qur'anic foundation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without over-analysis. Then read the literal translation to grasp the overall argument.
2
Identify the consensus claim
Notice that the author begins by stating that the Companions did not differ on the reality of believers seeing their Lord. This consensus adds weight to the doctrine.
3
Analyse Imam Malik's argument
Malik uses the principle of contrast: if Allah veils His enemies so they do not see Him, then He manifests to His friends so they see Him. He supports this with Qur'an 83:15.
The verse reproaches disbelievers for being veiled—if believers also did not see, the reproach would be meaningless.
4
Analyse Imam al-Shafi'i's argument
Shafi'i argues that veiling with anger (sukht) indicates that some see Him with pleasure (riḍā). This is a logical inference from divine justice.
5
Reflect on the supplication
The passage ends with a du'a asking for the blessing of looking at Allah's face. This shows the practical and emotional significance of the doctrine.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد كان العلماء من أصحاب النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم لا يختلفون في وقوع رؤية المؤمنين ربهم في الآخرة ، وكذلك من بعدهم من أهل العلم ؛ قال الإمام مالك رضي الله تعالى عنه: لما حجب أعداءه فلم يروه، تجلّى لأوليائه حتى رأوه ، ولو لم يَرَ المؤمنونَ ربَّهم يومَ القيامة لم يُعيَّرِ الكافرونَ بالحجاب ، قال تعالى: ﴿كَلَّا إِنَّهُمْ عَن رَّبِّهِمْ يَوْمَئِذٍ لَمَحْجُوبُونَ﴾ [المطففين: 15] وقال الإمام الشافعي: لما حجب قوما بالسخط دلَّ على أنَّ قوما يرونه بالرضا. اللهم متعنا بالنظر إلى وجهك الكريم، في جنات النعيم ، واجعلنا من الذين أنعمت عليهم برضاك ، ولا تجعلنا من المحجوبين بسخطك يا أكرم الأكرمين ، اللهم آمين.
And the scholars from the companions of the Prophet (peace be upon him) did not differ about the occurrence of the believers seeing their Lord in the Hereafter, and likewise those after them from the people of knowledge. Imam Malik (may Allah be pleased with him) said: When He veiled His enemies and they did not see Him, He manifested to His friends until they saw Him. And if the believers did not see their Lord on the Day of Resurrection, the disbelievers would not be reproached with the veil. Allah said: 'Nay, indeed they are on that day veiled from their Lord' (al-Mutaffifin: 15). And Imam al-Shafi'i said: When He veiled a people with anger, it indicates that a people see Him with pleasure. O Allah, grant us the joy of looking at Your noble face in the gardens of bliss, and make us among those upon whom You have bestowed Your pleasure, and do not make us among the veiled with Your anger, O Most Generous of the generous, O Allah, Amen.
Source pages p. 373

Commentary
This passage establishes the consensus of the Prophet's companions and later scholars on the reality of believers seeing Allah in the afterlife. The author states that the Companions did not differ on this matter, and the same is true for the generation after them. Two prominent imams are quoted. First, Imam Malik argues using the principle of contrast: if Allah veils His enemies so they do not see Him, then His friends must see Him. He cites the Qur'anic verse from Surah al-Mutaffifin (83:15) where the disbelievers are described as 'veiled from their Lord'—a reproach that would be meaningless if believers also did not see Him. Second, Imam al-Shafi'i makes a similar argument: the fact that Allah veiled some people with anger (sukht) indicates that others see Him with pleasure (riḍā). The passage ends with a supplication (du'a) asking for the blessing of looking at Allah's face and to be among those who are not veiled. This serves as a practical and emotional conclusion to the doctrinal proofs.

Key Terms
الحجاب
المحجوبون
التجلي
السخط
الرضا

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The veiling of disbelievers in the afterlife is only a spiritual veil, not a visual one.
Correction: The Imams use the verse and logical inference to argue that the veil is literal and visual, since the reproach is specifically about not seeing Allah.

Checkpoint

A) That the Prophet saw Allah in this world.

C) That the moon simile proves it.
D) That the Qur'an says 'faces looking at their Lord'.

Key Takeaway
The consensus of the Companions and the arguments of Imam Malik and Imam al-Shafi'i solidly establish that the believers will see Allah in the Hereafter.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The Rational Doubt of Opposition and Its Refutation
The Mu'tazila's strongest rational doubt against ru'ya and Ash'ari response

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila's strongest rational objection to the vision of Allah and the Ash'ari response that ru'ya is a direct perception free from spatial conditions.

Core Idea
Seeing Allah does not require spatial opposition; vision is direct perception created by Allah.

The Mu'tazila raised a powerful rational objection against the possibility of seeing Allah, arguing that vision necessarily entails spatial relationships incompatible with divine transcendence. This passage presents that doubt—called shubhat al-muqabala—and the classic Ash'ari refutation. Understanding this debate clarifies a key difference between rationalist and traditionalist approaches to divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
First read the Arabic passage and its English translation to get an overview of the argument and response.
Notice the structure: first the doubt, then the word 'laken' introducing the refutation.
2
Identify the four premises of the doubt
Break down the Mu'tazila's argument: if Allah were seen, He would be opposite the seer; then He would be in direction/space; then He would be a substance or accident; then He would be either limitless or divisible.
Each premise leads to an impossibility for the transcendent Creator.
3
Understand the Ash'ari response
Focus on the phrase 'bila kayf'—vision does not require conditions like opposition, direction, or distance. It is a direct perception Allah creates.
The refutation redefines vision as an created act of perception, not a physical relationship.
4
Reflect on the concept of 'bila kayf'
Consider why Ash'aris insist on stripping ru'ya of all modalities. This protects divine transcendence while affirming the eschatological vision.
This is an example of affirming an attribute without specifying 'how'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قسم الإلهيات
بشبه عقلية، أقواها: شبهة المقابلة، وتقريرها: أنه تعالى لو كان مرئيًّا لكان مقابلًا للرائي بالضرورة؛ فيكون في جهة وحيز، وهو محال، ولكان إما جوهرًا أو عرضًا؛ لأن المتحيز بالاستقلال جوهر، أو بالتبعية عرض، ولكان المرئي إما كله، فيكون محدودًا متناهيًا محصورًا، وإما بعضه فيكون متبعضًا متجزئًا إلى غير ذلك، وهذه الشبهة أشار إلى جوابها بقوله: (لكن) النظر الحاصل بحاسة البصر للرائين (بلا كيف) أي: تكيف للمرئي من مقابلة، وجهة، ومسافة مخصوصة، وإحاطة به، بل يجب تجرده عنه؛ فإن الرؤية نوع من الإدراك يخلقه الله تعالى متى شاء، ولأي شيء شاء؛ فالمراد بالمخالفة في الكيف وجوب خلو رؤية الواجب تعالى عن الشرائط والكيفيات المعتبرة في رؤية الأجسام والأعراض.
Section on Divine Matters. By rational doubts, the strongest of which is the doubt of opposition, and its formulation: that if the Exalted were seen, he would necessarily be opposite the seer; thus he would be in a direction and space, which is impossible, and he would be either a substance or an accident; because that which occupies space independently is a substance, or dependently is an accident, and the seen would be either all of him, then he would be limited, finite, enclosed, or some of him, then he would be divided into parts, and so on. This doubt is indicated by his saying: (But) the sight obtained by the sense of sight for the seers (without how) i.e., any qualification of the seen from opposition, direction, specific distance, and encompassing it, rather it must be free from that; for vision is a type of perception that Allah creates when He wills and for whatever He wills; so the meaning of 'difference in how' is the necessity of the vision of the Exalted being free from the conditions and modalities considered in the vision of bodies and accidents.
Source pages p. 375

Commentary
This passage presents the strongest rational doubt used by the Mu'tazila to deny the possibility of seeing Allah (ru'ya). The doubt is called shubhat al-muqabala (the doubt of opposition). It argues that if Allah were seen, He would have to be opposite the seer, thus in a direction and space, which is impossible for the transcendent Creator. Furthermore, He would have to be either a substance or an accident, and either seen entirely (implying finitude) or partially (implying divisibility). The author then gives the Ash'ari response, indicated by the word 'laken' (but). The response states that vision obtained by the sense of sight does not require the seen object to have a specific how (kayf) of opposition, direction, distance, or encompassing. Rather, vision is a type of perception that Allah creates directly, free from such conditions. The author concludes that the difference lies in the necessity of stripping the vision of the Necessary Being from the conditions and modalities that apply to the vision of bodies and accidents.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Assuming that all vision requires spatial proximity, opposition, and physical conditions.
Correction: The Ash'ari response clarifies that vision can be a direct perception created by Allah without any spatial or physical modalities.

Checkpoint

A) Seeing Allah requires spatial opposition, which is impossible for Him.
B) Vision is impossible because Allah is not a physical body.
C) Allah cannot be seen because He is transcendent in essence.
D) Vision requires direction and distance, which are impossible for Allah.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari refutation of the doubt of opposition affirms that ru'ya is a direct perception created by Allah, requiring no spatial conditions.

The Mu'tazila's Scriptural Doubt from Quran 6:103
Mu'tazila's scriptural doubt using Quran 6:103

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila's strongest scriptural argument against the vision of Allah based on the verse 'La tudrikuhu al-absar' and their reasoning from the context of praise.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila argue that if vision were possible, it would be a deficiency for Allah.

The Mu'tazila relied on scriptural evidences to deny the vision of Allah, the strongest being the verse 'No vision can grasp Him' from Surah al-An'am. In this lesson, we will examine how they interpret this verse and why they consider vision a deficiency.

Guided Path
1
Read the verse and translation
Start by reading the Arabic verse and its English translation. Notice the word 'tudrikuhu' (comprehend).
The verse is Quran 6:103: 'لَا تُدْرِكُهُ الْأَبْصَارُ وَهُوَ يُدْرِكُ الْأَبْصَارَ'.
2
Understand the Mu'tazila's reasoning
The Mu'tazila argue that negating idrak (comprehension) is in a context of praise, so its opposite (idrak) would be a deficiency, which is impossible for Allah.
This argument aims to show that vision is not even possible (jawaz) for Allah.
3
Reflect on the strength of this doubt
Consider why this is considered the strongest scriptural doubt. The praise context gives it interpretive weight.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شبه المعتزلة في نفي الرؤية وردها
وتمسكوا أيضا بشبه سمعية، أقواها قوله تعالى: وَلَا تُدْرِكُهُ الْأَبْصَرُ وَهُوَ يُدْرِكُ الْأَبْصَرَ . وتقرير التمسك به الذي تعرض لجوابه: أن نفي إدراكه تعالى بالبصر وارد مورد التمدح، مدرج في أثناء المدح؛ فيكون نقيضه - وهو الإدراك بالبصر - نقصا، وهو على الله تعالى محال، وهذا الوجه يدل على نفي الجواز، وأشار إلى جواب هذه بقوله: (ولا انحصار) يعني أننا نقول: إنه تعالى يرى، بمعنى: أنه ينكشف للأبصار انكشافا تاما عند الرائي بلا إحاطة ولا انحصار له عنده؛ لاستحالة الحدود والنهايات والوقوف على حقيقته، كما هو محل النفي في الآية الشريفة. وبيانه أنا لا نسلم أن الإدراك بالبصر في الآية الكريمة هو مطلق الرؤية، بل هو رؤية مخصوصة، وهي التي تكون على وجه الإحاطة بجوانب المرئي، فالإدراك المنفي في الآية أخص من الرؤية ملزوم لها، بمنزلة الإحاطة من العلم؛ فلا يلزم من نفي الإدراك على هذا نفي الرؤية.
The doubt of the Mu'tazila in negating vision and its refutation. They also held to scriptural doubts, the strongest of which is His saying: 'And eyes comprehend Him not, and He comprehends all vision.' And the formulation of their adherence to it, which he addressed in his response: That the negation of comprehension of the Exalted by sight is in the context of praise, inserted within praise; so its opposite—which is comprehension by sight—would be a deficiency, and that is impossible for Allah, and this aspect indicates the negation of permissibility. And he indicated the answer to this by his saying: (And no encompassing) meaning that we say: The Exalted is seen, in the sense that He is disclosed to eyes completely to the viewer without encompassing or restriction with Him; due to the impossibility of limits, ends, and stopping at His reality, as is the import of the negation in the noble verse. And its explanation is that we do not accept that comprehension by sight in the noble verse is absolute vision, rather it is a specific vision, which is that which involves encompassing the aspects of the seen, so the comprehension negated in the verse is more specific than vision, entailing it, analogous to encompassing in relation to knowledge; so negating this comprehension does not entail negating vision.
Source pages p. 375

Commentary
This passage presents the strongest scriptural doubt used by the Mu'tazila against the vision of Allah, which is the verse 'La tudrikuhu al-absar' (Surah al-An'am 103). The Mu'tazila argue that the verse is in the context of praise (tamaduh), so negating 'idrak' implies that vision would be a deficiency (naqs), which is impossible for Allah. The author then gives the Ash'ari response, indicated by the phrase 'wa la inksar' (and no encompassing). The response explains that Allah is seen in the sense of being disclosed completely to the viewer, but without encompassing or restriction, because He has no boundaries. The negation in the verse is interpreted as negating 'idrak' which is a specific type of vision that involves encompassing the object from all sides. Since 'idrak' is more specific than 'ru'ya', negating 'idrak' does not negate 'ru'ya'. This is analogous to the relationship between encompassing (ihata) and knowledge ('ilm): negating encompassing does not negate knowledge.

Key Terms
لا تدركه الأبصار
الإدراك
الرؤية
المدح
النقص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse 'La tudrikuhu' proves that Allah cannot be seen at all.
Correction: The verse negates 'idrak', which is a specific type of seeing, not all vision.

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazila's argument, why does the negation of idrak in Quran 6:103 imply that vision is impossible for Allah?
A) Because the verse explicitly says Allah cannot be seen.
B) Because the negation is in a context of praise, making its opposite a deficiency.
C) Because idrak is the only type of vision mentioned in the Quran.
D) Because the verse also negates all human perception.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila's strongest scriptural doubt is based on interpreting the negation of idrak in a praise context as implying vision would be a deficiency.

What could you do this week to humbly submit to the fact that He is beyond your grasp?
?
This argument shows that even a single verse can have profound implications for our understanding of Allah. The Ash'ari Response: Idrak is Not Ru'ya
Ash'ari refutation distinguishing idrak from ru'ya

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari distinction between idrak and ru'ya, and how it refutes the Mu'tazila's doubt by showing that negating idrak does not negate vision.

Core Idea
Negating idrak does not negate vision; idrak is a specific kind of seeing involving encompassing.

The Ash'ari response to the Mu'tazila's doubt hinges on the meaning of 'idrak'. While the Mu'tazila equate idrak with vision, the Ash'aris argue that idrak is a narrower concept. In this lesson, we will explore the response as given by the author, including the analogy with knowledge and encompassing.

Guided Path
1
Read the Ash'ari response
Read the Arabic passage starting from 'وأشار إلى جواب' and its translation. Notice the key phrase 'ولا انحصار' (and no encompassing).
2
Understand the distinction between idrak and ru'ya
The author explains that idrak is a specific type of vision that involves encompassing (ihata) all sides of the object. Ru'ya is broader, meaning disclosure without encompassing.
Since Allah has no boundaries, encompassing is impossible, but vision without encompassing is possible.
3
Appreciate the analogy
The analogy with ihata (encompassing) and 'ilm (knowledge) is given: just as negating encompassing does not negate knowledge, negating idrak does not negate vision.
This analogy strengthens the response.

Distinguishing Idrak from Ru'ya: Refuting the Mu'tazila
Refutation of Mu'tazila on ru'ya by distinguishing idrak from ru'ya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the Mu'tazila argument by distinguishing idrak (encompassing perception) from ru'ya (vision), showing that the verse only negates the former.

Core Idea
Idrak is a specific kind of vision; negating it does not negate all vision.

The Mu'tazila cite the verse 'No vision can grasp Him' (6:103) to deny that believers can see Allah in the afterlife. The author counters by clarifying that the verse negates idrak—a perception that encompasses the object—not ru'ya (vision) itself. This distinction is crucial for understanding the Sunni position on the beatific vision.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow. Then read the literal translation. Pay attention to the key terms: idrak, ru'ya, ihata.
Notice how the author starts by presenting the Mu'tazila doubt before responding.
2
Identify the Mu'tazila doubt
Find where the author states the Mu'tazila argument: because negation of idrak is praise, its opposite (idrak) would be a deficiency, hence ru'ya is impossible.
3
Understand the author's response
The author distinguishes idrak from ru'ya. Idrak is a specific vision that involves encompassing (ihata). Allah is seen without encompassing.
The key phrase is 'wa la inhisar' (and no encompassing).
4
Analyze the logical relationship
Idrak is more specific (akhas) than ru'ya and implied by it (malzum laha), like ihata is to knowledge. Negating the specific does not negate the general.
This is the core logical move.
5
Apply the distinction to the verse
Understand why the verse's negation of idrak does not negate ru'ya, and why that does not imply deficiency.
The verse praises Allah for not being encompassed, not for being invisible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شبه المعتزلة في نفي الرؤية وردها المدح؛ فيكون نقيضه - وهو الإدراك بالبصر - نقصا، وهو على الله تعالى محال، وهذا الوجه يدل على نفي الجواز، وأشار إلى جواب هذه بقوله: (وَلَا انْحِصَارِ) يعني أننا نقول: إنه تعالى يرى، بمعنى: أنه ينكشف للأبصار انكشافا تاما عند الرائي بلا إحاطة ولا انحصار له عنده؛ لاستحالة الحدود والنهايات والوقوف على حقيقته، كما هو مَحْمِل النفي في الآية الشريفة. وبيانه أنا لا نسلم أن الإدراك بالبصر في الآية الكريمة هو مطلق الرؤية، بل هو رؤية مخصوصة، وهي التي تكون على وجه الإحاطة بجوانب المرئي، فالإدراك المنفي في الآية أخص من الرؤية ملزوم لها، بمنزلة الإحاطة من العلم؛ فلا يلزم من نفي الإدراك على هذا نفي الرؤية، ولا من كون نفيه مدحا كون الرؤية نقصا.
The doubt of the Mu'tazila in negating vision and its refutation: Praise; so its opposite—which is perception by sight—is a deficiency, and it is impossible for Allah Almighty. This aspect indicates the negation of permissibility, and he pointed to the answer to this by his saying: 'And no encompassing,' meaning we say: Indeed, He Almighty is seen, in the sense that He becomes fully disclosed to sight at the seer without encompassing or limitation with him; due to the impossibility of boundaries, endpoints, and grasping His essence, as is the import of the negation in the noble verse. And its explanation: We do not accept that perception by sight in the noble verse is absolute vision; rather, it is a specific vision, which is that which involves encompassing the sides of the visible object. Thus, perception negated in the verse is more specific than vision and is implied by it, like encompassing in relation to knowledge. So it does not follow from the negation of perception on this basis the negation of vision, nor from the fact that its negation is praise does it follow that vision is a deficiency.
Source pages p. 376

Commentary
This passage refutes the Mu'tazila argument against the possibility of seeing Allah (ru'ya). The author begins by stating the Mu'tazila doubt: they argue that because Allah praises Himself for not being perceived by sight (idrak) in the verse 'No vision can grasp Him' (6:103), and since being perceptible would be a deficiency, it follows that ru'ya is impossible. The author then presents the response, signaled by the phrase 'wa la inhisar' (and no encompassing). The key distinction is that idrak (perception) is not synonymous with ru'ya (vision); rather, idrak is a specific type of vision that involves encompassing (ihata) the object. Allah's disclosure to sight (inkishaf) is without encompassing. Therefore, the negation in the verse is of idrak, not of ru'ya. The author explains that idrak is more specific (akhas) than ru'ya and is implied by it (malzum laha)—just as ihata (encompassing) is related to knowledge but not identical to it. Hence, negating idrak does not negate ru'ya, nor does the praise for negating idrak imply that ru'ya is a deficiency. The argument follows a logical sequence: define terms, clarify the verse's intended meaning, and then refute the inference.

Key Terms
رؤية (ru'ya)
إدراك (idrak)
إحاطة (ihata)
انكشاف (inkishaf)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse 'No vision can grasp Him' means that Allah cannot be seen at all.
Correction: The verse only negates idrak (encompassing perception), not ru'ya (vision). Allah can be seen without being encompassed.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the relationship between idrak and ru'ya?
A) They are synonymous and both negated in the verse.
B) Idrak is a specific type of ru'ya that involves encompassing, so negating idrak does not negate ru'ya.
C) Idrak is more general than ru'ya, so negating idrak negates ru'ya.
D) Ru'ya is impossible because idrak is impossible.

Key Takeaway
The distinction between idrak (encompassing perception) and ru'ya (vision) is essential to understand the Sunni belief that Allah will be seen in the afterlife without being encompassed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever find yourself limiting your concept of Allah to what you can fully grasp, and if so, how does this passage challenge you to trust in a vision of Him that is beyond full comprehension?
?
The Mu'tazila concluded that if Allah cannot be encompassed by sight, then He cannot be seen at all. Reflect on an area of your faith where you might have made a similar logical leap, assuming that because you cannot fully understand something about Allah, it must not be true. What would it mean to humbly accept that your knowledge is limited and trust what Allah has promised?
?

Are you content to seek closeness to Allah even though you will never fully comprehend His essence, or do you secretly desire to have complete mastery over Him in your understanding?

Believers See Allah and Disbelievers Do Not
Author explains that only believers see Allah, disbelievers are veiled.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that only believers will see Allah in the afterlife, while disbelievers will be veiled from Him, and the scriptural evidence for this.

Core Idea
Only believers see Allah; disbelievers are forever veiled.

This passage clarifies a key belief about the afterlife: the beatific vision (ru'ya) is exclusive to the believers. The author explains the meaning of 'disclosure' and cites a Quranic verse to support that disbelievers will be veiled. A differing opinion is also noted.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get its rhythm, then read the English translation. Pay attention to the terms 'believers' and 'disbelievers'.
The passage is a commentary on a line of poetry.
2
Understand the condition for seeing Allah
The author specifies that only believers—those who die with true faith—will see Allah. Disbelievers and hypocrites are excluded.
The condition is being judged as a believer at death.
3
Note the proof and the alternative opinion
The author cites Quran 83:15 as proof that disbelievers are veiled. He also mentions a view that they see then are veiled, causing regret.
Imam al-Nawawi is mentioned regarding the disagreement.
4
Reflect on the implications
Think about why this distinction matters: it underscores the honor of believers and the loss of disbelievers in the afterlife.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعلق بقوله: «أَنْ يُنْظَرَ» (لِلمُؤمِنِينَ) لتضمنه معنى الانكشاف، أي: انكشافه تعالى بحاسة البصر انكشافا تاما، لكل فرد فرد ممن مات محكوما له باتصافه بالإيمان والتصديق الشرعي؛ سواء كلف به بالفعل أو كان صالحًا للتكليف به؛ فيخرج به الكفار والمنافقون، فلا يرونه تعالى; لقوله تعالى: ﴿كَلَّا إِنَّهُمْ عَن رَّبِّهِمْ يَوْمَذٍ لَمَحْجُوبُونَ ﴾ [المطففين: 15]. ولأنهم ليسوا من أهل الإكرام والتشريف، وقيل: إنهم يرونه ثم يحجبون عنه، فتكون الحجبة حسرة عليهم، وجعل النووي محل الخلاف
And he commented on his saying: 'that they look' to the believers because it implies the meaning of disclosure, i.e., His disclosure, may He be exalted, by the sense of sight, a complete disclosure, for each individual who died judged to possess faith and legal affirmation—whether actually obligated or fit for obligation. Thus, the disbelievers and hypocrites are excluded, so they will not see Him, may He be exalted; due to His saying, may He be exalted: 'No! Indeed, from their Lord that Day they will be veiled' [Al-Mutaffifin: 15]. And because they are not among the people of honor and nobility. And it is said that they see Him and then are veiled from Him, so the veiling becomes a regret for them. And Al-Nawawi considered the place of disagreement.
Source pages p. 377

Commentary
This passage comments on the line of poetry: 'For the believers, since this is attached to the permissible, and for the chosen, it is established in the world.' The author explains that the phrase 'that they look' (an yunzara) is attached to 'the believers' because it implies the meaning of disclosure (inkishaf). This disclosure is a complete seeing with the sense of sight. It applies to every individual who dies with the legal status of having faith and true belief, whether they were actually obligated to fulfil religious duties or were merely fit for obligation (like children). Consequently, disbelievers and hypocrites are excluded; they will not see Allah. The proof is the Qur'anic verse: 'No! Indeed, from their Lord that Day they will be veiled.' Additionally, because they are not worthy of honor and nobility. The author mentions a differing opinion that disbelievers will see Allah only to be veiled later, causing them regret. The location of the disagreement is attributed to Imam Al-Nawawi. Thus, the passage clarifies the scope of the beatific vision: it is solely for the righteous believers, not for the wicked.

Key Terms
مؤمنون (mu'minun)
كفار (kuffar)
حجب (hijab)
انكشاف (inkishaf)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All humans will see Allah in the afterlife.
Correction: Only believers will see Allah; disbelievers will be veiled from Him, as per Quran 83:15.

Checkpoint
According to the author, who will see Allah on the Day of Judgment?
A) All human beings, believers and disbelievers.
B) Only believers, and disbelievers are veiled from Him.
C) Only the prophets and angels.
D) Only those who were legally obligated in this world.

Key Takeaway
Only believers will see Allah on the Day of Judgment; disbelievers will be veiled from Him.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believe that you will see Allah as a reward for your faith, what changes do you need to make today to ensure you are among those who are not veiled? Are you living with the hope of that vision or are you distracted by this world?
?
The disbelievers are described as 'not among the people of honor and nobility' and will be veiled. In what ways might your current actions or attitudes reflect a disregard for the honor of faith? What would it take for you to truly value the blessing of seeing Allah?

Believers See Allah, Disbelievers Are Veiled
Commentary on the line defining the vision of Allah for believers and excluding disbelievers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the ruling that believers see Allah with complete disclosure and that disbelievers and hypocrites are veiled from seeing Him, based on the Quranic verse and scholarly discussion.

Core Idea
The vision of Allah is a complete disclosure for believers; disbelievers are veiled.

This lesson examines the commentary on the line from Jawharat al-Tawhid concerning the vision of Allah. The author clarifies that the phrase 'that He be seen' implies a complete disclosure through the sense of sight, and that this vision is specifically for believers. Disbelievers and hypocrites are excluded, as evidenced by the Quranic verse 'No, indeed, from their Lord that Day they will be veiled.' There is also a debated opinion that hypocrites may see Him briefly before being veiled as a source of regret.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to understand the surface meaning.
2
Identify the two groups and evidence
Notice that the passage distinguishes between believers (who see) and disbelievers/hypocrites (who do not). Find the Quranic verse cited as proof.
The verse is al-Mutaffifin 83:15.
3
Consider the debated opinion
The passage mentions that some scholars say hypocrites will see Allah then be veiled as regret. Reflect on why this adds nuance.

56 - To the believers, since it is linked to a possible thing, and for the chosen one it is established in the world. So by this the disbelievers and hypocrites are excluded, so they do not see Him, exalted be He, because of His saying: 'No, indeed, from their Lord that Day they will be veiled.' [al-Mutaffifin 83:15]. And because they are not among the people of honor and nobility. And it is said that they will see Him and then be veiled from Him, so the veiling becomes regret for them. And al-Nawawi considered the place of disagreement. (1) His saying: 'complete' i.e., not in the manner of suspicion, doubt, or illusion, by al-Shanwani. (2) With this restriction, children and the like enter. (3) His saying: 'so they do not see Him' i.e., in the standing-place, as the commentator mentioned. As for Paradise, there is no vision for them by consensus because they do not enter Paradise. (4) His saying: 'they see Him' i.e., only in the standing-place, and it is not meant that they see Him in Paradise because they do not enter it, by al-Shanwani. (5) The imam, the proof, the righteous saint, Yahya ibn Sharaf ibn Murri ibn Hasan ibn Husayn ibn Hizam ibn Muhammad ibn Jumu'a al-Nawawi, ... (biography) ... It is not hidden = 376. The scriptural evidence of Ahl al-Sunna on the permissibility of seeing Allah regarding the hypocrite. As for the disbeliever other than him, he does not see Him by agreement, just as other non-rational animals do not see Him. And angels enter, and believing jinn and previous nations, and children, and the feeble-minded, and the insane who reached puberty while insane and died upon it, and those who are characterized with monotheism from the people of the interval because it is sound faith, since it is in the ruling of what the Messenger brought. They see Him in Paradise. The sentence, based on the fact that men other than this nation see Him in, and it is a place of vision without disagreement. As for seeing Him in the squares of the Resurrection, there is disagreement about its occurrence for the believers therein, and the correct view is that it occurs.
Source pages p. 377

Commentary
This passage is an explanatory commentary on a line of poetry from the matn of 'Jawharat al-Tawhid' concerning the vision of Allah (ru'ya). The author begins by glossing the line 'For the believers, since it is linked to a possible thing' and explains that the phrase 'that He be seen' (an yunzar) implies disclosure (inkishaf) through the sense of sight. The vision is complete, not based on doubt or illusion. It is for every believing individual who dies in a state of faith, whether obligated or not (including children). This excludes disbelievers and hypocrites, who will not see Allah based on the verse 'Kalla innahum 'an rabbihim yawma'idhin lamahjubun' (83:15). Some say hypocrites will see Him but then be veiled as a source of regret. Imam al-Nawawi is noted as placing the disagreement on this point. The passage then lists categories of beings who will not see Allah: disbelievers, animals, angels (with a disputed opinion), believing jinn, previous nations, children, feeble-minded, insane, and the people of the interval. The place of vision is Paradise, with disagreement about the resurrection squares, but the correct view is that believers will see Him there as well.

الانكشاف
المؤمنون
الكفار
المنافقون
الحجاب

Key Takeaway

Extended Classification: Who Sees Allah and Who Does Not
Classification of beings regarding the vision of Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the detailed classification of beings who will see Allah and those who will not, including animals, angels, jinn, children, and others.

Core Idea
Not all beings see Allah; only believers among humans, jinn, angels, and certain others see Him.

After establishing the basic rule that believers see and disbelievers do not, the commentator provides a more detailed classification. He states that disbelievers other than hypocrites do not see by agreement, and neither do non-rational animals. Then he lists categories that do see: angels, believing jinn, previous nations, children, the feeble-minded, the insane, and monotheists from the people of the interval. The lesson also clarifies the place of vision (Paradise) and discusses the disagreement about seeing in the resurrection squares.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully. Then read the translation to grasp the classification.
2
List the groups
Create two lists: those who do not see (disbelievers, animals) and those who do see (angels, jinn, etc.).
Notice that children, insane, and people of the interval are included among those who see.
3
Understand the place of vision
The passage mentions that the vision is in Paradise and that there is disagreement about seeing in the resurrection squares. The correct view is that it occurs for believers.

Scriptural Evidence for Seeing Allah
Introduction to sam'i evidence for ru'ya

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Ahl al-Sunna rely exclusively on scriptural evidence (Qur'an, Sunna, consensus) to prove the possibility of seeing Allah, and how the verse about Musa links it to a rational possibility.

Core Idea
Scriptural evidence, not reason, is the foundation for proving the vision of Allah.

This passage introduces the core methodology of Ahl al-Sunna in proving the beatific vision (ru'ya). It emphasizes that reason alone is insufficient; the proof comes from the Book, Sunna, and consensus. The author then cites a key Qur'anic example: when Musa asked to see Allah, Allah linked the vision to the settling of the mountain—an intellectually possible event—thereby establishing the possibility of seeing Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal English translation to get the gist.
The passage is an introduction, so it sets the stage for the evidence.
2
Identify the three sources of sam'i evidence
Notice that the author specifies the scriptural evidence consists of the Book, Sunna, and consensus. These are the primary sources for Ahl al-Sunna.
This is a foundational principle in Sunni theology.
3
Focus on the Qur'anic argument
The passage uses the verse about Musa to argue that Allah suspended the possibility of vision on something rationally possible (the mountain's stability). This shows ru'ya is possible, not impossible.
The argument is based on the principle that if the condition is possible, the conditioned is also possible.
4
Reflect on the role of reason
Consider why this passage emphasizes that scriptural evidence is the relied-upon foundation. Reason only establishes the possibility, but scripture confirms it.
This guards against the Mu'tazila who argued that ru'ya is rationally impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أدلَّةُ أهلِ السّنّة السمعيَّة على جواز الرُّؤية

Commentary
This passage is a sub-section heading and introduction to the scriptural (sam'i) evidence for the permissibility of seeing Allah (ru'ya). The author states that the criterion for proving ru'ya among Ahl al-Sunna is exclusively the sam'i evidence, which consists of the Qur'an, the Sunna, and consensus (ijma'). He notes from the Qur'an many verses, pointing to the line of poetry 'since it is linked to a possible thing' which indicates that Allah suspended the vision on something intellectually possible: the stability of the mountain when Moses asked to see Him. The argument is that if the mountain's settling is possible, then seeing Allah is possible. The passage ends mid-sentence, likely continuing with more evidence.

Scriptural Evidence for the Permissibility of Seeing Allah
Al-Laqqani states the reliance on scriptural evidence for ru'ya and cites the Quranic proof from the verse of Musa.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ahl al-Sunnah rely on scriptural evidence for ru'ya and how the Quranic story of Musa demonstrates its permissibility.

Core Idea
The evidence for seeing Allah is scriptural, not rational — and a conditional verse proves it possible.

This passage begins a discussion on the proofs for the permissibility of seeing Allah (ru'ya). Al-Laqqani immediately clarifies the methodology of Ahl al-Sunnah: they do not base this doctrine on rational argument alone but on transmitted sources. He then lists the three scriptural sources and unpacks a key Quranic proof from the story of Prophet Musa, showing that Allah conditioned ru'ya on an intellectually possible event, thereby affirming its possibility.

Guided Path
1
Identify the primary source of evidence
Read the first sentence and note that Ahl al-Sunnah rely exclusively on scriptural proof (dalil sam'i) for ru'ya. Ask yourself why reason alone is not enough.
The passage explicitly says 'إنما هو الدليل السمعي' – it is only scriptural proof.
2
List the three scriptural sources
Find the three sources mentioned: the Book (Quran), the Sunna, and Consensus (Ijma). Understand that these are the standard categories of transmitted evidence.
3
Examine the Quranic example
Read the explanation of the verse 'إذ بجائز علقت'. See how Allah's conditioning of ru'ya on the mountain's stability shows that ru'ya is intellectually possible, because conditioning it on an impossible thing would be invalid.
This logical point is crucial: the very act of conditioning implies possibility.

Source Text

أدلة أهل السنة السمعية على جواز الرؤية. والمعول عليه في إثبات الرؤية عند أهل السنة إنما هو الدليل السمعي: وذلك الكتاب والسنة والإجماع. أما الكتاب فآيات كثيرة منها ما أشار إليه بقوله: (إذ بجائز علقت) أي: حكمنا بجواز الرؤية وإمكانها عقلا؛ لأن الله تعالى علقها بوجود أمر جائز عقلا، وهو استقرار الجبل حين سأله موسى.

Source pages p. 378

Commentary
This passage opens with a heading announcing the topic: the scriptural proofs of Ahl al-Sunnah for the permissibility of seeing Allah (ru'ya). The author immediately clarifies that the foundation for establishing ru'ya according to Ahl al-Sunnah is exclusively the scriptural (sam‘i) proof, not rational proof alone. He then lists the three sources: the Quran, the Sunna, and consensus (ijma‘). Proceeding to the Quranic evidence, he points to a particular verse—the story of Musa—by quoting the phrase 'idh bi-ja’izin ‘ulliqat', which means 'when it is conditioned upon something possible.' The explanation that follows: we have judged ru'ya to be permissible and intellectually possible because Allah made it conditional upon an intellectually possible matter, namely the mountain's stability when Musa asked to see Him. This shows that ru'ya is possible because if it were impossible, it could not be validly conditioned upon a possible event.

The Logical Inference from the Verse of Musa
Al-Laqqani details the syllogistic and exceptional proofs for ru'ya derived from the Quranic verse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Laqqani derives the possibility of ru'ya from the Quranic verse through syllogistic and exceptional proofs.

Core Idea
The verse of Musa proves that seeing Allah is possible through logical necessity.

This lesson explores the detailed logical inference from the famous verse where Musa asks to see Allah. Al-Laqqani unpacks the verse's implicit syllogism and shows how the conditional statement establishes that ru'ya is possible. He also provides an additional proof based on the impossibility of Allah's news being false and the infallibility of prophets.

Guided Path
1
Read the verse and its translation
Start by reading the Quranic verse (7:143) and its translation. This is the foundation of the argument.
The verse contains a conditional statement that Al-Laqqani will analyze.
2
Understand the syllogistic argument
Identify the minor premise (conditioning ru'ya on the mountain's stability) and the omitted major premise (everything conditioned on the possible is possible). See how they lead to the conclusion that ru'ya is possible.
The major premise is known to the audience, hence omitted.
3
Examine the conditional logic
Grasp the meaning of ta'liq (conditioning) and why it necessitates that ru'ya cannot be impossible.
If ru'ya were impossible, Allah's conditional statement would be false.
4
Analyze the exceptional proof
Consider the two points: (1) If ru'ya were impossible, Allah's news would be false (khalaf), which is impossible. (2) Musa would not have asked for something impossible, as prophets know Allah's attributes.
This reinforces the conclusion from another angle.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Synthesize the arguments: ru'ya is possible in the afterlife, as affirmed by Ahl al-Sunnah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ورب أرني أنظر إليك قال لن تراني ولكن انظر إلى الجبل فإن استقر مكانه فسوف تراني [الأعراف: ١٤٣]. وتقرير الدلالة منه: أنه إشارة إلى قياس حذفت كبراه للعلم بها، ترتيبه: الله تعالى علق رؤية ذاته المقدسة على استقرار الجبل حال تجليه تعالى له، وهو أمر ممكن في نفسه ضرورة، وكل ما علق على الممكن لا يكون إلا ممكنا لأن معنى التعليق: الإخبار بأن المعلق يقع على تقدير وقوع المعلق عليه، والمحال لا يقع على شيء من التقادير؛ فلو لم تكن الرؤية ممكنة لزم الخلف في خبره تعالى، وهو محال، ولو كانت ممتنعة في الدنيا لما سألها موسى، ولا يجوز على أحد من الأنبياء الجهل بشيء من أحكام الألوهية.
And 'Lord, show me [Yourself] that I may look at You.' He said, 'You will not see Me, but look at the mountain; if it should remain in its place, then you will see Me' [Al-A'raf: 143]. And the explanation of the indication from it: that it is a reference to a syllogism whose major premise was omitted due to being known. Its arrangement: Allah Most High conditioned the vision of His sacred essence upon the stability of the mountain at the moment of His manifestation to it, and that is a possible matter in itself necessarily, and everything that is conditioned upon the possible is nothing but possible because the meaning of conditioning is reporting that the conditioned thing occurs on the condition of the occurrence of that upon which it is conditioned, and the impossible occurs in no condition; so if the vision were not possible, there would be a contradiction in His news, which is impossible, and if it were impossible in this world, Musa would not have asked for it, and it is not permissible for any of the prophets to be ignorant of anything concerning the rulings of divinity.
Source pages p. 379

Commentary
This passage presents the detailed logical inference (taqrir al-dalalah) from the verse of Musa. It first quotes the verse. Then it explains that the verse is an indication of a syllogism (qiyas) whose major premise is omitted because it is well known. The author reconstructs the syllogism: the minor premise is that Allah conditioned the vision of His essence (ru'yat dhatihi al-muqaddasa) upon the stability of the mountain at the moment of His manifestation, and that stability is a possible matter in itself necessarily. The major premise (implied) is that everything conditioned upon something possible is itself possible, because the meaning of conditioning (al-ta'liq) is that the conditioned occurs only if the condition occurs, and the impossible never occurs under any condition. Therefore, ru'ya is possible. The author then presents a second exceptional proof (istidlal istithna'i): if ru'ya were not possible, then Allah's news would be false (khalaf), which is impossible. Also, if ru'ya were impossible in this world, Musa—who was a prophet—would not have asked for it, because prophets know what is permissible and impossible for Allah. Asking for something impossible would be ignorance, which is not allowed for prophets. Thus, ru'ya must be possible.

Key Terms
الرؤية
القياس
التعليق
الممكن
المحال
الخلف
الأنبياء
أحكام الألوهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the verse proves that ru'ya is impossible because Allah said 'You will not see Me'.
Correction: Al-Laqqani shows that the conditional (if mountain then you will see Me) proves ru'ya is possible, and the 'will not see' refers to this world, not the afterlife.

Checkpoint
What is the function of the omitted major premise in the syllogistic argument from the verse?
A) It states that ru'ya is impossible
B) It states that everything conditioned on the possible is possible
C) It states that Musa was ignorant
D) It states that the mountain will not remain stable

Key Takeaway
The verse of Musa, through its conditional structure and the infallibility of prophets, demonstrates that seeing Allah (ru'ya) is rationally possible and will occur for the believers in the afterlife.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just learned that Musa, a prophet, asked to see Allah because he knew it was possible. In your own dua, do you ever hold back from asking Allah for something great because you secretly think it's impossible? What does that reveal about your certainty in His power and generosity?
?
The proof hinges on the fact that prophets are never ignorant of Allah's attributes. Whom do you trust for your understanding of Allah's nature—and how have you verified that they are free from error?
?
The mountain crumbled when Allah manifested, but the possibility of seeing Him remained. What conditions for seeing Allah in the afterlife are you actively fulfilling right now? What are you neglecting?

Rational Proof for the Possibility of Seeing Allah
Consensus and rational proof for possibility of seeing Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the consensus and rational syllogism that establish the possibility of seeing Allah in the Hereafter.

Core Idea
Seeing Allah is possible because He exists, and whatever exists can be seen.

This lesson presents both the transmitted consensus of the Companions and a simple rational argument for the possibility of seeing Allah. The author combines scriptural foundation with logical reasoning to show why Ahl al-Sunnah affirm this doctrine.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Pay attention to the two main parts: consensus and rational proof.
The passage is concise; note the transition from 'As for consensus' to 'And the explanation of the intellectual proof'.
2
Identify the two parts
Identify the consensus statement (Companions' agreement on ru'ya) and the rational syllogism (Creator exists, every existent can be seen, therefore Creator can be seen).
The consensus provides transmitted authority; the syllogism provides rational grounding.
3
Reflect on the scope of the proof
Understand that the rational proof only establishes possibility (jawaz), not obligation (wujub). The obligation comes from transmitted evidence (sam').
This distinction is crucial: reason shows it is possible, revelation shows it is actual.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الإجماعُ : فهو أنَّ الصَّحابة رضي الله تعالى عنهم كانوا مجمعين على وقوع الرُّؤية في الآخرة، وأنَّ الآيات والأحاديث الواردة فيها محمولةٌ على ظواهرها من غير تأويل . ولهذهِ الأدلَّةِ السَّمعيَّة أَطبقَ أهلُ السُّنَّة على أنَّ رؤيةَ الله للجائزة عقلا واجبة سمعاً . وبيان الدليل العقلي على جوازها بطريق الاختصار: أنَّ الباري موجود ، وكلُّ موجودٍ يصحُ أنْ يُرى ؛ فالباري يَصحُ أَنْ يُرى .
As for consensus: it is that the Companions (may Allah be pleased with them) were unanimous upon the occurrence of the vision (ru'ya) in the Hereafter, and that the verses and hadiths concerning it are to be understood according to their apparent meanings without interpretation. And due to these transmitted proofs, the People of the Sunnah (Ahl al-Sunnah) have agreed that the vision of Allah, which is possible intellectually, is obligatory by transmitted evidence. And the explanation of the intellectual proof for its possibility in brief is: that the Creator exists, and every existent may validly be seen; therefore the Creator may validly be seen.
Source pages p. 381

Commentary
This passage is a proof. It begins by citing the consensus of the Companions on the occurrence of seeing Allah in the Hereafter, which establishes the transmitted basis. Then it states that the People of the Sunnah agree that it is intellectually possible and transmittedly obligatory. Then it gives the rational proof: since Allah exists, and every existent can be seen, Allah can be seen. This is a simple syllogism.

The Comprehensive Meaning of the Two Testimonies
Al-Sharnubi defines 'wa jami' ma'na' and explains the inclusion of all beliefs in the two testimonies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the phrase 'wa jami' ma'na' refers to the intended meaning that the two testimonies of Islam encompass all obligatory beliefs concerning divinity and prophethood in terms of obligation, possibility, and impossibility.

Core Idea
The two testimonies are the comprehensive foundation of all Islamic creed.

Al-Sharnubi begins by unpacking the phrase 'wa jami' ma'na' from the poem. He explains that it refers to the meaning that has been established—the totality of beliefs that every Muslim must hold. This lesson clarifies what that meaning is and how the two testimonies relate to Islam itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Al-Sharnubi uses a very compressed style here.
2
Understand the definition of 'wa jami' ma'na'
Identify that the phrase refers to the intended meaning which is the sum of all obligatory beliefs. Note that these beliefs are categorized by three rational judgments: wajib, jaiz, mustahil.
3
Examine the three relationships to Islam
The passage gives three ways the two testimonies relate to Islam: as the greatest part, as essential for attainment, or as indicators. Reflect on why these are important.
Each relationship emphasizes the centrality of the testimonies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قسم النبوات بالتحقيق» فقال: (وَجَامِعٌ مَعْنَى) وهو : ما يُراد مِن اللَّفظ (الَّذِي تَقَرَّرَا) أي جُعِلَ في قرارٍ ومحل يُرجع إليه فيه ، وهو جميع العقائد الإيمانية الواجبة الاعتقادِ شرعاً ، ممَّا يَرجعُ إلى الألوهية والنُّبوَّة وجوبا وجوازا واستحالة، شَهَادَنَا الإِسْلَام) أي : معنى الشَّهادتين اللتين هما الجزء الأعظم مِن مُسمَّى الإسلام، أو اللتين لا يحصل الإسلام إلَّا بهما، أو اللتين تدلان على الإسلام؛ فهو من إضافة الجزء للكُلِّ ، أو السَّبب للمُسبَّب، أو الدَّال للمدلول، وبيان ما ذكره: أنَّ الجملة الأولى أثبتت الألوهية له تعالى، في بيان تضمُّنِ الشَّهادتين لجميع العقائد الإيمانية الوَاجِبة. ونفتها عن كلّ ما سواه، وحقيقة الألوهية: وجوب الوجودِ والقدم الذاتي، ويلزم منه استغناؤه عن كلّ ما سواه، وافتقار كلّ ما سواه إليه ، كما يوجب له البقاء، ومخالفة الممكنات، والقيام بالذات والتنزه عن النقائص كالأغراض الأفعال والأحكام، وعن وجوب شيء عليه تعالى ؛ لئلا يكون مُستكملا بفعله أو تركه ؛ فلا يثبت له الاستغناء المطلق، ووجوب افتقار الممكنات إليه يستلزم وجوبَ حياته ، وعموم قدرته وإرادته وعلمه ، ووحدته وعدم تأثير شيء سواه تعالى في شيء منها، ومتى وجبت هذه الأمور له تعالى ؛ استحالت نقائضها عليه تعالى، وجاز ما سوى ذلك في حقه ؛ فقد اشتملت الجملة الأولى على أقسام الحكم العقلىّ الثَّلاثة الراجعة إليه تعالى.
He said: (And comprehensive of the meaning) which is what is intended from the word (that has been established), i.e., placed in a fixed place and location to which reference is made, and it is all the creeds necessarily believed by law, from what pertains to divinity and prophethood in terms of obligation, possibility, and impossibility. (The two testimonies of Islam), i.e., the meaning of the two testimonies which are the greatest part of the name of Islam, or the two without which Islam is not attained, or the two that indicate Islam; so it is either an addition of the part to the whole, or the cause to the effect, or the indicator to the indicated. And the explanation of what he mentioned: that the first sentence established divinity for Him, Exalted, in explanation of the two testimonies' inclusion of all the obligatory creeds. And it negated it from everything other than Him. And the reality of divinity: necessity of existence and self-eternity, and from it follows His independence from everything other than Him, and the dependence of everything other than Him upon Him, as it necessitates for Him permanence, and being different from contingent beings, and self-subsistence, and transcendence above deficiencies such as purposes, actions, and rulings, and above the obligation of anything upon Him, Exalted, so that He not be completed by His action or abandonment; thus absolute independence is not established for Him. And the obligation of the dependence of contingent beings upon Him necessitates the obligation of His life, and the comprehensiveness of His power, will, and knowledge, and His oneness, and the non-effect of anything other than Him, Exalted, in anything from them. And when these matters become obligatory for Him, Exalted, their opposites become impossible for Him, Exalted, and whatever is other than that is possible with respect to Him. So the first sentence included the three divisions of rational judgment pertaining to Him, Exalted.
Source pages p. 403

Commentary
This passage defines the phrase 'wa jami' ma'na' from the poem and explains how the two testimonies encompass all obligatory beliefs. It begins by stating that the phrase means the intended meaning of the word that has been established in a fixed place, and that this meaning refers to all the creeds necessarily believed by law, which pertain to divinity and prophethood in terms of obligation (wujub), possibility (jawaz), and impossibility (istihalah). Then it explains the relationship of the two testimonies to Islam, giving three possibilities: they are the greatest part, or without them Islam is not attained, or they indicate Islam. Then it focuses on the first testimony: it establishes divinity for Allah and negates it from all else. It then defines the reality of divinity as necessity of existence and self-eternity, which entails independence from all others and dependence of all others on Him. From that follow other necessary attributes like permanence, being different from contingents, self-subsistence, transcendence from deficiencies, and freedom from obligation. The author then explains that the dependence of contingents necessitates Allah's life, power, will, knowledge, oneness, and non-effect of others. Once these are obligatory (wajib), their opposites become impossible (mustahil), and whatever else is possible (jaiz). Thus the first testimony contains all three rational judgments (wajib, mustahil, jaiz) regarding Allah.

Key Terms
شهادتا الإسلام
أقسام الحكم العقلي
وجوب
جواز

The First Testimony and the Three Rational Judgments
Al-Sharnubi shows how the first testimony encompasses the three rational judgments regarding Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the first testimony (لا إله إلا الله) establishes divinity and necessarily leads to the three rational judgments of obligation, impossibility, and possibility regarding Allah.

Core Idea
The first testimony logically entails all necessary divine attributes and their opposites.

In the second part of the passage, Al-Sharnubi demonstrates the rich implications of the first testimony. He shows that by affirming divinity for Allah and negating it from all else, the testimony logically implies a chain of necessary attributes, their impossible opposites, and all possibilities in between. This lesson traces that logical derivation.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Pay attention to the logical connectives like 'فإن', 'وكما', 'ومتى'.
2
Identify the starting point: divinity affirmed and negated
The first testimony establishes divinity for Allah and negates it from everything else. That is the foundation.
3
Follow the entailment chain
From the reality of divinity (wajib al-wujud) follow necessary attributes like independence, life, power, etc. Notice how each step is derived from the previous.
This is a crucial logical argument.
4
Conclude with the three rational judgments
Once the necessary attributes are established, their opposites become impossible, and all else is possible. Thus the testimony contains wajib, mustahil, and jaiz regarding Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قسم النبوات بالتحقيق» فقال: (وَجَامِعٌ مَعْنَى) وهو : ما يُراد مِن اللَّفظ (الَّذِي تَقَرَّرَا) أي جُعِلَ في قرارٍ ومحل يُرجع إليه فيه ، وهو جميع العقائد الإيمانية الواجبة الاعتقادِ شرعاً ، ممَّا يَرجعُ إلى الألوهية والنُّبوَّة وجوبا وجوازا واستحالة، شَهَادَنَا الإِسْلَام) أي : معنى الشَّهادتين اللتين هما الجزء الأعظم مِن مُسمَّى الإسلام، أو اللتين لا يحصل الإسلام إلَّا بهما، أو اللتين تدلان على الإسلام؛ فهو من إضافة الجزء للكُلِّ ، أو السَّبب للمُسبَّب، أو الدَّال للمدلول، وبيان ما ذكره: أنَّ الجملة الأولى أثبتت الألوهية له تعالى، في بيان تضمُّنِ الشَّهادتين لجميع العقائد الإيمانية الوَاجِبة. ونفتها عن كلّ ما سواه، وحقيقة الألوهية: وجوب الوجودِ والقدم الذاتي، ويلزم منه استغناؤه عن كلّ ما سواه، وافتقار كلّ ما سواه إليه ، كما يوجب له البقاء، ومخالفة الممكنات، والقيام بالذات والتنزه عن النقائص كالأغراض الأفعال والأحكام، وعن وجوب شيء عليه تعالى ؛ لئلا يكون مُستكملا بفعله أو تركه ؛ فلا يثبت له الاستغناء المطلق، ووجوب افتقار الممكنات إليه يستلزم وجوبَ حياته ، وعموم قدرته وإرادته وعلمه ، ووحدته وعدم تأثير شيء سواه تعالى في شيء منها، ومتى وجبت هذه الأمور له تعالى ؛ استحالت نقائضها عليه تعالى، وجاز ما سوى ذلك في حقه ؛ فقد اشتملت الجملة الأولى على أقسام الحكم العقلىّ الثَّلاثة الراجعة إليه تعالى.
He said: (And comprehensive of the meaning) which is what is intended from the word (that has been established), i.e., placed in a fixed place and location to which reference is made, and it is all the creeds necessarily believed by law, from what pertains to divinity and prophethood in terms of obligation, possibility, and impossibility. (The two testimonies of Islam), i.e., the meaning of the two testimonies which are the greatest part of the name of Islam, or the two without which Islam is not attained, or the two that indicate Islam; so it is either an addition of the part to the whole, or the cause to the effect, or the indicator to the indicated. And the explanation of what he mentioned: that the first sentence established divinity for Him, Exalted, in explanation of the two testimonies' inclusion of all the obligatory creeds. And it negated it from everything other than Him. And the reality of divinity: necessity of existence and self-eternity, and from it follows His independence from everything other than Him, and the dependence of everything other than Him upon Him, as it necessitates for Him permanence, and being different from contingent beings, and self-subsistence, and transcendence above deficiencies such as purposes, actions, and rulings, and above the obligation of anything upon Him, Exalted, so that He not be completed by His action or abandonment; thus absolute independence is not established for Him. And the obligation of the dependence of contingent beings upon Him necessitates the obligation of His life, and the comprehensiveness of His power, will, and knowledge, and His oneness, and the non-effect of anything other than Him, Exalted, in anything from them. And when these matters become obligatory for Him, Exalted, their opposites become impossible for Him, Exalted, and whatever is other than that is possible with respect to Him. So the first sentence included the three divisions of rational judgment pertaining to Him, Exalted.
Source pages p. 403

Commentary
This passage defines the phrase 'wa jami' ma'na' from the poem and explains how the two testimonies encompass all obligatory beliefs. It begins by stating that the phrase means the intended meaning of the word that has been established in a fixed place, and that this meaning refers to all the creeds necessarily believed by law, which pertain to divinity and prophethood in terms of obligation (wujub), possibility (jawaz), and impossibility (istihalah). Then it explains the relationship of the two testimonies to Islam, giving three possibilities: they are the greatest part, or without them Islam is not attained, or they indicate Islam. Then it focuses on the first testimony: it establishes divinity for Allah and negates it from all else. It then defines the reality of divinity as necessity of existence and self-eternity, which entails independence from all others and dependence of all others on Him. From that follow other necessary attributes like permanence, being different from contingents, self-subsistence, transcendence from deficiencies, and freedom from obligation. The author then explains that the dependence of contingents necessitates Allah's life, power, will, knowledge, oneness, and non-effect of others. Once these are obligatory (wajib), their opposites become impossible (mustahil), and whatever else is possible (jaiz). Thus the first testimony contains all three rational judgments (wajib, mustahil, jaiz) regarding Allah.

Correction: The passage shows that affirming divinity necessarily leads to a chain of attributes and the three rational judgments.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The first testimony encompasses the three rational judgments (wajib, mustahil, jaiz) regarding Allah by logically deriving necessary attributes and their opposites.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You recite 'la ilaha illa Allah' multiple times a day. But have you truly internalized that this testimony logically demands that Allah is absolutely independent and you are completely dependent? Where in your life do you still act as if you are self-sufficient or as if others hold ultimate control over your wellbeing? What would change if you lived every moment with this awareness?
?
The passage states that if an obligation were upon Allah, He would be completed by His action. This means Allah does not act for His own benefit. When you worship, do you ever subconsciously think that your worship benefits Allah or that He needs your devotion? How does this truth reshape the sincerity of your intention?
?
Understanding that Allah's attributes like knowledge, power, and will are not arbitrary but logically necessary from tawhid — does this deepen your conviction? When doubts arise about these attributes, will you return to this logical derivation to strengthen your faith? How can you make this proof a living part of your belief?

The Consensus on Prophet Muhammad's Superiority
States the consensus that Prophet Muhammad is the best of creation and commands acceptance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the superiority of Prophet Muhammad over all creation is a matter of consensus (ijma') and why it is impermissible to dispute it.

Core Idea
The Prophet's superiority is not debatable—it is a settled consensus.

In this passage, the author establishes a foundational belief of Ahl al-Sunnah: that Prophet Muhammad is the best of all creatures. He presents this as a matter of consensus (ijma'), and commands the reader to accept it without dispute. Let's examine the text and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic carefully, then the English translation. Notice the author's commanding tone.
2
Identify key terms: إجماع and شقاق
These terms are central. Understanding 'ijma' as a source of authority is crucial.
The footnote explains the etymology of shiqaq.
3
Reflect on the command to turn away from dispute
Why does the author emphasize avoiding dispute? Because the matter is settled by consensus.
This connects to the broader principle of respecting ijma.
4
Apply: Why is breaking consensus not permissible?
The passage says directly: because it is not permissible to break consensus. This is a foundational principle in Islamic theology.
Consensus is a binding proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بيان أن أفضل المخلوقات جميعها هو نبينا محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم
إذا عرفت هذا الحكم المُجمع عليه (فَمِلْ عَنِ الشَّقَاقِ) أي : المُنازعة فيه (١) ، واجزم به معتقدا صحته ؛ لأنَّه لا يجوز الإقدام على خرق الإجماع.
(١) سميت المنازعة ،شقاقا ، لأن كلا من المتنازعين يكون في شي ليس فيه الآخر، أي: في جانب من الشنواني.
Statement that the best of all creatures is our Prophet Muhammad, peace be upon him. If you have known this agreed-upon ruling, then turn away from discord, that is, dispute (1), and be certain of it, believing in its correctness, because it is not permissible to proceed to break consensus. (1) The dispute is called 'shiqaq' because each of the two disputants is in a side not in the other, i.e., in a side of al-Shanwani.
Source pages p. 410

Commentary
This passage asserts that Prophet Muhammad is the best of all creation, a matter of consensus. The author commands the reader to avoid disputing this and to hold it as certain belief, since breaking consensus is prohibited. The footnote explains the term 'shiqaq' (discord) as deriving from each disputant being in a separate 'side' (shiq).

?

Ranking of Virtue: Prophets and Angels
Explains the ranking of virtue after Prophet Muhammad: prophets then angels, with details on variation and belief.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Islamic hierarchy of virtue after Prophet Muhammad, including the positions of prophets and angels, the variations among prophets, and the basis of their ranking.

Core Idea
After Muhammad, the most virtuous are the prophets, then the angels.

This lesson clarifies the order of virtue among Allah's creation. Starting from our Prophet Muhammad, the text explains how the prophets follow him, and then the angels follow the prophets. It also touches on how believers should approach the ranking of specific individuals based on revelation, avoiding speculation.

Guided Path
1
Understand the overall hierarchy
The passage begins by stating that the prophets follow Prophet Muhammad in virtue, and after them come the angels. Grasp this basic structure before diving into details.
2
Explore variation among prophets
Notice that prophets vary in their closeness to Muhammad. The text highlights the Ul al-Azm messengers as superior to other messengers, and messengers superior to non-messenger prophets.
The ranking among prophets is based on revelation, not speculation.
3
Learn the rule of belief in ranking
The passage emphasizes that we must believe in superiority according to what has been reported, and we must not rush to designate superiority where no specific revelation has come. This is why the author left some ambiguity.
This principle prevents theological error.
4
Angels rank after prophets
After the prophets, the rank of angels follows. In general, angels are above non-prophet humans, but in detail, only the chief angels (like Jibril, Mikail, Israfil, Azrael) directly follow the prophets.
Even non-messenger angels are superior to saintly humans.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والأَنْبِيَا يَلُونَهُ في الفَضْلِ وَبَعْدَهُمْ مَلائِكَةُ (٢) ذِي الفَضْلِ
الصَّلاة والسَّلام يجب أن يُعتقد أنَّهم (يَلُونَهُ) أي:، (والأَنْبِيَاء) عليهم يتبعون نبينا محمداً ( في الفَضْل) فمرتبتهم فيه بعد مرتبته ؛ وإن تفاوتوا فيها بالنسبة للقُرب (٣) على ما يأتي في قوله: (وبعض كلّ بعضه قد يفضّل فبقيَّةُ أولى العزم (٤) مِن الرُّسل أفضلُ مِن بقيَّة الرُّسل ، ثم بقيَّة الرُّسل أفضل من الأنبياء غير الرُّسل ، والواجب اعتقاد أفضليّة الأفضل على طبق ما ورد الحكم به تفصيلا في التفصيلي ، وإجمالاً في الإجمالي، ويمتنع الهجوم على التعيين فيما لم يرد فيه توقيف ، ولهذا أَبْهِمَ النَّاظم(٥) في الفاضل والمفضول لينطبق كلامه على كلّ مَن عُلم كذلك.
(٢) ملائكة : يُقرأ المتن بسكون التاء ، وإدغامها في الذال ؛ للوزن الشنواني .
(٣) قوله : (للقرب) أي: القرب المعنوي من جهة الفضل، فمرتبة إبراهيم قريبة من مرتبته قربا معنويا ، ويليه موسى . الشنواني
(٤) قوله: (أولي العزم العزم في الأصل التصميم على الشيء، ثم نقل إلى: الصبر وتحمل المشاق، وهو المراد هنا ، أي : وأصحاب الصبر . وأفضل أولي العزم على التحقيق: محمد
، ثم إبراهيم، ثم موسى ، ثم عيسى ، ثم نوح ، ونظم بعضهم ترتيبهم بقوله: محمد إبراهيم موسى کلیمه فعيسى فنوح هم أهل العزم فاعلم قال الشيخ عبد السلام ولو ذهب ذاهب إلى الوقف عن تعين الفاضل منهم والمفضول بعد نبينا .. لم يبعد من الصواب ، ثم إن التفضيل إنما يرجع لاختياره سبحانه لا لعلة في الفاضل وليست في المفضول ، والله يفضل من يشاء لا يسأل عما يفعل. الشنواني .
(٥) أي في قوله: (وبعض كل بعضه قد يفضلُ .

بيانُ أنَّ رُتبة الملائكةِ تَلي رُتبة الأنبياء في الفَضْل]
(وَبَعْدَهُم) أي: وبعد الأنبياء في الفضيلة (مَلَائِكة) اللهِ (ذِي الفَضْلِ) فمرتبتهم تلي مرتبة الأنبياء عليهم السَّلام في الجملة ؛ فالملائكة - ولو غير رُسل - أفضل مِن غير الأنبياء من البشر ولو كان وليا ؛ كأبي بكر وعمر وإنما قلنا: في الجملة» لأنَّ الذي يلي الأنبياء من الملائكة على التفصيل « إِنَّما هو رؤساؤهم، كجبريل وميكائيل وإسرافيل وعزرائيل.
And the prophets follow him in virtue, and after them the angels of virtue. Prayer and peace must be believed that they follow him, i.e., the prophets follow our Prophet Muhammad in virtue; so their rank is after his rank, although they vary in it with respect to proximity (3), as will come in his saying: 'And some of each may be preferred over others; the rest of the Ul al-Azm (4) from the messengers are better than the rest of the messengers, then the rest of the messengers are better than non-messenger prophets.' And it is obligatory to believe the superiority of the superior according to what has been reported of the ruling in detail in detailed matters and in summary in summary matters, and it is prohibited to rush to designation where no stop (tawqif) has come, and for this the author left ambiguous (5) the superior and the inferior so that his speech applies to all who are known as such. (2) 'Mala'ika': the text is read with sukun on the ta' and its assimilation into the dhal for the meter of al-Shanwani. (3) His saying: 'for proximity' i.e., spiritual proximity in terms of virtue; the rank of Ibrahim is close to his rank spiritually, followed by Musa. Al-Shanwani. (4) His saying: 'Ul al-Azm': 'azm originally means determination, then transferred to patience and bearing hardships, which is intended here, i.e., the people of patience. And the best of Ul al-Azm in verification is Muhammad, then Ibrahim, then Musa, then Isa, then Nuh. Some have organized their order with his saying: Muhammad, Ibrahim, Musa his interlocutor, then Isa, then Nuh, they are the people of determination, so know. Sheikh Abdul Salam said: And if someone were to go to stop at determining the superior and inferior among them after our Prophet... it would not be far from correctness. Then, preference is due to His choice, Exalted is He, not due to a cause in the superior that is not in the inferior, and Allah favors whom He wills; He is not asked about what He does. Al-Shanwani. (5) i.e., in his saying: 'And some of each may be preferred over others.'

Statement that the rank of angels follows the rank of prophets in virtue: 'And after them' i.e., after the prophets in virtue, the angels of Allah of virtue, so their rank follows the rank of the prophets, peace be upon them, in general. So the angels—even if not messengers—are better than non-prophet humans even if a saint, like Abu Bakr and Umar. And we only said 'in general' because those who follow the prophets from the angels in detail are only their chiefs, like Jibril, Mika'il, Israfil, and Azrael.
Source pages p. 410

Commentary
This passage details the hierarchy of virtue: after Prophet Muhammad, the other prophets follow, then the angels. It explains that prophets vary in closeness to him, with the Ul al-Azm messengers being superior to other messengers, and messengers superior to non-messenger prophets. It emphasizes that belief must align with revealed details, and one should not speculate about unspecified rankings. The angels' rank generally follows prophets, and even non-messenger angels are above non-prophet saints. However, in detail, only the chief angels (Jibril, etc.) directly follow prophets. Footnotes clarify linguistic readings and elaborate on the Ul al-Azm order and the principle that preference is by divine choice, not inherent cause.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All angels are equal in rank to prophets.
Correction: The passage specifies that only the chief angels (Jibril, Mika'il, Israfil, Azrael) directly follow the prophets; other angels are still above non-prophet humans but below prophets.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which angels are specifically mentioned as following the prophets in rank?
A) All angels
B) The angels of mercy
C) The archangels Jibril, Mika'il, Israfil, and Azrael
D) The angel of death

Key Takeaway
The Islamic hierarchy of virtue after Prophet Muhammad is: first the prophets (with variations), then the angels, and belief must adhere to revealed details without speculation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see someone else being given a position or favor that you desire, do you react with resentment or with acceptance of Allah's choice? This lesson teaches that Allah's preference is not based on causes we can comprehend. Can you truly surrender your judgment to His wisdom in the specific areas of your life where you feel overlooked? Resolve today to say 'Allah prefers whom He wills' with full contentment.
?
The lesson reminds us that belief in the revealed hierarchy of virtue is obligatory, and speculation about what is not revealed is prohibited. Are there areas of your faith where you find yourself demanding answers that Allah has not given? Are you comfortable with ambiguity in religious matters, or do you insist on knowing everything? Commit to trusting the limits of revelation.
?
Angels, even non-messenger angels, are superior to the greatest human saints like Abu Bakr and Umar. Does your heart truly accept this ranking, or do you feel a pull of pride for human achievements? How does this affect your humility before the unseen creation? Make a firm intention to never consider any human, including yourself, as superior to the angels in virtue, and let that shape your awe of Allah's creation.
