The Relative Merits of the Prophet's Wives
Sanusi presents opinions on ranking the Prophet's wives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the different bases for ranking the Prophet's wives and the scholarly caution against definitive ranking.

Core Idea
Preference among the Prophet's wives varies by virtue, but ultimate ranking by reward is best left undecided.

Sanusi presents two key positions on the relative merits of the Prophet's wives. Shaykh al-Islam argues that each wife excels in a particular aspect, while al-Ash'ari advises stopping from ranking altogether. This lesson explores these views and their implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Notice the different opinions presented.
2
Identify the two main opinions
Look for the opinion of Shaykh al-Islam and the alternative view of al-Ash'ari. What is the basis for each?
Shaykh al-Islam considers different aspects; al-Ash'ari considers reward.
3
Understand the basis for each wife's virtue
Note the specific virtue attributed to 'Aisha, Khadija, Fatima, Maryam, and Asiya.
Each has a distinct excellence: knowledge, precedence, kinship, etc.
4
Reflect on the cautionary principle
Consider why al-Ash'ari and the author favor stopping from ranking. How does this affect our understanding of these reports?
5
Apply to contemporary discourse
Think about how this nuanced approach can prevent oversimplified comparisons among the Prophet's family.
The lesson teaches humility in ranking.

تفاضل زوجاته وبناته فيما بينهن ومع نساء العالمين
وقال شيخُ الإسلام في شرح البخاري: الذي أختارُه الآنَ أَنَّ الأفضلية محمولة على أحوال، فعائشةُ أفضلهنَّ مِن حيثُ العِلم، وخديجة من حيثُ تَقدُّمُها وإعانتها له في المهمات، وفاطمة من حيثُ القرابة، ومريم من حيثُ الاختلافُ في نُبُوَّتها وذكرها في القرآن مع الأنبياء، وآسية امرأة فرعون من هذه الحيثية، لكن لم تُذكر مع الأنبياء، وعلى ذلك تُنَزَّلُ الأخبار الواردة في أفضليَّتِهنَّ. اهـ. وهذا جيّد إن قلنا: إن التفضيل بالأحوال وكثرة الخصال الجميلة، وأما إن قلنا : إنَّه باعتبار كثرةِ الثَّواب، فالأقرب الوقف، كما هو قول الأشعري له.
وفي كلام البرهان الحلبي: أنَّ زينب بنت جحش تلي عائشة رضي الله تعالى عنهما، ولم يقف أستاذنا على نص في باقيهنَّ.
قسم النبوات
بعض أبنائه الذكور على بعض، ولا في المفاضلة بينهم وبين البنات الشريفات، سوى ما شرف الله به الذَّكور على الإناث مطلقا، ولا بينهنَّ سوى فاطمة، فإنَّها أفضلُ بناته الكريمات ولا بين باقي البنات سوى فاطمة الزوجات الطَّاهرات، وإن جرت عِلَّة فاطمة بالبضعيَّة في الجميع فالوقف أسلم، والله أعلم.
The preference of his wives and daughters among themselves and with the women of the worlds. And Shaykh al-Islam said in his commentary on al-Bukhari: What I choose now is that preference is borne upon circumstances, so 'Aisha is the most virtuous of them in terms of knowledge, Khadija in terms of her precedence and her assistance to him in important matters, Fatima in terms of kinship, Maryam in terms of the disagreement about her prophethood and her mention in the Qur'an with the prophets, and Asiya the wife of Pharaoh in this respect, but she was not mentioned with the prophets, and upon that are interpreted the reports that came regarding their preference. End. And this is good if we say: preference is by circumstances and abundance of beautiful qualities. But if we say: it is by consideration of abundance of reward, then the closest is to stop, as is the statement of al-Ash'ari. And in the speech of al-Burhan al-Halabi: That Zaynab bint Jahsh follows 'Aisha, may Allah be pleased with them both, and our teacher did not find a text concerning the rest of them. The Section of Prophethood. Some of his male sons over others, and there is no preference between them and the noble daughters, except what Allah honored the males over the females absolutely, nor between them except Fatima, for she is the best of his noble daughters, nor between the rest of the daughters except Fatima among the pure wives, and if the cause of Fatima by the union applies to all, then stopping is safer, and Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 452

Commentary
This passage discusses the relative merits (tafaadul) of the Prophet's wives and children. It begins by reporting two key opinions. First, Shaykh al-Islam Zakariyya al-Ansari holds that preference among the wives is based on different aspects: 'Aisha excels in knowledge, Khadija in precedence and support, Fatima in kinship, Maryam and Asiya in their unique association with prophethood. This means the reports about their virtues should be understood in this light. The author then critiques this view: it is acceptable if we consider preference by circumstances and qualities, but if we consider preference by reward (thawaab), then the safer position is to refrain from judgment (al-waqf), as al-Ash'ari held. Second, al-Burhaan al-Halabi states that Zaynab bint Jahsh comes after 'Aisha, but no definitive text is found for the others. The passage then moves to the Prophet's children: males are preferred over females absolutely, but among the females, Fatima is the best. Among the wives, Fatima is also singled out, but if the reason for her superiority applies to all (i.e., her being a part of the Prophet through marriage), then stopping from further ranking is safer. The lesson emphasizes cautious ranking and reliance on explicit textual indications.

Key Terms
تفاضل
الأفضلية
الوقف
أحوال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One of the Prophet's wives is absolutely superior to all others in every way.
Correction: Each wife excels in a specific aspect (e.g., 'Aisha in knowledge, Khadija in precedence), and ranking by reward is best left undecided.

Checkpoint
According to Shaykh al-Islam, on what basis is 'Aisha considered the most virtuous of the Prophet's wives?
A) Her wealth and lineage
B) Her knowledge
C) Her early conversion
D) Her number of children

Key Takeaway
The Prophet's wives have distinct virtues, but definitive ranking is avoided to remain faithful to the texts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at the Prophet's wives and their distinct virtues, do you ever catch yourself comparing people in your own life to see who is 'better' overall? How can you practice the humility of waqf—stopping judgment—when you don't know someone's full reward with Allah?
?
This lesson says: 'if we say preference is by reward, then the closest is to stop.' Is there a relationship or person in your life where you have been quick to rank or judge based on outward qualities, forgetting that only Allah knows the true reward? What would it look like to suspend that judgment today?
?
The scholars differed on how to rank the Prophet's wives, yet they remained respectful and cautious. Are you willing to let go of the need to definitively rank people’s spiritual status, including your own, and instead focus on cultivating multiple virtues as each of these great women did?

The Relative Merits of the Prophet's Children
Sanusi addresses the ranking of the Prophet's children and the caution against overranking.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the general principle that male children are preferred over female children, and that Fatima holds a special status among the daughters and wives, but further ranking is safest left undecided.

Core Idea
Among the Prophet's children, males are generally superior, and Fatima is the best of his daughters, but caution advises against further ranking.

Sanusi continues by discussing the relative merits of the Prophet's children. He clarifies that males are preferred over females absolutely, and that Fatima is the best of his daughters and among the wives. However, if the reason for Fatima's distinction applies to all, stopping from ranking is safest. This lesson examines these nuanced distinctions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the section heading 'قسم النبوات'.
2
Identify the key points about sons and daughters
Note that there is no preference among sons, and that males are generally favored over females.
The passage says 'لا في المفاضلة بينهم وبين البنات' except for the general honor of males.
3
Focus on Fatima's status
Understand that Fatima is explicitly said to be the best of his daughters and also among the pure wives.
She is singled out for excellence.
4
Understand the cautionary conclusion
If the cause (budge'iyya) applies to all, then stopping from ranking is safer.
The term 'budge'iyya' refers to being part of the Prophet's family through marriage.
5
Reflect on the overall lesson
Consider why the author ends with 'والله أعلم' (Allah knows best), indicating humility in such matters.

The Expiation of Sins: Major and Minor
Commentary states rule on expiation of sins and cites Quran and hadith

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Islamic teaching that major sins require repentance or Allah's grace, while minor sins can be expiated by good deeds, provided major sins are avoided.

Core Idea
Major sins need repentance; minor sins are erased by good deeds.

In this passage, the commentator explains a fundamental principle of Islamic theology regarding sin and forgiveness. He distinguishes between major and minor sins and clarifies the means by which each is expiated. The lesson draws from the Quran and Prophetic traditions to ground this teaching.

Guided Path
1
Understand the distinction
Learn the difference between major sins (kaba'ir) and minor sins (sagha'ir) and their respective expiation requirements as stated in the passage.
Major sins require repentance or Allah's grace; minor sins can be expiated by good deeds.
2
Examine the evidence from revelation
Read the Quranic verse (Hud 11:114) and the hadith about following evil with good, which support that good deeds erase minor sins.
The passage also cites hadith about wudu and prayer as examples.
3
Recognize the condition for acceptance
Understand that all acts of worship (wudu, prayer, Ramadan, Hajj) are conditioned upon avoiding major sins, as mentioned in the two Sahihs.
This condition is crucial to avoid misunderstanding that good deeds suffice for major sins.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كفَّارَاتُ الذُّنوبِ وأَسبَابُ المَغفِرة الصَّحيح ، وأمَّا الكبائر فلا يُكفّرها إِلَّا التَّوبةُ أو فضل الله تعالى. وأشار بقوله: (وَجَا الْوُضُو يُكَفِّرُ) الصَّغائر أيضا .. إلى عدم انحصار تكفيرها في اجتناب الكبائر لقوله تعالى: ﴿إِنَّ الْحَسَنَتِ يُذْهِبْنَ السَّيِّئَاتِ [هود: ١١٤]، وفي الحديث : وَأَتَّبع السيئة الحسنة تمحها، وأراد بقوله: (وَجَاء) أي: في السُّنَّة ؛ إذ فيها : مَن تَوضّأ نحو وضوئي هذا ، ثُمَّ قامَ فركع ركعتين لا يُحدِّث فيهما نَفْسَهُ) يعني بسوء «غُفِرَ له ما تقدَّم مِن «غُفِرَ له ما تقدَّم من ذنبه» وفي رواية: «لا يتوضأ رجلٌ مُسلِم فيُحسن الوضوء فيُصلِّي صلاة .. إِلَّا غُفر له ما بينه وبين الصَّلاة التي تليها » وكذا الصَّلوات الخمس ، وكذا رمضان ، وكذا الحج المبرور ، والكلُّ مَشروط باجتناب الكبائر كما في الصحيحين.
Expiation of sins and means of forgiveness: The correct view is that major sins are not expiated except by repentance or the grace of Allah, may He be exalted. And he indicated by his saying: (And wudu came expiating) minor sins as well… to the non-exclusivity of their expiation by avoiding major sins, because of His saying, exalted: 'Indeed, good deeds remove evil deeds' [Hud 11:114], and in the hadith: 'Follow the evil deed with a good deed and it will erase it.' And he intended by his saying: (And came) meaning in the Sunnah; for in it: 'Whoever performs wudu like this wudu of mine, then stands and prays two rak'ahs without speaking to himself between them,' meaning with evil, 'will be forgiven his past sins.' And in another narration: 'No Muslim man performs wudu and does it well, then prays a prayer, except that he is forgiven for what is between it and the prayer that follows it.' Likewise the five prayers, likewise Ramadan, likewise the accepted Hajj, and all are conditioned upon avoiding major sins as in the two Sahihs.
Source pages p. 528

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a line of poetry about expiation of sins. It begins by stating the correct position: major sins (kaba'ir) are not expiated except by sincere repentance (tawba) or by the sheer grace (fadl) of Allah. For minor sins, the author points to the line (interpreted as 'wudu came expiating minor sins') to show that expiation is not limited to avoiding major sins. He supports this with Qur'an 11:114 and a hadith command to follow a bad deed with a good one. The hadith about wudu and two rak'ahs is quoted, as well as variations. The same applies to the five daily prayers, Ramadan fasting, and the accepted Hajj. However, all these are conditioned upon avoiding major sins, as established in al-Bukhari and Muslim. The overall point is that while good deeds erase minor sins, they do not suffice for major ones without repentance.

Key Terms
الكبائر (major sins)
الصَّغائر (minor sins)
التَّوبة (repentance)
الحسنات (good deeds)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some believe that good deeds like wudu automatically expiate all sins, including major ones.
Correction: The passage clarifies that good deeds only expiate minor sins; major sins require repentance or Allah's grace.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what expiates major sins?
A) Wudu and prayer
B) Avoiding major sins only
C) Repentance or the grace of Allah
D) Good deeds generally

Key Takeaway
Major sins require repentance or Allah's grace, while minor sins are expiated by good deeds, provided one avoids major sins.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever relied on your regular prayers or fasting as a 'spiritual safety net' while knowingly holding onto a major sin you have not repented from? What is stopping you from making sincere tawba today?
?
When you perform wudu and pray, do you consciously remind yourself that this act is a means to erase your minor sins, or do you rush through it mechanically? How can you make your wudu and prayer more intentional as an act of seeking forgiveness?
?
This lesson teaches that avoiding major sins is a condition for your good deeds to expiate minor sins. Which major sin are you most vulnerable to, and what practical steps can you take this week to guard against it?

The Divine Permission to Forgive Sins Other than Shirk
Al-Laqani discusses the permissibility and wisdom of forgiving sins other than shirk.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the theological basis for why Allah may forgive sins other than shirk, and the wisdom behind making forgiveness conditional on divine will.

Core Idea
Forgiveness is Allah's right, but His mercy outweighs punishment for sins, not for disbelief.

This lesson explores a crucial distinction in Islamic theology: why Allah can forgive any sin except shirk. Al-Laqani lays out a rational and scriptural argument that forgiveness of sins is a beneficent act with no harm, supported by multiple Quranic verses, and explains the wisdom behind differentiating sins from disbelief. Let's examine his reasoning and the evidence he provides.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of pardon
Read the first part where Al-Laqani defines 'al-‘afw' as dropping a punishment due to Allah. Notice that he argues it is good because it benefits the servant without harming anyone.
This sets the moral framework for forgiveness.
2
Examine the Quranic evidence
Three verses are cited. Identify each and how they build the case: (1) Allah accepts repentance and pardons, (2) Allah forgives all sins, (3) Allah does not forgive shirk but forgives what is less to whom He wills.
The third verse is key—it explicitly excludes shirk.
3
Clarify what forgiveness means
Note that forgiveness involves both waiving punishment and concealing the sin after accountability.
4
Understand the wisdom behind the distinction
Al-Laqani gives two reasons: sins involve fear and hope, and they arise from momentary passion, whereas disbelief is a permanent doctrine whose sanctity cannot be lifted.
This rationalizes why shirk is unforgivable without repentance.
5
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this balances divine justice and mercy, and why it encourages repentance while maintaining the gravity of disbelief.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بيان أن غفران غير الكفر مسوغ للشفاعة
العفو (١): بأنَّ العقابَ حَقَّهُ تعالى ، فيَحسُن إسقاطه ، مع أنَّ فيه نفعا للعبد من غير ضرر لأحدٍ ، وفي القرآن (٢): ﴿وَهُوَ الَّذِى يَقْبَلُ التَّوْبَةَ عَنْ عِبَادِهِ، وَيَعْفُوا عَنِ السَّيِّئَاتِ ﴾ [الشورى: ٢٥]. ﴿إِنَّ اللَّهَ يَغْفِرُ الذُّنُوبَ جَمِيعًا﴾ [الزمر: ٥٣]. ﴿ إِنَّ اللَّهَ لَا يَغْفِرُ أَن يُشْرَكَ بِهِ، وَيَغْفِرُ مَا دُونَ ذَلِكَ لِمَن يَشَاءُ ﴾ [النساء: ٤٨] .
والمراد بغُفْرَانِها والعَفْو عنها (٣) : تركُ عُقوبَة صاحبها والستر عليه بعد المؤاخَذة.
والحكمة في غُفران المعاصي دون الكفر: أنَّها لا تنفك عن خوف عقاب (٤) ورَجاءِ عَفو ورحمةٍ وغير ذلك ، بخلاف الكفر ، ولأنَّها لوقت الهوى والشَّهوة (٥) فقط ، بخلاف الكفر ؛ فإنَّه مَذهب يُعتَقدُ للأبد، وحرمته لا تحتمل الارتفاع أصلا ، فكذلك عقوبته ، بخلاف المعصية.
Explanation that forgiveness of other than disbelief is permissible for intercession. Pardon (1): that punishment is the right of Allah, so dropping it is good, and there is benefit for the servant with no harm to anyone. And in the Quran (2): {And it is He who accepts repentance from His servants and pardons misdeeds} [Shura: 25]; {Indeed, Allah forgives all sins} [Zumar: 53]; {Indeed, Allah does not forgive association with Him, but He forgives what is less than that for whom He wills} [Nisa: 48]. And what is meant by forgiving and pardoning them (3): leaving off punishing its perpetrator and concealing after calling to account. And the wisdom in forgiving sins but not disbelief: that they [sins] are not free from fear of punishment (4) and hope of pardon and mercy and other things, unlike disbelief. And because they [occur] at the time of passion and desire (5) only, unlike disbelief; for it is a doctrine held forever, and its sanctity cannot be lifted at all, and so its punishment, unlike sin.
Source pages p. 560

Commentary
This passage is an argument for the permissibility of forgiving sins other than shirk. The author begins by defining 'pardon' (al-‘afw) as the act of remitting a punishment that is rightfully due to Allah, which is morally good because it benefits the servant without harming anyone. He then cites three Quranic verses as evidence: the first shows Allah accepts repentance and pardons misdeeds; the second affirms that Allah forgives all sins; and the third explicitly states that while Allah does not forgive shirk, He forgives everything else to whom He wills. Next, he clarifies that the terms 'forgiveness' (ghufrān) and 'pardon' (al-‘afw) both refer to waiving punishment and concealing the sin after accountability. Finally, he explains the divine wisdom behind forgiving sins but not disbelief: sins are always accompanied by fear of punishment and hope for mercy, and they arise from momentary passion and desire, whereas disbelief is a permanent, willful creed whose sanctity cannot be lifted—therefore its punishment is eternal, unlike that of sin.

Key Terms
العفو
الغفران
الكفر
المعصية
الحكمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that forgiveness means sins are trivial or that one can sin freely without consequence.
Correction: Forgiveness is contingent on divine will and repentance, and the severity of sin varies; shirk is particularly unforgivable without repentance.

Checkpoint
Which verse does the author cite to show that Allah forgives all sins except shirk?
A) وَهُوَ الَّذِى يَقْبَلُ التَّوْبَةَ عَنْ عِبَادِهِ
B) إِنَّ اللَّهَ لَا يَغْفِرُ أَن يُشْرَكَ بِهِ
C) إِنَّ اللَّهَ يَغْفِرُ الذُّنُوبَ جَمِيعًا
D) b and c

Key Takeaway
Allah may forgive any sin except shirk, because sins are rooted in human weakness while shirk is a permanent rejection of the divine.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you truly feel both fear of Allah's punishment and hope for His mercy, or do you treat His forgiveness as a license to do as you please? What one change can you make today to take your sins more seriously?
?
If shirk is a permanent doctrine held forever, examine your own heart: is there any belief, desire, or attachment that you hold onto so tightly that it becomes like a doctrine? Would you be willing to abandon it for Allah's sake?
?
The passage says sins arise 'at the time of passion and desire only.' Think of a recent sin you committed. What specific passion or desire drove it? How can you guard yourself against that trigger starting tomorrow?

The Ash'ari View: Existence Is the Reality of a Thing
Al-Shanwani defines the Ash'ari position on existence and the non-existent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari doctrine that existence is not a separate attribute added to the essence, and the consequent status of the non-existent.

Core Idea
For the Ash'aris, existence is the very reality of a thing, not a quality like redness.

This passage from al-Shanwani's commentary clarifies the Ash'ari position on existence, distinguishing it from the Mu'tazilite view. Al-Shanwani explains that existence is not an additional attribute that inheres in a subject; rather, what exists in the external is the essence itself qualified by existence. This leads to the conclusion that the non-existent has no reality in the external world.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the English translation. Note the key terms: 'sam'iyyat', 'wujud', 'mahiyya', 'ma'dum'.
The passage is dense; take your time with each phrase.
2
Identify the central claim
Find the statement that existence is 'the very reality' (ayn al-haqiqa) and not additional to quiddity. Understand the contrast with the example of redness.
The redness analogy shows existence is not a quality like color.
3
Understand the consequence
See how the Ash'ari view leads to the conclusion that the non-existent is nothing in the external—it only becomes real through existence.
This refutes the Mu'tazilite view that the non-existent has some reality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قسم السمعيات في الموجودات عين حقيقته وليسَ زائدا على الماهيَّة ، بمعنى أنَّه ليس الخارج والمحسوس إلَّا الذَّات المتّصفة بالوجود من غير أن يتحقق فيه ذات معروضة للوجود لها فيه تحقق ، ولعارضها المسمى بالوجود وجود آخر كوجود الذَّاتِ المتّصفة بالحُمرة وعارضها الذي هو الحمرة القائمة بها هذا ما عليه الأشاعرة، وعليه : فالمعدوم ليس في الخارج بشيء ولا ذات ولا ثَابت أي: لا حقيقة له في الخارج، وإنَّما يتحقق بوجوده فيه.
The section on sam'iyyat (سمعيات) [deals with] the existents: [existence] is the very reality of [the thing] and is not additional to the quiddity (ماهية), meaning that nothing is [found] in the external and the sensible except the essence qualified by existence, without there being in the external an essence that is a subject for existence having a realization [of its own] and for its accident called existence another existence, like the existence of the essence qualified by redness and its accident which is the redness subsisting in it. This is the position of the Ash'aris. Based on this: the non‑existent (معدوم) is not a thing (شيء), nor an essence (ذات), nor a fixed entity (ثابت) in the external; i.e., it has no reality in the external; rather it becomes realized through its existence in it.
Source pages p. 583

Commentary
This passage is a doctrinal definition and clarification of the Ash'ari position on existence. The author begins by stating that in the section on sam'iyyat (non‑rational matters known from revelation), existence is the very reality (عين) of a thing and is not additional to its quiddity in the external world. This means that what exists externally is nothing other than the essence qualified by existence; there is no separate entity that is a subject for existence and also a distinct existence. He compares this to redness: a red thing has the essence and the quality of redness, but existence is not a quality like that. This is the Ash'ari view. Consequently, the non‑existent is nothing in the external world—it has no reality or fixity; it only becomes real when it comes into existence. This refutes the Mu'tazilite view that the non‑existent is a thing with some reality.

Key Terms
الوجود
الماهيّة
المعدوم
السمعيات
الأشاعرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Existence is a separate attribute like whiteness or redness that inheres in a subject.
Correction: The passage explains that existence is not additional to quiddity; it is the very reality of the thing, unlike accidents like redness that subsist in a subject.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'ari position explained in this passage, what is the status of the non‑existent in the external world?
A) It is a fixed entity with some reality.
B) It is nothing, neither a thing nor an essence nor a fixed entity.
C) It exists as a potential in the mind.
D) It is a thing but not an existent.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'aris hold that existence is identical to the essence in the external, so the non-existent has no reality until it exists.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that nothing in your life—your health, your wealth, even your very self—has any reality apart from Allah's constant act of bringing it into existence? Or do you secretly assume your own essence is somehow self-standing? How would your daily gratitude change if you internalized this?
?
When you sin, do you think your sin 'exists' as a fixed reality, or do you recognize that even your wrong actions have no permanence except as Allah creates them? How might this affect the urgency of your repentance?
?
If the non-existent has no reality at all, then your existence right now is entirely dependent on Allah's will. Are you living as if that is true—with constant awareness that you could be annihilated in an instant? What one change would such awareness produce in your priorities today?

Preserving the Rational Soul and Retaliation
Defines preservation of rational soul and its legal implications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Islamic law protects the rational soul and how retaliation (qisas) upholds this protection.

Core Idea
The rational soul is inviolable; qisas deters its violation.

This lesson examines a brief but foundational passage from the Itḥāf al-Murīd. The commentator specifies that the universal of 'preserving the soul' refers specifically to the rational soul, which distinguishes humans from animals. This distinction grounds the legal prohibition of killing and maiming without right, and it explains why the law of retaliation (qisas) was legislated. As you read, consider how a single attribute—rationality—carries profound legal consequences.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without stopping. Then read the translation. Notice the logical flow: definition → prohibition → legislation.
The parenthesis before 'ثُمَّ' is part of the original text.
2
Focus on the phrase 'rational soul'
Why does the commentator specify 'rational'? What does this exclude? The commentary clarifies that it includes all humans, even those without actual reason, but excludes animals.
This is the key distinction.
3
Understand the two prohibitions
The passage forbids killing and cutting limbs without right. Ask: what counts as 'without right'? Legal justifications (e.g., punishment, self-defense) are exceptions.
4
Connect to the legislation of qisas
Retaliation (qisas) in life and limb is presented as a means to enforce the prohibition. How does qisas deter violations? The commentary calls it a deterrent.
Qisas is both a punishment and a preventive measure.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثُمَّ نَفْسٍ) عاقلة، فلا يُباح قتلها، ولا قطع أعضائها بغير حقِّ، ولذا شُرعَ القِصاصُ في النَّفس والطَّرف.
Then soul, rational, so its killing is not permitted, nor cutting of its limbs without right, and therefore retaliation (qisas) in life and limb was legislated.
Source pages p. 596

Commentary
This passage defines the second universal: preserving the soul (nafs). The commentator specifies that it is 'a rational soul' ('aqilah), meaning that it pertains to humans, including the insane and minors who have the potential of rationality, thus excluding animals. The protection means that it is not permissible to kill it or cut its limbs without legal justification. Hence, the law of retaliation (qisas) for murder and injury was prescribed to deter such violations.

Key Terms
النفس
العاقلة
القتل
قطع الأعضاء
القصاص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think 'rational soul' means only people who are actually rational (like sane adults) are protected, and the insane or children are not.
Correction: The term 'rational soul' refers to the human species, which possesses the potential for reason. Thus, all humans, regardless of their actual mental state, are protected under this universal.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why was qisas (retaliation) legislated?
A) To allow revenge
B) To protect the rational soul by deterring violations
C) To punish animals that attack humans
D) To provide a financial alternative for murder

Key Takeaway
Islamic law protects the rational soul by prohibiting killing and maiming without right, and reinforces this protection through the law of retaliation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believed every human soul is inviolable, how would you change the way you speak about others or treat them? What specific step will you take today to honor that inviolability?
?
The law of retaliation aims to deter violation of life and limb. In your own life, how do you deter yourself from harming others, even in minor ways? Are you relying on fear of punishment or something deeper?
?
Have you ever thought of qisas as a mercy rather than a punishment? How does this passage challenge your understanding of justice and mercy in Islamic law?

Preserving Wealth: Definition and Legal Safeguards
A definition of wealth and a statement of its protection via punishments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Islamic legal definition of wealth and why its protection necessitates prescribed punishments.

Core Idea
Wealth is anything lawfully ownable, and its sanctity is enforced by fixed penalties.

The commentator now turns to the third universal necessity: the preservation of wealth. He provides a precise legal definition and explains the rationale behind the punishments for theft and brigandage. This lesson clarifies what constitutes protected wealth and the gravity of violating it.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition carefully
Read the Arabic phrase 'كل ما يحل تملكه شرعا ولو قل' and its translation. Note the key elements: 'halal', 'ownership', 'even if little'.
The definition is broader than common understanding of wealth.
2
Identify the prohibitions
The passage mentions two specific violations: theft (sariqah) and usurpation (ghasb). Reflect on why they are singled out.
3
Connect to the punishments
Ask yourself: why did Allah legislate fixed punishments for these crimes? The answer lies in the sanctity of wealth as a necessary good.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحفظ (مال) وهو : كلُّ ما يَحِلُّ تملُّكُه شرعًا ولو قل ؛ فلا يُباح بسرقة ولا غَصب ؛ ولذا شُرِعَ حَدُّ السَّرقة وقطع الطريق، ولهما معًا شُرعَ حَد الحرابة .
And preserving wealth, and it is everything that is lawful to own according to shari'ah, even if little; so it is not permitted to take it by theft or usurpation; and therefore the prescribed punishment for theft and highway robbery was legislated, and for both together the punishment for brigandage was legislated.
Source pages p. 596

Commentary
This passage explains the third universal: preserving wealth (mal). The commentator defines wealth as everything that is lawful to own according to shari'ah, regardless of quantity. The rule is that it may not be taken by theft (sariqah) or usurpation (ghasb). Consequently, specific punishments (hudud) were instituted: the punishment for theft (cutting of the hand), for highway robbery (cutting of hand and foot), and for brigandage (hirabah) which combines both crimes of taking life and property.

Key Terms
المال
التملك
السرقة
الغصب
الحرابة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Wealth is only money or valuable property above a certain amount.
Correction: The passage defines wealth as anything lawful to own, even if little. Even a small lawful item is protected.

Checkpoint
What is the definition of wealth (mal) according to the commentator?
A) Only gold and silver
B) Everything that is lawful to own, even if little
C) Property that exceeds a certain minimum value
D) Anything that can be stolen

Key Takeaway
Wealth is everything lawful to own, and its protection justifies the fixed punishments for theft and brigandage.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever taken something that is lawfully owned by another person—even something as small as a pen or a minute of work time—without their full consent? What does this lesson compel you to do about that right now?
?
If you truly believe that every lawful object in your possession, no matter how insignificant, is protected by Allah's severe punishments, how would your attitude toward ownership and theft change today?
?
When you see others adopting a careless attitude toward taking public property or 'borrowing' without permission, does this lesson make you feel personally responsible to correct them or at least to never follow their example? How will you concretely guard against such behavior this week?
